THE RED PAGE.
Lawson’s Last Book—A Temporary
Adjustment .
THERE was published last month for Henry
Lawson a second collection of verses, en-
titled “Verses Popular and Humorous. 5 ’
Though dated four years later than “ In
the Days When the World was Wide,” it repre-
sents equally with that volume his thirteen years
of literary effort, —being indeed a second
ohoice or second vintage of verses late and
early, for the most part republished from
The Bulletin and other journals. Naturally
the second vintage is inferior to the first ;
and indeed Lawson’s wine here seems running
perilously near its lees. The appeal in the new
volume is less that of a poet, however rough, than
of a racy rhymester, vigorous in vernacular. Many
of the pieces included, though probably earning
their place in ephemeral newspaper columns,
have no claim to be set on permanent record ; and
the perpetuation of inferior work reacts upon
Lawson’s deservedly high reputation.
That reputation, in Australia generally, is per-
haps a little higher than Lawson’s deserts; but he
was so long underrated that the public which
pays a surplus gives only an equivalent. The
growth and decay of Australian literarv reputa-
tion are always interesting. At first an author is
known only to the few who can appreciate him as
highly as his work deserves. Gradually his merit
and their advocacy make a wider way for him,
and more and more adherents are gathered to the
standard. When he is reviewed patronisingly by
an English magazine, the body of Respectable
readers begin to think his work should be read and
praised. Possibly Govt. House sets the seal on his
fame. But all this takes time; and before the
process is ended the writer’s work may
have degenerated, he may have exhausted his
mind’s alluvial and found no reefs beneath. Then
the small body of critics, who watch and value
each piece of work as it is put forward, come to a
halt and look backward. But with" the momen-
tum of advertisement the writer’s reputation may
still be increasing, and it is given to few
to know how far it is ' solid and how far
a hollow shell. Then, in Australia, the mass
of intelligent readers awake to the fact that the
author has ceased to interest them ; and his stock
suddenly falls. In more conservative England
people go on buying and reading inferior work
because it bears the name of an author who has
once written good work. That attitude of ad-
miring the Present for the sake of the Past is
luckily rare in this country. Here, while admir-
ing the we insist that the Present shall jus-
tify itself. So a local writer is compelled to pro-
duce good work as long as is possible to him, by
the knowledge that bad work will gain no credence.
Henry Lawson is still valued, on the merit of
his earlier work, as the most characteristic writer
whom-Australia has produced, and the memory
of Ins good things is imperishable. Perhaps he
cannot yet be said to have commenced his literary
descent. But for the last three years his real
fame has remained at a standstill. With few
exceptions, the best of his verses and sketches
were written in the ten years from 1887 to 1897, and
since then he has barely maintained his standard.
Since then, indeed, many of his compositions
show a decided loss of force, a loss of value. He
seems to have used all his vivid objective impres-
sions without replacing them by subjective ideas
of equal literary significance. And the work
which he has printed in The Argosy and else-
where, since he journeyed to London, is barely
second-rate journal-work, destitute of life or
power. One naturally hopes that he will recover
and improve. In the meantime, we have his
already splendid Australian books to re-read and
treasure.
“Verses Popular and Humorous ” takes its own
place among those books. It is good Lawson,
with all his defects and most of his fine qualities.
It is weak on the democratic side, the revolu-
tionary side, the side of rebellious verses like
“The Army of the Rear” (not yet reprinted);
but that side is not Respectable, and might
offend purchasers. Of strong bush pictures,
of strong human emotion, there is no lack. Law-
son’s vein of grim humour and grimmer satire is
mined again ; there is his old touch of selection
romance; there are some early verses after Ken-
dall, and some later ones after Kipling, Paterson,
and Ogilvie ; there is Lawson’s familiar personal
complaint; and there are his city sketches of
larrikin life, as biting and graphic as any-
thing he has written. But the poetic level
of “The Vagabond,” for example, in the
first volume is nowhere reached; and in all his
other .phases but the larrikin phase the first volume
contains better work than that which he now puts
forward. The workmanship is still crude ; and i'
Lawson had submitted rhymes like “ broad ” anc
“board,” or “soon as” and “Rooneys”— or
anti-climaxes like “With God, or a dog, to watch
they slept ” —or worthless lines like—
And in the next room, with a cold in her head,
Our Mary was sewing on buttons—in bed.
to the “literary friends” he sneers at, he coulc
have been taught how to improve his work all
small cost. But Lawson apparently prefers to
“pose as a martyred god,’’despite two disclaimers
on pp. 106, 119 ; and the martyred rules of prosody
can only shriek desperately in his determined
clutch.
The sneer at the “ literary friend,” by-the-way
(excellently illustrated by Mahony) would seem
ungracious in anyone but Lawson. In Lawson it
seems merely in character. Yet nobody knows
better than Lawson how generously he has been
helped by the brains and time of men much less
talented, but in some ways more talented than
himself;—how the misspelt, ungrammatical, but
forcible copy of his early days was edited into some-
thing like decent form—form for which Lawson
took credit with the matter; —how many crudities
and deformities have been removed from his work
by friendly hints. The original value of the work
was always his own ; but the helpers who strove
to make that value more manifest, whose help
was often necessary to make it manifest, deserve
something better than a sneer for their pains.
These things are of little importance; they are
not the best side of Lawson; and it is the
best side that one wishes to dwell on and
remember. In the new volume, Lawson’s quick
eye, his sympathetic emotion, his sincerity, his
power, his forcible phrasing, are abundantly
manifest. Consider the strength and truth of
this picture of “ How the Land was Won.”
Starvation and toil on the tracks they went,
And death by the lonely way ;
The childbirth under the tilt or tent,
The childbirth under the dray,
The childbirth out in the desolate hut
With a half-wild gin for nurse—
That’s how the first were' born to bear
The brunt of the first man’s curse 1
They toiled and they fought through the shame of It—
Through wilderness, flood, and drought ;
They worked, in the struggles of early days,
Their sons’ salvation out.
The white girl-wife in the hut alone,
The men on the boundless run,
The miseries suffered, unvoiced, unknown—
And that’s how the land was won 1
No arm-chair rest for the old folk then—
But, ruined by blight and drought,
They blazed the tracks to the camps again
In the big scrubs further out.
The worn haft, wet with a father’s sweat,
Gripped hard by the eldest son,
The boy’s back formed to the hump of toil -
And that’s how the land was worn !
This is not high poetry; but the passion, the
gn p of it, make it valuable and in Australia
memorable. And Lawson at his best commands
a stronger pulse-beat than most of his compeers •
he may lack grace and elegance, but when he both
feels and writes the flood of his power sweeps
away cavils and demurrers. He is so very human
that ones humanity cannot but welcome him—as
when he writes “The Jolly Dead March
If I ever be worthy or famous—
Which I’m sadly beginning to doubt—
When the angel whose place ’tis to name us
Shall say to my spirit, “ Pass out 1 ”
I wish for no sniveling about me
(My work was the work of the land),
But I hope that my country will shout me
The price of a decent brass band.
Thump ! thump ! of the drum and Ta-ra-rit /
Thump ! thump ! and the music—it’s grand
If only in dreams, or the spirit,
To ride or march after the band I
And myself and my mourners go straying
And strolling and drifting along,
With a band in the front of us playing
The tune of an old battle-song I
I ask for no “ turn-out ” to bear me ;
I ask not for railings or slabs,
And spare me Imy country—oh, spare me
The hearse and the long string of cabs !
I ask not the baton or “ starts ” of
The bore with the musical ear,
But the music that’s blown from the hearts
The men who work hard and drink beer /
And so on with, the all-so-human doggerel whose
humanity and sincerity give it force that one often
vainly looks for m the verses of more correct bards
to a comma. Such rhymes
™ BI H and, Jim Fall Out,” “ The Green-Hand
Rouseabout, The God-Forgotten Election,”
Constable McCarty’s Investigation,” and three
or four more m the new book, are similarly delight-
ful for the truth, the humour, the humanity, and
the vital ring of them They only represent,
after all, the backblocks bard at his best; but the
back blocks bard at highest is a man worth know-
ing and esteeming.
The new book is largely an expression of Law-
son s personality ; and by reason of his literary
power Lawson’s personality is interesting. In
Ibe Men Who Come Behind ” he denounces the
imitators who “feel their way behind a bolder
spirit s back ”
There are many, far too many, in the world of prose and
rhyme,
Always looking for another’s “ footsteps on the sands of
time.”
Journalistic imitators are the meanest of mankind,
And the grandest themes are hackneyed by the pens that
come behind.
Lawson is quite unconscious that he himself, since
inspiration began to wane, is occasion-
ally in trie ranks of the men who come behind. Yet
his“At the Tug of War ’’versifies an incident of
which the force was first seen, and strongly ex-
pressed, by A. B. Paterson when sketching a'Syd-
ney tng-of-war for The Bulletin; “The Rovers”
—with its verse about the
Unlisted, uncommissioned,
Untaught in ally school,
is influenced by Kipling’s “Legion that Never
Was Listed,”—while the verse that tells how—
Through drought and desolation
They won their way Out Back ;
The commonplace and selfish
Have followed on their track—
is indebted to Ogilvie’s “The Men that Blazed
the Track ; Lawson’s “ Old Stone Chimney ” re-
peats Ogilvie’s “Abandoned Selections” and
“ Deserted a fine idea, but Ogilvie saw it first ;
m “When Ladies Come to the Shearing Shed ”
two lines —
They are girls from the city. (Our hearts rebel
When we squint at their dainty feet—
echo Mowbray Morris’s lines in “A Voice from
the Bush ” (long printed as Gordon’s)—
Booted and bearded and burn’d to a brick
I loaf along the street;
I watch the ladies tripping by
And bless their dainty feet.
Lawson’s " The Lights of Cobb and Co.” takes the
idea of Ogilvie’s previously-printed “Cobb and
Co.”—with the lines
The drovers round their camp-fire hail
The lights of Cobb and Co.
and Lawson’s lines
And throw our top coats open to drink the mountain
air
Pause bird-like on the summit—then breakneck down
the pinch
are plainly inspired by the lines in Paterson’s
“Travelling Post Office”—
A moment on the topmost grade while open fire doors
glare,
She pauses like a living thing to drink the mountain
air-
“ The Travelling Post Office itself dependent for
inspiration on Kipling’s “ Overland Mail.” And
so on.
Since Lawson, then, takes his own where he
finds it, he might be less severe on the others who,
as he conceives, take their own from himself.
Lawson’s best work has always been stimulated by
generous enthusiasm ; his worst by ungenerous sus-
picion. If he could realise that, he would refrain
from the sneer, and hold to the unselfish emotions
which have been his chief inspiration.
Well, there are spots on the sun; and the flaws
in Lawson’s literary work, though they may
sadden and disappoint his friends, will be for-
gotten in the fine, performance which has estab-
lished his credit. To the perpetuation of his
value and fame many pieces in “Verses, Popular
and Humorous” will contribute. It is only to be
regretted that so much has been included which
is neither worthy of Lawson the writer nor of
Lawson the man.
The Trees.
YOU have never listened to the whispering
of the trees ; or, if you did, have you ever
understood ? Go out into the untouched
forest or out to the big hilksciiibs, and
you will hear vague murmurs when the winds
blow from far-off settlements. If you could only
comprehend ! The trees whisper of what is to be.
Down below, to the big forest trees, the crash of
some stricken brother is borne by the quiver-
ing breeze. Then you hear the sighing
and whispering and moaning which makes
the forest so mysterious even at midday.
The big trees among the hill-scrub see the hills
which were oiice scrub-clothed and are now bare
to the sky. The axe-men are coming closer. The
smoke from the funeral pyre of their stricken kin-
dred is blown over and amongst them from time
to time by the same wind that carries the sound
of the woodman’s axe, telling of further destruc-
tion of their brethren. And when the smoke is
blown about amongst the trees you will hear the
moaning eerie noises in the scrub. You will see
the trees, even the proudest and most stately, bow
down their heads in sorrow and sob.
They sorrow not for themselves: ’t is only the
parasites and cumbrous growths give way to sel-
fish grief. The best of the trees are prepared and
resigned ; for have they not seen, and has not the
wind brought the tidings from forest to forest,
from scrub to scrub, the tidings of what is to be ?
You will hear them talk and whisper—whisper the
wish for a good man and a sharp axe when their
time comes.
And, oh, how the trees pity these men who
come to work their destruction ! The agony, the
fatigue of the work! The big sweat-drops fall
from the men’s foreheads in a stream almost as
great as the life-sap which flows from the cut of
the axe. How the trees pity those men who work
among the trees—mere tools of other men ! Well
do they know ; for have not the winds brought
the story from the first forest that was ever
cleared ? and do not the winds tell of laboring
man being robbed of the fruits of his labor every
day? And the big trees listen to the tale of the
wind, and all the forest and scrub are filled with
sorrow for poor lowly man. . .
The big tree waits patiently for the last stroke
of the axe ; and the wind soothes it in its agony.
You will hear no moan from that tree —only a
gentle rustle from its topmost leaves as they give
their last love-kiss to the wind. But at the final
moment the stump cannot bear to part with the
topmost branches, and it holds the trunk up and
calls to the vines to help it. And the vines that
have twisted and played around that tree, since
its growth began, catch it in a close em-
brace and hold it up. But the axe is
falling, and the agony is growing greater.
The top boughs tremble with pain, and the
wind in pity lifts the big tree from its
stump and lays it down upon the hillside.
What you call the big crash is the last good-bye
of a thousand little branches, twigs, and leaves,
to the murmuring wind ; and the groan from the
stump is the last farewell of a lover to his loved
one who is dead.
The big trees are filled with pity for the poor,
struggling axeman, for they know all his sweat-
drops, all his labour, go for nothing. There is no
future for him but work—incessant work. And
so a big tree will tremble on its stump, and wait,
and the man will step back and trip, and the tree
in pity will fall on him and end his days of weari-
ness. His working mates, in their ignorance, will
curse and abuse the tree and all trees ; for they
know no better. And as the dead man’s spirit,
freed from all care, floats upwards through the
treetops, you will hear the trees whisper a prayer
—not for the man who is dead and at rest, but
for those who are doomed to work throughout life,
robbed of their legitimate leisure and its enjoy-
ment. All this you will hear at any time out in
the big scrubs or the untouched forests.
But, to understand, you must have the sym-
pathy of. the trees.
Q.
MITTY.
That useful and pertinacious writer, “E. W.D.,”
who takes tithe of our mint, and anise, and cum-
min, suggests that Dorrington’s “paque”
(24/11/’0) should have been “pyx.” And it
should. And he complains that he was included,
among possible shrine-desecrators (15/12/’0) when
he did not compete in the search for time, place,
etc., but merely quoted one of Rossetti’s sonnets.
That also is true.
A countryman’s impression of holiday Sydney—
“ Ohapge is good,” said I; “ I will leave the bush and
go to town.” (It is sometimes easier to follow a prompt-
ing than to analyse its value in terms of truth.) Sydney’s
salutation is at Uedfern you meet the stink, the odour
of a great city, the acrid perfume of congested humanity.
We hustle ; the train is stationary, the trams endless. No
matter. The fever of the city grips—the nervous ten-
sion, the madness of perpetual motion. Break open a
termites’ nest and you shall see a multitude of galleries
along which, in endless streams, the blind white-ants
pass in ceaseless running to the making of excrement.
Nature, God’s trees, man’s house and barn, are all
potential excrement to the termite.
The knee is a bending joint, so I pass to the modern
Joss-house, the office of a paper, to see the priests of the
God up-to-date. I find them sitting in their cells digest,
ing the paper offerings of the faithful as they secrete
mental pepsine. They are preparing the weekly sacri-
fice. You fear to speak, to interrupt, even for a moment,
the gastric process. Goaded by wild prompting you bid
them shut their cells, let their Joss go hang, and come
out and have a drink and a stroll under God’s clear sky.
No ! —Joss’s appetite is insatiable ; his worshippers are
bound to the machine; their nerves, livers, eyes and
brains have long been laid on the altar ; besides, they' all
run to catch trams twice a day, and the man who runs
;wice a day to catch anything is damned eternally.
In George-street the termites hurry without rest. Feet
are not fast enough ; horse, steam, electricity, add to the
momentum. A strange likeness in the mass, each one
willing to dress like another, like every other. This is
the unconscious aim; to sink personality, to lose
edge and point by friction in the mass. Yet beach
stones are useless in a wall; they make no bond.
The crowd does not laugh, play, rejoice or sing together;
its one content is contiguity. Saturday night might see
it home ; but no, on shop-closed block it moves in denser
mass, yet caring nothing for the fellows it mutely
assimilates. On Sunday, perforce, it is thrown on what
is left of itself ; it sickens at the prospect. “ Oh, let us
to the tram, or train, or boat again, and be nobodies with
everybody. Let us move without aim, talk without
thought on the pancreating throng ! for this is life and
living—my grinding to extinction.”
In the stewing crowd the larrikin is the garlic : he per-
meates. The bygone slum, hulks and chain-gang have
banished his nerves and rounded his angles ; he is less a
character than a type, fixed now so that he grinds with-
out being ground. See how he ear-marks every assembly,
nor can one Sydneyite know himself free of taint.
So do spectral gibbets on Gallows Hill chortle re-
trospective glee, as past infamies, like all infamies
whatsoever, are being paid for. Consider the
Chinese ; he never becomes European; but many Euro-
peans become Chinese. Ten thousand years of city
grinding has so fixed the type that China could digest a
tribe of Israel in its ostrich maw. The larrikin can con-
template the future with confidence and oaths. Sydney
is his, the City Council and the Parliament; and of a
surety he will expectorate on the Federal Senate through
the boots he has placed in the Federal forum.
H.F.

The Return of Rip Van Winkle
O’Sullivan.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The O’Sullivan, speaking at the opening of the tramway
to the Spit on Nov. 3, said, wistfully, that “he would likt
to be able to return in a hundred years’ time, for he had
no doubt that he would then see a nation of which the
whole world might well be proud.”
A hundred fateful years
Had passed away, and gone
With laughter, or with tears,
Into Oblivion,
When, with a heart elate—
A resurrected man !
To where he once was great
Came The O’Sullivan.
With wonder, and with awe,
He did the city view :
The Sydney that he saw
Was not the town he knew.
The Sydney of the past,
In brick-and-mortar dress,
Was fine; This was a vast
Stone-mantled Titaness.
Yea, she was great and fair
And unconfined and free ;
One hand’s white fingers were
Dipped in the Hawkesbury
One hand laved in the rills
Beside the Sisters Three :
Her head lay on the hills,
Her feet were in the sea.
And statesmen, firm and brave,
And legislators wise,
Sat in her halls, -with grave,
Far-seeing, sovran eyes.
For long to Ichabod
Had gone, with sick’ning slump,
The Footbridge-Period,
The Era of the Pump.
Was the O’Sullivan
By all these wonders dazed ?
Not he : the ancient man
On all serenely gazed.
And, to a gaping crowd,
Who stared with awe profound,
He spoke in accents proud,
With sweep of arm around.
“ Of all this splendour vast,
Which I with pleasure see—
Who laid the firm and fast
Foundations? It was Me!
“In Me behold the Man
Equal to any circs.—
I’m The O’Sullivan,
Once Minister for Works.
“ I oped the Tramway famed,
The tramway to the Spit ”
x’hey laughed at what they named
His coarse, old-fashioned wit.
They would not hear him more ;
His voice with groans they drowned ;
They said he was a Bore
Who should be underground.
In vain he tried to point
Out works that he did plan :
The times were out of joint
With The o‘Sullivan.
Of fame he was bereft :
There was no trace —not one—
No faintest record left,
Of all that he had done.
And then some persons rude—
For such will always be—
In grimly playful mood
Made with his person free.
They danced his coat-tails on;
They bashed his hat effete,
With energy upon
His “cobbra” obsolete.
Then, in the nick of time,
A showman passed who cowed
The authors of the crime,
And shouted for the crowd.
That showman knew his trade,
And lectured up and down,
Upon the Find he’d made.
And so from town to town
There passed, in painted van,
And painful public circs.,
“The strange Primeval Man—
Once Minister for Works.”
Does he still travel round,
An Exhibition weird ?
I know not; I have found
Out only what’s appeared.
But were I the O’S.
I’d not re-view, I vow,
The scene of my success —
A hundred years from now.
N.S.W.

THIS ILLUSTRATION is from a Photo-
graph of the Annex Building just
erected on the Grounds of Govern-
ment House, designed by the
Government Architect as special
accommodation for Vice-regal re-
tinue. The method of construction
being somewhat novel justifies the
following description.
THE FRAMING TIMBERS are of small
dimensions, the Studding and Raf-
ters being only 2in. x 3in. The
Braces are made of l£in. flat Galv.
Iron, strongly nailed to each piece
of timber.
THE SHEATHING is made of Galv.
Wire Netting 36in. x3in. x 16 gauge
secured after the method described
on page 88 of our New Price List.
THE COVERING.—This is done by use
of P. and B. Ruberoid Roofing fixed
after the method described on page
88 *of our New Price List, with
the additional feature of being
cemented as well as nailed to the
studs, thus giving uniform and
maximum strength.
THE SEAMS are cemented with the P.
and B. Ruberine (or liquid Cement)
and are covered with narrow strips
of wood. The wooden strips are
not necessary, but add to the
appearance.
THE CEILINGS and interior walls are
covered in the same manner, and
with the same material.

whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,

SATURDAY, JANUARY 12, 1901.
How N.S.W. Got Left.
A few years ago New South Wales held a
tolerably commanding position among the
manufacturing States of Australia. It wasn’t
ahead of the other States in proportion to
population, but it had kept level with them.
That was before it made its first real
approach to Freetrade, and Got Left as the
natural consequence. Up to the date of the
abolition of the Dibbs tariff N.S. Wales was
a Protectionist State of a more or less
hidden and apologetic kind. Parkes,
M'Miixan, Bruce Smith and Co. were
enthusiastic Freetraders who protected
almost everything in a small way. They
levied heavy protective duties on beer, wine,
tobacco, cigars, cigarettes, bacon, biscuits,
butter, fruits, candles, cement, cheese, con-
fectionery, cornflour, “ soft ” drinks, timber
of all kiuds, doors, window-sashes, railway
sleepers, posts and rails, all kinds of gal-
vanised-iron goods, hams, tongues, jams,
jellies, sugar, molasses, treacle, red and
wiiite lead, oils, paints, salt, varnish, news-
papers, and many other things. They sacri-
ficed much revenue in doing it—revenue
that would have been gained if they had
made the excise on the local article equal to
the customs tax on the foreign one and not
protected anything. They .stood up on many
platforms and scouted the very name of the
dreadful thing called Protection, but in prac-
tice they were pretty good Protectionists of
a kind. The two survivors of them still
scout Protection in theory and shudder at
the name of that allegedly hated policy, but
if they got into office they would probably
again be found in practice pretty good Pro-
tectionists of a kind. The absence of these
two good practical Protectionists, Bruce
Smith and M‘Millan, from the Federal
Cabinet would be almost regrettable, but for
the fact that the politician who looks one
way and pulls another, even if he pulls the
right way, isn’t a desirable individual.
Dibbs expanded the Protectionist policy of
Parkes, M'Millan and B. Smith. And
Reid, who followed him, preached absolute
Freetrade, and got so near to it that he only
protected beer, wine, sugar, treacle, molasses,
cigars, cigarettes, tobacco, biscuits, confec-
tionery, jams, jellies and newspapers.
* * * *
Up to the time when the Reid very-
slightly-Protectionist Freetrade tariff got into
working order, N.S. Wales, as already men-
tioned, kept its place reasonably well as a
manufacturing State. In its struggle with
Victoria and S.A. it had the advantage of a
splendid coal supply, which they both lacked,
many years' start, and a vastly larger area of
good country, and, aided by the reasonably
liberal Protection kindly granted by these
enthusiastic Freetraders, M‘Millan, Bruce
Smith and Co., it kept about level with its
younger, less-favored, but more-protected
rivals. It was with the advent of George
Reid and a very much reduced measure of
Protection that N.S. W. began to get really
left. In 1894 N.S.W. had 42,751 hands
employed in its factories against Victoria’s
41,000 (the figures are published by N.S.W.
Statistician Coghlan on pages 598 and 599
of his latest issue of the “ Seven Colonies ”),
and had thus a solid advantage of 1751 em-
ployes. And then, under the blessings of
the Reid policy, its neighbors moved ahead
of it, and the proceedings degenerated into
a procession with N.S.W. in the dust-cloud
at the rear.
* * * *
If a beginning is made from the year 1896,
when the advantages of Freetrade (or the
nearest approach to it N.S.W. has yet
possessed) had begun to soak in, the indus-
trial progress of the five leading Australian
States has been something as under. The
figures are all taken from Coghlan’s “ Seven
Colonies ” for 1900, pages 598 to 601 :
Hands Employed in Factories.
1896. 1899. Increase.
Victoria .. 50,448 .. 60,070 .. 9,622
Queensland.. 19,733 .. 27,200 .. 7,467
N.S.W. .. 49,840 .. 55,646 .. 6,806
S.A 12,785 .. 15,155 .. 2,370
1895. 1898.
Tasmania .. 1,754 .. 3,629 .. 1,875
The Tasmanian figures are only available up
to 1898, but in each case the comparison
covers a period of four years. There are no
statistics covering a period of four years
available from either Westralia or Maoriland,
therefore it is impossible to include the
whole seven States. But, so far as the
information goes, it isn’t quite the best
advertisement that a Freetrade system (even
a partially-Protective Freetrade system like
Reid’s) could have. N.S. Wales has com-
pletely lost the industrial advantage it had
over Victoria in the last days of the
Mother State’s avowed Protectionism. There
isn’t even the hollow satisfaction of saying
that the increase in Victoria’s factory em-
ployes is an increase in the employment of
women. N.S. Wales, before it took to
something approaching real Freetrade, was
distinctly ahead of Victoria in the number of
its male factory-workers ; now, as regards
both male and female workers, it is falling
further and further back. During the four
years of Reid’s tariff the progress has been
as follows (N.S.W. figures from page 569 of
Coghlan’s “ Statistical Register” for 1899 ;
Victorian figures from page 47 of Fenton’s
“ Abstract of the Statistics of Victoria” for
1899): —
Four years’ in- Four years’ in-
crease in crease in Total
male female increase,
employes. employes.
Victoria .. 6,262 .. 3,360 .. 9,622
N.S. Wales.. 4,155 .. 1,651 .. 5,806
Vic. majority. 2,107 1,709 3,816
Victoria is leaving N.S. Wales even faster
in the matter of male industrial workers than
in the matter of female operatives.
* * * *
And the unkindest cut of all is that Pro-
tected Queensland, with little more than
one-third of N.S. Wales’s population, had,
in four years, an increase of 7467 factory
employes against Freetrade N.S.W.’s in-
crease of only 5806. Its industries are not
only growing faster in proportion to popula-
tion, but faster in the aggregate. In pro-
portion to population N.S. W.’s manufactur-
ing industries have been, during the last
four years, the most stagnant in the five
States for which returns are available. The
blight of George Reid fell upon the Corn-
stalk land, and, as hereinbefore alluded to,
it Got Left.
* * * *
Going by population, indeed, N.S.W. has,
within the last four years, Got Left very
badly. The following is the rate of progress
of the five States :
New factory employes
for whom work was
found in four Proportion to
years, 1896-99. population.
Queensland .. .. 7467 .. 1.55 per cent.
Tasmania .. .. 1875 .. 1.03 „
Victoria .. .. 9622 .. 0.83 „
South Australia .. 2370 .. 0.64 ~
New South Wales .. 5806 .. 0.43 ~
All the four Protectionist States are finding
new industrial employment for their new
population at a faster rate than N.S.W. If
the Reid tariff could have remained in force
for another 20 years, N.S.W., on the above
record, promised to occupy a very lone-
some position indeed at the industrial
tail of Australia. In 1896 (according
to Coghlan’s “ Seven Colonies,” page
597) the factory employes of all Australasia
numbered 164,840. N.S.W., with 49,840
of them, then represented 30 per cent, of
the lot. It had also exactly 30 per cent, of
the population; while the Protectionist
States had 70 per cent, of the population
and 70 per cent, of the manufacturing
classes; so N.S.W., up to the end
of its Protectionist or semi-Protectionist
era, just held its own industrially with its
Protectionist neighbours. In 1899 N.S. W.
still represented almost exactly 30 per cent,
of the population, but with 55,646 factory
employes out of an Australasian total of
201,100, it only represented 27$ per cent, of
the industrial workers. The Protectionist
States had 70 per cent, of the people
and 72J -per cent, of the manufactur-
ing population. Merely to keep its
place according to population—the place it
held before the Reid tariff came into exist-
ence—and to prove that Reid’s variety of
Freetrade was as good as Protection—
not better than Protection but only
as good—N.S.W. should have 63,330 fac-
tory employes instead of 55,646.
The fall isn’t a very large one in the aggre-
gate, but it is very large when it is remem-
bered that it has all taken place in the short
space of four years. And it is a very bad
advertisement for Freetrade when it is re-
membered that it all took place within the
only four years when N.S. W. could be called,
even with some reservations, a Freetrade
State, and during the period when the Free-
trade party’s air of superiority was, if
possible, a little more insufferably pompous
than usual.
* * * *
If there were any compensating advan-
tages in Freetrade, then the painful process
of Getting Left might not matter so much,
but it has never been shown that there are
any. The only two States w r here statistics
have been reduced to a science are N.S.W.
and Victoria, and their statistics prove that
the protected Victorian workman is a trifle
better paid than the Freetrade one to the
north of him ; that he is fed and sup-
plied with the various necessaries of life
at as cheap a rate ; that he has more
money per head laid up in the Savings
Bank or some other bank against a day
of misfortune, and that he has more
insurance on his life. And, in addition to
that, he gets more employment, and Protec-
tion can afford to give him a legal minimum
wage as a guarantee against sweating, which
Freetrade has never dared to attempt. In
fact, Freetrade gives nothing special by way
of compensation for the grief of Being Left
save the devastating glare of George Reid’s
glass-eye, and the assurances of parrot
philosophers that Henry George, oi some-
body, has alleged something, and it must be
true because Henry George, or somebody,
has alleged it.
The Disease Called Commerce.
Theke are two kinds of commerce, and one of
them is a Disease. Australia has got the disease
kind badly, and the Freetrade party in Australia
worships the disease. Commerce, in the cant
meaning of the term, is the profitable or unpro-
fitable shifting of goods over the imaginary streak
on a map which marks a political division. Send-
ing goods, even at a profit to all parties concerned,
450 miles from Newcastle to Albury, both in
N.S.W., is not commerce in the Freetrade accep-
tation of the term, and doesn’t amount to much.
Sending goods, even at a dead loss, three miles
from Albury in N.S. W. to Wodonga in Victoria
is commerce, and therefore it is the source of all
earthly goodness and greatness, the only true test
of prosperity, the fulfilment of man’s noblest
destiny, the root of all progress, and the begin-
ing, middle, and end of things in general. The
Cornstalk Freetrade party says : ‘ ‘ Look at our
commerce as compared with that of Victoria,”
and holds that the fiscal problem is settled
for good and all. For the one purpose of Pro
gress and Civilisation is to tear much raw material
out of the ground and drag it up a plank on to a
ship, and to drag much manufactured goods down
a plank off a ship. And the country which drags
much foreign goods down a plank is Saved, and
the one which drags comparatively little raw
material up a plank is Lost, and the country
which doesn’t see that its one ambition should be
to drag exports up a plank and to drag imports
down a plank is Damned—politically, that is.
* * * *
And this faith leads to curious results. The
Freetrader measures things almost wholly by
exports and imports, and regards local industry as
nothing in particular by comparison with his
fetish—Commerce. If a country hauls out of the
ground an extra £1,000,000 worth of raw material,
and puts it on board a ship, and pays an enormous
price to have it carried by some foreigner to
the farthest ends of the earth, that is com-
merce. And if it pays £3,000,000 for the
same material, on an equal amount of similar
material, brought back in a manufactured state
from the farthest ends of the earth, and also pays
a great deal to some foreign shipowner to carry it
back, that is also commerce. The people at the
other end of the earth may have got the great
bulk of the profits, but the items appear in the
trade returns as £4,000,000 worth of commerce,
and there is great drinking at Freetrade, banquets
about the obvious increase in the country’s pro-
sperity under a civilised and enlightened fiscal
policy. And if a Protectionist country also digs
up £1,000,000 worth of raw material out of its own
soil, and manufactures it on the spot into
£3,000,000 worth of goods for the use of its own
people, and keeps the £2,000,000 worth of employ-
ment involved in the manufacturing process for
its own people, then it has no £4,000,000 worth of
extra commerce to show, and the dreadful results
of Protection are therein manifested. Yet
so far as commerce consists in this kind
of exchange—and a vast amount of Aus-
tralia’s commerce does so consist—it is a curse
and a disgrace, and an evidence of the nation’s
lack of enterprise and lack of capacity, and a
proof that it is largely confined to the poorest,
least intelligent, and most animal of occupations—
that of scratching raw material out of the soil and
shipping it away to be manufactured by more
intelligent communities. If Australia, with its
existing population, was more advanced in the
industrial sense, it would have less commerce.
It would send away less raw material, for it would
use more in its own industries, and it would bring
in less manufactured goods, because it would
make more for its own use. And so far as its
exports represent the sending away of its raw
material to be made up by other people into
goods for its own use, and so far as its imports
represent the return of its own or similar raw ma-
terial manufactured for it by more enterprising
races, Australia’s commerce is a misfortune and a
curse, and the Freetrade policy which seeks to
foster it is a curse also.
• * « *
As already mentioned there are two kinds of
commerce—of external commerce that is—and
one of them is a Disease. Although the Austra-
lian Freetrade party habitually speaks of this
country’s commerce and that of Britain as if they
were the same thing, there is really very little
resemblance. Britain, by successful war, con-
quest, fortunate adventure, enterprise and Protec-
tection, became, early in this century, the carrier
of the world. It still holds the position to a
great extent, though not in the same proportion
as it once did, and it still holds a prominent
position as a manufacturing State, though not by
any means the position it once held. Britain’s
commerce consists in loading its own and other
people’s- mostly other people’s—goods, mostly
manufactured goods, on its own ships, and carrying
them all over the earth. The carriage of these
goods is one of Britain’s important sources of
revenue. Australia’s commerce consists mostly in
loading its own raw material on other people’s
ships, and paying other people at great expense
to carry them, The one is driving a cart and
being paid handsomely for it. The other is sitting
in a cart and paying heavily for it. “ Tattersall”
and his clients may both be classed as gamblers be-
cause they are both in the same gamble, but “Tat-
tersall ” gets 10 per cent, of all the money that
goes through and his clients lose 10 per cent.
Commerce, as it is understood in Britain, France,
and Germany, and commerce as it is understood
and bragged about by the Australian Freetrader,
are both commerce in a way, only with much the
same difference as in Tattersall’s case.
* * * *
The producer of raw material is, on the whole,
the worst-paid of the industrial workers. Yet
Australia must remain in a great measure this
Gibeonite among toilers —or else it must damage
that beautiful commerce which is the Freetrader’s
Ark of the Covenant. To the Freetrader there is
a great and sacred joy in the fact that New South
Wales, in proportion to population, exports and
imports more than Victoria. He is glad to his
very heart because New South Wales makes less
of its own clothes and boots and hats, and under-
wear and ironware and leatherware, and other
things, and therefore uses less of its own raw
material in its own industries, and so has more of
them to haul up a plank on to a foreign-owned
ship at a wharf—which is commerce. An'd he is
overjoyed to the bottom of his soul because, for the
same reason, it has to drag more clothes, boots,
hats, shirts, ironmongery, leather goods, &e.,
down a plank off a foreign-owned ship at a wharf
—which is also commerce. His great ambition is
to be, in proportion to population, the greatest
wharf-lumper on earth—a tremendous national
wharf-lumper for other nations’ ships. It is his
ideal to be an ox-like beast of burden on a quay
—not his own ox, even, but the ox of the British
and French and German shipowner, on the quay
that was built with money borrowed in England.
He wants to spread the wharf-lumping complaint
all over Australia, and he is moved to profound
pity for other communities which don’t wharf-
lump quite so much as he does.
• * » •
As already mentioned, -there are two kinds of
commerce, and one of them is a Disease. A nation
which buys raw material, of the kind which it
can’t produce sufficiently itself, from less enter-
prising and intelligent races, and makes it into
finished goods, and sells them to backward
communities which don’t manufacture much for
themselves, has one kind of commerce. A nation
which sells raw material only because it
is only far enough advanced to be a digger and
delver, or a ploughman, or a person who pursues
cattle and sheep, and which buys its finished
materials from more intelligent communities —it
has the other kind of commerce. To the Free-
trader, all kinds of commerce are alike, because
they all consist of dragging something heavy off
or on a ship ; but in reality a good deal of the
honor and profit and the evidence of progress de-
pend on the nature of the goods dragged and the
ownership of the vessels they are dragged from or
to. Even the question whether the wharf they are
dragged off or on has been paid for or not enters, to
some extent, into the question. If Australia ever
becomes a self-dependent nation, and supplies its
own requirements in the way of manufactured
goods, and furnishes work and wages for its own
people in the manufacturing process, and keeps
the profits of manufacture for itself, and ceases to
waste money needlessly in paying other- nations
for carryiiig its goods to and fro, the first sign of
the great improvement setting in will be a large
diminution in the amount of its commerce in pro-
portion to population. There will be less wool and
hides humped on to ships and less clothes and boots
and saddlery humped off before that portion of Aus-
tralia’s trade which is only a Disease will abate; and
some ships which formerly came in at our ports to
carry the Disease will cease to call here; and some
wharves which they formerly occupied in the
process of the Disease will be deserted. And then
the Freetrader will wail bitterly because this
country isn’t so vast a wharf-lumper as it used to
be, and will point out for the billionth time that
the only true test of a country’s prosperity is the
amount of wharf-lumping that it accomplishes,
and the country that doesn’t lump is Lost.
The Menace of Militarism.
The State banquet at Sydney Town Hall, on the
evening of Commonwealth Day, was a significant
function. “Never before in the history of Aus-
tralia,” said the daily press, “has there been such
a brilliant assemblage under one roof. Nearly all
the prominent politicians, lawyers, and officials of
the six federating States were present.” The
object of the banquet was to commemorate, by the
time-honored fashion of feasting and toasting, the
foundation of the Commonwealth. Naturally and
properly, then, the Commonwealth should have
come first on the toast-list. To an assemblage of re-
presentative Australians there can be nothing on
earth more interesting or more important than Aus-
tralia. But representative Australians nowadays are
of a servile breed. First they toasted Queen Vic-
toria, the representative of that monarchy whose
very existence is a stultification of the claim to
national freedom. Then they toasted the Prince
of Wares and the Boval Family, so engrained is
the habit of crawling before individuals whose
characters and lives have only the accident of birth
to make them notorious. Then, at least, one
would have thought that the fetish of punctilio
had been sated, and the new-born Nation would
receive due honor. But no; Premier I.yne
grovelled before Governor-General Hopetoun.


Then Premier Lewis proposed the British
navy. Then George Reid proposed the
Lieutenant-Governors. Then John See pro-
posed the Army and Volunteers. And only then,
after the useless appanages of Government and
the British navy and army had been toasted, was
Chief-Justice Griffith permitted to propose, and
Edmund Barton to reply to, the toast of the
Commonwealth !
» • * *
That order of toasts supplies an accurate
estimate of the opinions of Australia’s temporary
leaders, and of the real worth at which they
appraise the Australian Nation as compared
with the British connection to which they toady.
But The Bulletin hopes to live to see
the day when, for every Australian, Australia
will come always first ; and when monarchs and
the military will be relegated, in public and private,
to their proper place as mere adjuncts of the
national scheme in which the first place, the first
honor, belongs naturally and inevitably to the
Australian Nation. That day will come whenever
Australians choose ; and if there is in them any
love of country, any pride of patriotism, they will
choose quickly.
• • * *
When Lieut.-Col. Wyndham, commanding the
contingent of British troops sent to N.S.W.
Commonwealth celebration, rose to reply to the
toast of the army, the cloven hoof of militarism,
seen throughout the celebration, rose boldly and
marched across the dinner-table. The British
army, said Col. Wyndham,
Thought that in the next campaign Australia would
claim the right to fight side by side with her English
comrades—(cheers) —and they hoped to see in England
detachments from
The Australian Army
attending their camps and manoeuvres to learn the latest
ideas in drill, tactics, and staff duties, so that in the
future they might hope to find a regiment, brigade, or
even an army corps, commanded by Australian officers,
and with such an army the Empire would be able to say
checkmate to any power which came across its path to
block the way to freedom and prosperity. (Cheers.)
That was the message they had come half across the
world to deliver.
And that is the prospect before Australia ! How
do Australian workers like it ?
* # * •
Is this country to be a land of peace or a land
of war ? Are we to be a self-centred community,
developing industry, promoting prosperity ? — a
community which believes with Burns that
To make a happy fireside clime
For weans and wife :
That’s the true pathos and sublime
Of human life.
Or are we to be a military dependency of Britain,
with the flower of our youth trailed at Britain’s
skirts round the world wherever there is a British
war ? That is the question which the sending of
Australians to Africa has emboldened Britain to
ask. Are we to have in Australia an Australian
army, fed from the life-blood of Australian
industry, kept at the beck and call of Britain ?
Are we to have in Australia an idle military caste,
entrenched in privilege, supported by the sweat
of the farmer, the miner, and the stock-raiser?
Yes, says Britain. Yes, say the tuft-hunters
whom Australians have placed at the head of
their affairs. And that is the message which
Lieut.-Col. Wyndham, on behalf of the British
army, has come half-across the world to deliver.
* * * •
Luckily the ultimate answer does not rest with
Australian tuft-hunters or British politicians or
the British army. It rests with the Australian
People. Only themselves can bow their
necks to the military yoke. They know
that every professional soldier, unless necessary
for the defence of Australia, is a drone and worse
than a drone, since every soldier is carried on the
back of a worker who toils doubly—once to sup-
port himself and his family, once to support the
professional soldier. They know the evils that
spring from the creation of an idle military caste,
however useful it may be to provide a living and
a social standing for the sons of rich and ob-
sequious titled politicians. They know that the
people which bows its back to the burden of a
standing army saps industry at its foundation,
and elects a master who will sooner or later
make it a slave. Why should we spend
blood and treasure in Britain’s foreign wars ? We
have a task for hundreds of years to come in
developing our country, and establishing the
happiness of our children and our children’s chil-
dren. Let us adopt the watchword once and for
all: Australia for Australians, and Aus-
tralians for Australia !
* * * *
This thing is in the hands of the people. There
is manhood suffrage over all Australia. Let this
question of militarism be made a crucial ques-
tion at the Federal elections. Let no candi-
date be approved —be it Barton himself
who will not pledge himself not only not
to support, but to actively oppose, the creation
of an Australian army to fight beyond the limits
of Australia. We cannot spare one man for
export to foreign wars. We cannot spare one
penny which is not clearly paid for national
defence. Recognition of that truth is the essen-
tial factor of Australian policy at the present
time. We want neither to be pro-Boer or pro-
English :we want to be pro-Australia. And the
only Australian army compatible with the ideal
of the Australian nation is a citizen army, trained
as the Boers train their citizens, electing its own
officers as the Boers elect their officers, forming no
happy-liunting-ground for wealthy snobs or social
parasites, and maintained rigorously for Aus-
tralian defence. But from the menace of the
Black Shadow of Militarism may Australian
voters deliver us !
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Melbourne’s Board of Fitzgibbon.
Melbourne Board of Works Inquiry Commission
was last week safely delivered of its report. The
job might have been done much more cheaply and
expeditiously, though not more thoroughly, by a
handy man with a bucket of whitewash and a large
brush. Eighteen months ago most of the local
municipal councils met in conference and formu-
lated 25 distinct charges against the fatuous and
cumbersome Board, and more recently the Age
reiterated them. These charges included such as
engineering blunders whereby sundry main sewers
have sunk from 4 to sft. ; favoritism in dealing
with contractors whereby, in one case, a contractor
was relieved of portion of his contract and paid in
full, the balance being afterwards re-let at a cost
of £9OOO ; disorganisation among the Board’s
officers and a reckless system of management
whereby, in expending £1,197,000 in sewer construc-
tion, the Board spent £1,000,000 in supervision,
salaries, etc. ; financial incapacity whereby in ’92
the Board only got £9l 17s. 9d. for a 4 per cent.
loan against Sydney city’s £99 4s, 4d. at the same
interest rate ; whereby also the excessive price of
£l7 10s. per acre was paid for Wernbee sewage
farm, being in excess of the highest valuation
made by experts; whereby also the stupendous
sum of £90,000, against an estimated £23,000, was
paid for preliminary surveys, &c., and so forth.
The charges were strong enough to have shifted any
self-respecting Board off its collective seat, with a
loud demand for complete investigation in de-
fence of its own miserable character. But while
the public and the press were clamouring for full
inquiry the stinkful Board pulled the wires so
vigorously and successfully that the Commission
of Inquiry, when at length appointed, was limited
to “investigating the manner of effecting house
connections with the sewers” and “considering
the constitution of the Board.” On both these
points the Commission cordially whitewashes the
subject of inquiry, and remarks that noth-
ing could be sweeter, cleaner, or more
desirable than the said body. And this awful
anomaly, being thus exculpated, will now take a
firmer grip of its seat than ever, complacently
fold its hands over its collective bingey, and con-
tinue to cable loyal messages and fritter away the
ratepayers’ money. It will go on doing this until
those patient ratepayers—being suddenly struck
by a 25 or 30 per cent, increase in their rates —get
really annoyed and suddenly abolish the whole
concern with its nominee delegates, and start the
Greater Melbourne Council as a going concern.
And the sooner that happens the better.
G-. 8,. on the Stump.
Seeing that ex-Premier Reid of N.S.W. is shortly
going to stump Tasmania, Victoria and Queens-
land in the Freetrade interest this paper thinks
the time ripe to offer that gentleman a suggestion.
It proposes that in this campaign Mr Reid, when
he takes to delivering statistics in support of the
fiscal faith that he didn’t practise, should stick to
the solid truth for a change. When The Bulletin
deals in statistics in defence of its fiscal faith it
quotes its authority, and generally even the page
of its authority, so that anybody and everybody
who cares can, on the shortest possible notice,
refer thereto and find out whether the facts are
real or not. Mr. Reid doesn’t follow this
accurate though possibly rather dreary practice,
for the sufficient reason that his figures and
evidences of the advantages of Freetrade can’t be
found on any page in any authority whatever.
The man who invents his “facts” as he, goes
along, or takes the “ facts” that someone else has
invented for him, naturally can’t give authorities.
But, all the same, it spoils the effect of his
addresses, so far as thoughtful people are con-
cerned. If Mr. Reid would for once depart from
his usual methods, and tell where his wild
inaccuracies can be verified, it would help to pre-
vent him looking like the chartered prevaricator
he looks now. In his last Freetrade campaign he
stated that the population of S.A. for the six
years after S.A. took to Protection (he had pre-
viously-fixed the date of that event at the end of
1886) only increased 19,000, which wasn’t a. fact;
anybody who cares to look at the published S.A.
“ Statistical Register ” can see that the increase
was about 32,000. He said that the protected
barbed-wire industry of Victoria employed three
hands, which wasn’t a fact, the real figures
being 38. He said that starch (one of Vic-
toria’s protected products) sold for l|d. per lb.
less in Sydney than in Melbourne, and anyone
who cares to look at the price-lists of two such
firms as Moran and Cato (of Melbourne) and
Anthony Hordern (of Sydney) will find it quoted
at exactly the same price in each city, so that
wasn’t a fact either. He said that, in the United
States, 78 per cent, of the earnings of the work-
ing-classes over and above what they required for
actual sustenance, or 1365 million dols. a year,
went in paying taxes and, as the whole
national and State taxation of the U.S., as shown
in the latest “ Statesman’s Year-Book,” is less
than 1000 million dols. a year, and about half of
it consists of taxes on land and property, that
was obviously not a fact either, any more than
the rest. He told how, in Protectionist times,
almost every woman in England toiled in a factory
or mine, and implied that Freetrade had changed
all this, and as the two articles on “Occupa-
tions” in Mulhall’s “ Dictionary of Statistics ”
show that the number of female employes in-
British factories has increased from 1.314,000 in 1841
(at the close of the Protectionist period), t 02,380,000
in 1891, that was another fact which wasn’t thei - e.
He told one of his audiences that the number of
male employes in Victorian factories had decreased
during the last five years or so, and anyone who
cares to refer to page 47 of Fenton’s “ Abstract of
Victorian Statistics” can discover that they have
very largely increased, so that that is the same
kind of fact as the rest. In short, there was hardly
an assertion of any kind which Mr. Reid made
in his Freetrade campaign of a few weeks ago
that was a fact. Wherefore The Bulletin pro-
poses that he should this time adopt its practice
of giving his authority every time, so that the
public can know how often lie is his own authority
and how often his authority is some other person
of as little value as himself. Last time, after
being harried over and over again with demands
for his authority as regards the famous starch
Ananias, he reluctantly admitted that he knew
nothing about it personally, but a man named
HIRSCH had told him about it. A statesman
should know things for himself, and not be a mere
trustful repetition of anybody named Hirsch or
Slush or anything like that.

Six Deep.
Whenever it is proposed to do anything for any
Australian city, the fact that it is invariably
divided into 10 to 43 municipalities, whose 10 or
43 mayors and councils can never, by any chance,
agree about anything, rises like a great cat-fight
and blocks all progress. Adelaide can’t construct
new electric trams because of this division, and
the old weary horse drags the superannuated hat-
box structure round the city as of old pending the
agreement that never comes. Sydney can’t get
a municipal gas or electric-light system for
the same reason ; in faot it gets nothing
except in cases like that of the infected
area, where the Government firmly but kindly
boots the whole crowd and takes charge
itself. Melbourne has been paralysed for years
over the question of cemetery accommodation,
and, as usual, its many mayors and councils and
dog-inspectors are the cause. In the old Melbourne
cemeteries the dead are piled about six deep,
so that the bottommost corpse can’t breathe. An
unwholesome flavor of very old tombstone hangs
about them, and the last-buried departed is too
near the surface for sanitary science. Each of
Melbourne’s many municipalities is agreeable that
there shall be a new cemetery right against its
own door, and if it was proposed to establish
about 16 new cemeteries all at once, the matter
would be easily arranged. As it is not proposed
to establish more than three cemeteries, and as
every mayor wants a tombstone right before his
drawing-room window, no progress worth men-
tioning is being made. It is the garbage destruc-
tor business over again, with variations. That
idea is at a standstill because each municipality
wants to put its destructor in the next muni-
cipality, and the next municipality doesn’t see it,
and there is no strong central authority that can
put a large general destructor in the best place,
and drop all the 10 or 16 or 21 mayors into it if
they don’t keep the peace.
♦
When the present Queensland Government
attempts to do a good thing—which is seldom—it
generally spoils it for want of the necessary spine.
Its Factories and Shops Bill is a remarkably
liberal measure to emanate from such a source,
and the clauses dealing with sweating and the
hours of labor are especially promising. But just
as the Bill had got through the Assembly
tbe wholesale merchants of Brisbane woke
up and indignantly demanded that they
should be excluded from its provisions, and
should still preserve the blessed right to
work their employes any hours and under
any conditions they darned well pleased. The
Government had already decided that overwork
was as bad and unjust in wholesale houses as else-
where, but when it found that the wholesalers
wanted to retain the privilege of doing this injus-
tice it climbed down suddenly. By the votes and
support of ex-democrat Drake and the Govern-
ment party generally, the Leg. Council decided
that the wholesale man should be allowed to do as
he darned well pleased, and he is now doing it
with all his might.
Sydney Telegraph is in a sad plight—it doesn’t
know which way to wobble. The journal which
did its utmost to prevent the accomplishment of
Federation stuck as long as it could to the poli-
tician who did his utmost to prevent the accom-
plishment of Federation, because Lyne is
“amenable” —a weak tool in the Telegraph’s
hands ; and Barton won’t be bossed. Chamber-
lain and Hopetoun wanted Lyne to lead the
Commonwealth —and for the same reason;
because Lyne is dead sure to crawl on the steps
of the throne every chance he gets, and export
African contingents galore in payment for his
beknigbthood. There’s no knowing how much
Barton’s crawl is mere lip-service; and, anyway,
it’s certain he won’t be bossed by the Daily
Careygraph. So the Telegraph did its utmost
to ram Lyne down Australian throats ; and when
it was clear (thanks be to Australia !) that
Australia wouldn’t swallow him—not even with
the suggested renegade-GLASSEY for his col-
league—the Telegraph could only bite its
thumbs with Chamberlain, and glumly watch the
success of Barton. There was a day or two’s
advocacy of Parkes (as the Father of Federa-
tion) in order to belittle Barton’s credit; but
people didn’t take much interest in Parkes—
Parkes is too dead. So now the Telegraph is
booming Freetrade, and its arms are gradually
opening to receive George Reid. And still
Boss Carey isn’t knighted. This is really ter-
rible. The Bulletin implores Governor-
General Hopetoun, it implores Joe Chamber-
lain, it implores Queen V ictoria, for Heaven’s
sake, to give Boss Carey a title. Any little
old rag of a title will do, but please give him a
title. Truly he has earned it; and he won’t be
happy till he gets it. The circumstances are so
frightful that if Queen Victoria doesn’t call upon
Sir Major J. R. Randal Carey to arise next
Birthday, The Bulletin will arise him itself.
Anything rather than This Awful Crisis.
From a Bulletin correspondent:—
The joys of living in a Kanaka town. On Xmas five
1000 Katiakas, inspired by whisky, gin, and opium,
started to raise Cain in the town of Bundaberg. A
posse of police, assisted by sundry civilians, managed to
arrest the ringleaders and eventually put down the riot.
Some of these days some of the Kanaka towns of Queens-
land will be laid in ruins by the drunken orgies of their
semi-cannibal population. Nominally, grog is forbidden
and withheld from the Polynesian ; in reality, he gets as
much as ever he wants when a spree is contemplated.
Just think of it 1 A thousand semi-intoxicated heathen
raising Hades round the residences of decent citizens;
women and children unable to stir out without peril of
outrage ; business paralysed for hours in the neighbor-
hood and everyone filled with apprehension. And we are
gravely informed by leading Q. politicians that these fel-
lows “ made the country and created the sugar industry.”
If aforesaid legislators don’t mind, these same howling
savages will yet lay some of the cane-fields in ashes and
rouse such a'feeling in the sugar districts that deporta-
tion may become a necessity. Surely the alien exodus
from Normanton of some few years ago has not been for-
gotten 1

Launceston Examiner, in its issue of 28/12/’OO,
had another bang at its old fetish of commerce,
when it proved to its own entire satisfaction that
.Freetrade was the only enduring truth, by pub-
lishing a table showing the trade per head of
Britain before 1840 (when the Protectionist period
may be said to have ended) and since :
1820 .. .. .. £3 10s per head per annum.
1830 3 14s „ „
1840 ... .. .. 4 4s „ „
1850 6 4s „ „
1860 12 17s „ „
1870 17 17s „ „
1880 20 5s „ „
It wisely stopped there: if it had gone on to
1896 it would have shown British trade down to
£lB 14s. per head. But the really interesting
point is that the Examiner seems to know such
a lot about Britain and so very little about
its own country. If commerce is what it wants
there isn’t a Protectionist State in Australia that
can’t wipe the floor with Great Britain in the
matter of trade per head. This perpetual wail of
the Examiner’s that Australia should go in for
Freetrade so as to get as much trade per inhabi-
tant as Britain has, when the poorest of its States
has already got more, is growing to be very
weary as well as very silly.

The first Federal Ministry of Australia: Edmund
Barton (N.S.W.), Premier and Minister for Ex-
ternal Affairs ; William Lyne (N.S.W.), Minis-
ter for Home Affairs ; R. E. O’Connor (N.S.W.),
Honorary Minister for Nothing in Particular;
Neil Lewis (Tasmania), Honorary Minister for
the Same Thing as Mr. O’Connor ; George
Turner (Victoria), Treasurer; Alfred Deakin
(Victoria), Attorney-General and Minister for
Justice; C. C. Kingston (S.A.), Minister for
Trade and Customs; James Dickson (Queens-
land), Minister for Defence ; John Forrest
(Westralia), Postmaster-General.
N.S.W. gets three Federal Ministers, Victoria
two, Queensland, S.A., Westralia, and Tasmania
one each—the Tasmanian and one of the N.S.W.
Ministers being unpaid men without portfolio. The
results pan out as follows: Westralia gets one
Federal Minister to 171,030 people ; Tasmania,
one to 182,300 ; South Australia, one to 370,700 ;
N.S.W., one to 452,217 ; Queensland, one to
482,400; Victoria, which comes off worst of all,
one Federal Minister to 581,700 people. Forrest,
of W.A., the man from the least-populous State
and the one who stuck to provincialism longest,
thus comes in for the loaf and the fish, while Tas-
mania, which did much good work in the cause,
gets only an honorary portfolio. As Postmaster-
General, Bigjohn —who didn’t even know, till he
made his visit to the East when the Federal Bill
was under consideration, that it was possible to
sort letters in a moving train —promises to be the
joke of the Federal Cabinet.
—•%
That great logician, G. Reid, actually uses it as
an argument against Protection that Victoria has
a good anti-sweating law and a legal minimum
wage—neither of which exists in N.S.W. He
says that this shows that Protection makes these
things necessary. And if they didn’t exist he
would probably say that proved that a Proteo-
tionist State couldn’t afford to abolish sweating or
to raise wages. In like manner George should
be against N.S.W. Old Age Pension law on the
ground that it shows that Preetrade makes that
form of relief necessary. Also against early-
closing and the shortening of hours in coal-mines,
lest they should imply that under Freetrade there
had been late closing and unduly long hours in
coal-mines. Reid’s logio can be boiled down to
two sentences: —
(1) Every social reform that is brought about in a Pro-
tectionist State is bad. It proves that Protection created
the necessity for that, reform.
(2) And every social reform that is not brought about
in a Protectionist State is bad. It proves that Protection
is naturally antagonistic to reform, and that under Pro-
tection it is impossible to reform abuses because Protec-
tion lives on them, and would fall down if they were re-
moved.

Day after day, as they preside -over inquests on
boarded-out children, Melbourne Coroners Cand-
ler and Morrison and post-rnor-temist-in-chief,
Dr. Neild, dilate on the terrible infant mortality.
The present Victorian system of having all such
infants under the alleged care of registered nurses
is, no doubt, better than the old happy-go-lucky
no-system-at-all, but, as Dr. Neild says, the post-
mortems nearly always show the infant’s death to
be due mainly to improper feeding. And though
the inspecting constable usually gives the nurse a
good character as a respectable person who
keeps her house clean—according to his
notions —the early destination of the undesir-
able- infant still appears to be, as of old,
the grave. Also the fact that the said infant
has so often to be inquested proves that
that small entity ordinarily gets little or
no medical attention until two late. The appoint-
ment of married mothers as inspectresses has been
urged, and doubtless the ordinary married mother
of half-a-dozen youngsters knows more than a
large, heavy policeman about the requirements of
the nursery, but the only institution that can
afford any sort of a real remedy to the staggering
infant-mortality of to-day is a foundling hospital.
Society, by the stigma it places on the unmarried
mother, affords a direct incentive to the infant’s
speedy “removal”; as a State youngster —the
child of a country whose posterity is, so to speak,
becoming scarce by degrees and beautifully less—
it would be supplied with properly-instructed
nurses, qualified doctors, the best obtainable food,
and every other inducement to prolong its much-
needed existence.

B. R. Wise (in a cocked hat and sword) at Sydney
military banquet:
He trusted the visitors from England would return
with a feeling of being able to truthfully say that what
Australia had done during the last 12 months had not
exhausted its resources—(cheers)—and that Australians
were ever ready to bear their part in any just quarrel of
the Empire. (Cheers).
Mark the foxy Wise’s reservation “just quarrel”—
as if every Imperial quarrel (including the war to
force Indian opium upon the Chinese) wasn’t a
just quarrel. This is the B. R. Wise who, in
print, 12 years ago, characterised the Soudan
expedition as “ a lamentable eccentricity.” Same
B. R. Wise considered in ’BB that
The full development of civic life can never be attained
in a state of dependency; that subjection to a distant
country, however seldom that subjection may be mani,
fested, exercises a depressing influence upon the national
character, by creating a false standard of public virtue
and private taste.
Same B. R. Wise wrote in 1888 that—
Union with England we qladly believe will come ; but
it must be the union of an equal with an equal, and not
the union of a superior with a subordinate ; so that it is
no paradox to say that the first step towards union must
be Separation.-
And same B. R. Wise (in cocked hat and sword),
now trimming his sails the other way, doesn’t
think African contingents are “a lamentable
eccentricity”; has nothing to say on behalf of
Australian independence; has forgotten that the
first step to union with Britain is Separation from
Britain. Bulletin thinks it about time we bred
a new race of straight-going politicians. At pre-
sent we seem to have chiefly straight-going-for-
billets politicians.
The Queensland Factories and Shops Act, which
came into force on Ist January, is a good measure
with too many exceptions and apologies in it. For
instance, the Minister may forbid the taking of
meals in factories—or again he may not, and he
probably won’t. Yet a factory is not a very
healthy or desirable place for meals. No pre-
mium can be charged to apprentices in clothing
or boot factories unless the inspector consents—
which he probably will. No female under 18 and
no boy is to be employed in any factory after six at
night or before six in the morning unless the
inspector is agreeable. The inspector probably
will be agreeable. In fact, he is quite certain to
be agreeable. Boilers have to be inspected, and
the penalty for increasing pressure beyond what
the inspector certifies the boiler can bear, and
thereby running serious risk of explosion and
general slaughter, is not more than £lOO fine
or six months’ gaol. But apparently it may
be as little as Is. or the “rising of the court”
if the J.P. has a sympathy for unsafe boilers.
No person under 21 is to receive less wages than
2s. 6d. a week after the first month ; why the em-
ployer should be allowed to swindle any person
under 21 out of even the first month’s wage is a
mystery. There is no minimum wage fixed for
persons over 21 —another serious omission. All the
same, the Queensland Factories and Shops Act
has many good points. It provides for the fencing
of dangerous machinery, prohibits the employ-
ment of children under 13 in factories, limits
hours of labor to 48 per week with certain excep-
tions, provides for the inspection of boilers, for
providing seats for female employes, and in various
other ways gives the Bananaland employe a lift
in the social scale.
In the distributing of State railway extensions,
the Philp Govt, showed itself possessed of a fine
sense of justice —and discrimination. The
Northern line is to be extended to Richmond—
whereby the Townsville people, who thrive on
the trade of the Northern line, are to be placated.
The Brisbane lion, again, is to be tickled by a
line to Goondiwindi, and also by a small branch
from Nerang to Coolangatta, both of which
localities are already well supplied with means of
communication to adjacent markets. The desire
of saving them from the hateful influence of
N.S.W., and subjecting them to the sway of
Brisbane, is the root of this outburst of Govt,
generosity. On the other hand, the Central line,

have been done to the Federal assets by the
Private Interest brigade.
