The most terrible show of Commonwealth
week was the “Landing of Captain Cook” at
Kurnell. The idea first struck the capacious
brain of Daoey, M.L.A. (“Banjo” Paterson once
wrote some verses about one Dacey riding on a
mule), and the Lyne Government, which probably
wants his vote, fooled him to the top of his bent.
On the glaring sands and under the glaring sun,
several thousand people, more or less tight,
gathered and hiccuped to see Captain Cook land
once more on the almost uninhabited shores of
Australia. An old, battered vessel was borrowed
to represent the Endeavour. A few obscure
actors were engaged to be Captain Cook and
his companions. iSome of Meston’s tame niggers
were brought down from Queensland to be
the ferocious aborigines. The police (some of
them in a state of hie) chased the crowd
of politicians and others off the beach all they
knew, and explained that the shore was sup-
posed to be practically deserted, and it couldn’t
be practically deserted if 3000 people were there
shrieking with laughter under big umbrellas. It
was a gruesome make-believe, and it would have
saved a lot of trouble if Captain Cook had arrived
at Mark Foy’s shop in the Paddington omnibus,
it being agreed to carry the deception a little
further, and regard the Paddington omnibus as a
ship, and the big drapery shop as a lonely shore,
and Mark Foy as an aboriginal resisting the white
man’s landing.
** * *
The Sydney free theatrical matinees on
Jan. 4 were a complete success, and the theatre
lessees, actors and actresses must have felt
rewarded for their generosity. 8058 tickets were
issued and not many were unused or misused.
Comments were made by all the managers at the
prosperous-looking dress circles.; but the well-
dressed women who received tbhse tickets were
the guardians of children boarded out by the State
dept. A high standard is required by the Board
for these homes, and the guardians had evidently
vied with one another in turning out their little
charges creditably.
*» * *
The “ Bohemian Girl ” is the latest addi-
tion to Musgrove’s operatic list at Sydney Royal,
and it is certainly difficult to remember when
Sydney saw that war-worn old opera under such
expensive conditions. The distracted Austrian
empire is a blaze of color, and is clothed regard-
less of the exhausted state of the Austrian
exchequer. As for the principals, they are at
least highly respectable. There has probably
never been another such Devilshoof as Farley, but
Gilbert King is a good representative of the
character all the same, and a most picturesque
vagabond to boot. He is a smaller and more
frivolous and, judging by his stage complexion, a
harder-drinking Devilshoof than Farley was. As
the opera wears on the slow tenor of its way,
and the years go by in the tattered tents
of Bohemia, Devilshoof grows more flushed
and beery of aspect and more frivolous.
Between acts I. and 11. he has appar-
ently been out to see a dog thirteen years
long, and examined the animal thoroughly; In a
company where the men run so much to stoutness
this active and dissipated backslider is a person
that one is glad to meet. Umberto Salvi sings his
p irt moderately well, and talks it in a strange
accent, and his Thaddeus carries a dead weight of
corporation which must have grievously en-
cumbered any flying Pole. In the first act the
uniform of Thaddeus is a dream of loveliness, not
at all like the uniform of a battered and defeated
campaigner who has been sleeping out with the
Carpathian bear for a pillow. Lawrence Mooney
is a stout, comfortable father, a good actor, with a
fine, clear voice, and his ridiculous conduct in
praying for the restoration of his stolen daughter
instead of lighting out in pursuit of Devilshoof is
excusable, inasmuch as his chance of overtaking
that spry marauder over a rough country isn’t worth
mentioning. All the same, the absolute assur-
ance of the stout father that Providence is a thin,
long-legged institution which will promptly run
down the flying kidnapper is almost pathetic.
Providence leaves the matter alone for 13 long
years, and even then Arline is only restored
through the wickedness of an angry brown woman
who sees her fat Pole being snatched away, from
her, and through the meanness of a nephew who
gets drunk. Yet the “ Bohemian Girl ” lias never
been held up to the scorn it deserves as an irre-
ligious play which teaches that a long gun is
better than a short prayer, and that faith isn’t in
it with a stout heart and a swift pair of legs.
Probably Miss Agnes Janson, in the
rather thankless part of the Gipsy Queen, is
the gem of the present production. Miss Jan-
son is an artist in every respect, and her brown
woman makes the very best of her opportunities.
Miss Lilian Coomber is a pretty Arline and is very
finely dressed when she is restored to the marble
halls of the father who failed to run after her in
her childhood. She does her love-scenes with
some spirit, and her voice, though not very strong,
is mostly on hand when it is wanted. Also
Clarence Leumane’s Florestein is a very passable
idiot, and holds up an expensive uniform which
would otherwise fall on the floor. “ Bohemian
Girl ” isn’t on the Royal stage as a regular thing’
but after “Faust,” “Carmen,” “ Lohengrin”and
“II Trovatore ” have suffered casual resurrections
it makes a further reappearance on Satur-
day. After that its tired feet will probably come
up out of Bohemia at irregular intervals for the
rest of the season.
** * *
The great, dull pantomime at Her
Majesty’s. Sydney, has done well during the
holiday week, but then the holiday week was a
time when even a tombstone would draw an
audience. For the future it has to face the blast
of criticism with its own dreariness and its own
Dan O’Connor as its chief supports. The people
of 2001 A.D., while wandering in strange clothes
on Circular Quay, Sydney, meet an ancient with
white hair that hangs down to his waist and gets
into Gulliver’s ears, and this person, as he publicly
gives way at the knees, says he is Daniel
O’Connor. He also states that Parkes was the
Father of Federation, and asks people to come
for a row in his boat. Daniel, with his hair and
his boat and his collapsible knees, takes up a
great deal of time, and his nearest approach to
being funny is when he comes behind people and
lets his beard drift into their ears, And the rest
of the panto is much on a par with Daniel.
** * *
Sydney has accepted the “ Message from
Mars ” in a, friendly spirit, though that quaint
production is so completely out of the ordinary
dramatic run that it is difficult to place it, and
the public has an uneasy feeling that an attempt
is being made to improve its morals. The public
hates to have its morals improved. By way of a
timely advt. it was cabled last week that Tesla,
the great electrician, was firmly convinced that
certain electric signals recorded by his instruments
were received from Mars, and that the inhabitants
of that planet were trying to call to us across the
barren and unploughed wastes of space. There
was no special relevance about this cablegram, yet
the writer knows one man who, on reading it,
straightway booked a seat for the Palace show
in order to pursue the subject further. He was a
man who took little interest in theatres, too, in a
general way, but he felt that Mars was becoming
a live subject and wanted to keep his eye on it.
** * *
The days of the “New Barmaid” were
few and troubled at the beer-pump, as this paper
prophesied last week. That stout young lady
with the great bush of golden hair will dance
irrelevantly in the public-house for the last time
on Friday night, and “When the Lamps are
Lighted ” is to be staged at Sydney Lyceum on
Saturday. Judging by some faint remembrances
of the drama in question, it seems safe to pro-
phesy that its days will be short and troubled
also; in fact, the management has apparently
foreseen this, and the “ Lamps ” are announced for
six nights only. The writer has some vague recol-
lection to the effect that a certain eccentric slavey
did a great deal to brighten this play on its pre-
vious visit, and trusts to see that girl again on
Saturday.
“Monte Christo” is fixed for at least
another week at Sydney Criterion. Dampier, as
the holy-looking person who tells people to wait
and hope, and trust in Providence to give them a
chance of getting their knife into their enemy, is
as good as ever. In the play there does not seem
much reason for the elaborate scheme of vengeance
which Dampier concocts, nor does it appear why
his hair should vary, or why he should pretend to
be his own brother. Also there is an irrelevant
attempt to murder him which seems to have been
put in merely to fill up time. The play has been
painfully Englished so as to keep Mercedes faith-
ful to the memory of the lost Dantes instead of
married to the other fellow. English sentiment
exacts that the heroine must always keep faithful
to the first man’s memory through the tear-stained
years that intervene between her and the tomb.
All the same it is taking a sinful liberty with a
great story, and some night a short stout ghost
will call on Dampier and ask him in French what
he means by it, and say “ cochon ” and other like
epithets.
** * *
Fitzgerald’s Circus goes on as brightly as
before ; the three Dunbars cleave the air as reck-
lessly as usual, and Rhodesia, the lady juggler,
juggles in her blameless top-hat, after the manner
of one who has overcome the laws of gravity and
is on speaking terms with the Fourth Dimension
of Space. There is very little dullness in Fitz-
gerald’s Circus, which cannot be said of all the
Christmas shows, and the lady who stands on her
teeth and revolves gracefully thereon is, perhaps,
the least dull of them all.
** * *
Sydney Tivoli pantomime still rushes
along, and as it rushes one becomes more and
more impressed by the riskiness of Billie Barlow’s
first costume. It is a costume that doesn’t simply
suggest the “nood” in an ordinary sense; it
rather suggests that Miss Bill has taken off her
flesh and is wandering about clothed in her naked
soul. Noni Rickards is improving, and Mephisto
Diver’s idea of wearing red and green lights
over his nobility seems in keeping with
this time of illumination. The ballet which
dances in the Marble and Floral Hall
of Carrabas’ castle is a great effect ; . there
are two acrobats who do somewhat unusual
things; and a man on a bike ties himself and his
machine in a very large knot in a very small space..
These latter, however, are only clever. The
ballet in the marble hall contrives to look as if it
was a trifle sinful as well, and the public likes sin.
The man who can invent a perfectly new sin will
make a fortune.
*# * *
“Cast Adrift” has been given at the
small lost Sydney theatre where people fall down
habitually with a great noise in the gallery, and
where there is no dress circle. The cast is stated
on the programme to be “powerful,” and the
people who eat peanuts in the front seats are noti-
fied in the same document that carriages should be
ready atJlO.4O. “Cast Adrift,” wherein no one
is cast adrift at all, has a brilliant spot or two of
originality, as the funny man does not listen to
the murderous plot behind a door but beneath the
floor. There is also a girl who is tempted in an
incidental way to be the villain’s unmarried wife,
and who forthwith reels off so much about
her early days, and the innocent rosebush beneath
her infant window, and the father who taught
her to be virtuous, and various other facts, and
altogether makes herself so deadly dull, that she
never gets tempted any more. A good many
people are decoyed co deaths that don’t come off,
and a girl is tied to a rock to wait the rising tide,
which doesn’t rise because it is painted. There is
also a trial scene where a judge addresses an in-
visible jury, and one voice answers from behind
the scenes because the co. does not run to 12 good
men and true. Some wintry storms are also adver-
tised, but they take place behind the scenes, and
do not even howl at the windows. The Standard,
taking one consideration with another, is the best
comedy theatre in Sydney just now.
“ With Flying Colors ” will hold the ex-
pansive floor at Melb. Royal until further notice,
sanctified by the approval of the present historian.
A melodrama that aspires to sanity, but permits
bloodshed, and compels applause for the capital
offence of a felon named Lotan, is not a work to
be passed with a haughty stare. Bland Holt’s
latest is his best offering since “ White Heather.”
It appeals to human sympathies. The harmless,
brainless mariner who goes over to the spectral
majority in the first act gets shot through the
temple whilst singing and pounding away
at a piano, a time when many a fellow
creature might be potted off this mortal
coil with advantage to the Commonwealth.
The report of the pistol having been ap-
propriately drowned in the victim’s roar
of melody, Albert Norman spreads the corpse
neatly upon the carpet, with the pistol in its own
dead hand —the hand that had been smiting the
treble end of the key-board only a minute before.
So the police work up a brilliant case of suicide
against deceased, and the world proceeds as usual,
for one comic songster less, or more, makes no
difference to Society at large. The son of the sea
who fell suddenly from the summit of his
top-note is a warning to the audience, never-
theless. Lotan Hackett likewise points a
moral or two. Judging from his front name one
would expect the blackest wickedness of
Lotan, yet he proves that a man can be kinder
than his god-parents. Lotan rises superior to his
“ bloomin’moniker ”in many respects. He per-
sistently asks his wife if she loves him, and she
replies tiredly that of course she does, and he
believes her every time. Lotan’s astonishment
when Florence breaks in upon his penal servitude
to confess her sins is very pathetic. Other people,
including a giddy old admiral, had regarded the
lady with affectionate suspicion, whereas Lotan
went on believing like a Harmy convert. The
confession makes things quick and lively in
the closing scene. As a first aid to venge-
ance the escaped Lotan hurls an oil lamp
at his betrayer, who wards off the missile
with a chair, after which Lotan bangs his head
through a window, breaks the table across his
back and chokes him. The destruction of Mrs.
Lotan’s lodger by her infuriated old man is more
heard than seen, but the smashes speak for them-
selves, and the arrival of reinforcements with a
new lamp settles any doubts on the subject.
Albert Norman is discovered dead in two places,
with Lotan gloating over the ruin he has wrought.
A few remarks about Bland Holt’s honeymoon
residence are crowded out of this issue.
** * *
“ Floradora,” the place where the perfume
comes from, stands where it did, serene and
beautiful. The same overwhelming crop of pink
and blue vegetation that flourished on the hill
slopes a month ago is still waiting to be cut down
and cast into Cyrus’s distillery. No work has
been accomplished on the island since Rat
Bathurst first told the girls to start out with their
baskets and the dear creatures puffed cigarette-
smoke at him. A right-thinking overseer
wouldn’t expect them to toil. If they merely
go for a swim in the silent sea before
breakfast, and take a stroll among the flowers
to dry themselves, it should suffice to keep
them in perfect health. Carrie Moore declares
emphatically that she hates work. In fact, at
one time or other, Carrie hates everything and
everybody bar Brownlow, and the chances are
that the favoured baritone will be sorry he ever
made a countess of her. Nine marriages are im-
minent when the curtain drops on “Floradora,”
and they all look rather dangerous, but the riskiest
of the lot is the alliance between Irish peer
Brownlow and the dusky little scorcher with the
rolling eyes. If ever he should stop out very late
to record his hereditary vote Countess Carrie will
invade the House of Lords, and drag her particu-
lar legislator home bv the ear.
Last week’s bill at Rickards’s has been
renewed. Lottie Collins is alternately a ran-datl
spinster, a coachman’s wife, and a widow wno
hides her red intentions under an outward sem-
blance of black despair. The Leonards improve
on acquaintance, especially in their old-fashioned
family disturbance re the goodness and otherwise
of their respective mothers. And the fresh young
Sisters Pine also give a new flavor to a form or
duet which was ancient long before they had
parents.
# & *£ *
The Biograph at the Athenaeum Hall
seems likely to supply a want for another
fortnight or so. Some of the pictures are very
good, and some that look rather the worse for
wear wouldn’t be so worn if they were less
interesting. The Bull Fight is patchy but
educational. Although it does not come up to the
fight of fiction it gives a fair idea of how a
matador can scoot when the male cow with the
crumpled horn makes a move in the direction of
business.
** * *
The various pomps and vanities of Mus-
grove’s pantomime continue to pomp as before at
Melbourne Princess’s. Five hundred costumes
during the evening give a sense of satisfaction to
the girls who wear them and the people who have’
paid to gaze on the general result. Also, 1600
electric-lighted particles in Cinderella’s fairy car-
riage form a constellation devoutly to be admired,
and the tiny ponies attached to the transcendental
buggy look like near relations of the rats that
brought the bubonic to Sydney. Likewise,
the scene - paintings are worthy of the gar-
ments in the foreground, and the Darto
dances have an absinthian fascination which
is very acceptable for a change. All the same,
the few sips of Darto one gets at each performance
are quite as much as one really wants. As for the
new British comedians, they don’t yet show any
reason for having come such a long way to do so
little. Two of them impersonate Ugly Sisters in
the saddest styles of pantomome humor, another
is a painful boy-in-buttons, whilst the supposed
principal comedian of the crowd figures as a foggy -
voiced parent who gets considerably lost when
left to himself. In comparison with these com-
monplace strangers, the two local men, Cass and
Berkeley, as a pair of dilapidated bailiffs, seem
about good enough to have been imported from
the Moon. Not that they are very droll.
* * * *
Hard to say whether the “ book ” is
responsible for the depressions that set in between
the bursts of splendour. The fooling that fetches
laughs in a pantomime is worked along the lines
as a rule. A mummer who puts in no capital —bor-
rowed or original—isn’t worth worrying about,
and that’s what appears to be the matter with
Musgrove’s mummers from the northern hemis-
phere. If they have any private resources to
draw upon, let ’em draw and defend themselves
against the imputation. It is an awful thing for a
man to get suspected of prowling about a
pantomime without visible means of support.
The solemn duty of being funny is imposed upon,
every male person named on the bill, and if he
can’t provoke a smile any other way, lie should
roll violently down a steep place, as though he
were getting hurt. Nellie Stewart and her atten-
dant lady-boy go a little more briskly than at first.
The first duet of Nellie and Cinderella also im-
proves with age, and the demure heroine aforesaid
(Miss Elise Cooke) is adding a little new anima-
tion to her other charms.
#* * »
Up to now this has been an unusually dull
Xmas season for Melb. shows owing to the exodus
of festive people to Sydney and the gloominess of
the local commercial outlook. But it happens to
be a season of exceptional attractions at the
theatres. “ Florodora,” a big spectacle as well as
a charming musical comedy, was bound to do
satisfactory business. Musgrove’s panto, is a
record expenditure on dresses and fittings. Bland
Holt has put up a melodrama of the best quality
obtainable nowadays, and the programme at
Rickards’s includes the actually costliest “ turn ”
he has ever imported. Newspaper pars, speak
with awe of the £lOO a we 'k salary which Mus-
grove parts to the Dartos, but nobody has men-
tioned that Lottie Collins on her own gets 25 per-
cent, more than the Dartos’ “century.” Which
seems astonishing only to those who can’t appre-
ciate the difference between a gilt bevelled-edged
artist and a smart stereotyped serio-comic.
“ Smiler ” Hales has been spreading him-
self in London about the alleged adventures of
his past. He related how, when he was mining
reporter on a gold-field journal, and wanted te
find out what was the real state of the case in a
certain mine which had barred visitors,, he went
by night to. the unguarded prospecting-shaft, de-
scended nearly 300 ft. feet of ladders, and then fell
20ft. to the bottom—the last ladder having been
displaced. “I was soon in the workings, and
found the stuff as rich as could be. I went back
the same way as I came in, and the next day our
paper had such a report on the Golden Nugget
Mine as sent the shares up with a jump.
They’d been bearing .their own shares to
buy them in at nothing almost.” The ingenuous
Hales, however, forgot to explain how he fell up
that ladderless 20ft. of shaft! A recent Daily
News review of Hales’s “ Campaign Pictures,”
by the way, calls the author “a new type—a
living symbol of the great Imperialistic move-
ment —”, and says “he learnt self-help, and earned
his 1 tucker ’ under the free air of Heaven in the
back-blocks and the goldfields.” On the whole,
“ A.G.” must want a pretty big hat, now !

The Austral Otis Eng. Co., South Melbourne,
have been successful with their tender for the two new
pump ends for the Melbourne and Metropolitan Board of
Works, Spottiswood Pumping Station. These pump ends
have been designed by the Board Engineer, and are a
departure from the existing ones and weigh about 65
tons each.*

added during the year.”

Since the start of the Broken Hill Proprietary’s
new 5000-ton mill, most of the Barrier mines have been
engaged in remodelling their concentrating plants on up-
to-date lines, and wisely show a preference for machines
which have proved most successful on the difficult fines
and slimes. The machines that can claim this distinction
are the “Luhrig” Vanners, as supplied by Messrs. W. &
J. Lempriere, of Melbourne. The South’s new mill will
include a dozen or so, and the Junction Co. has also de-
cided to erect a number of Luhrig Vanners as an impor-
tant adjunct to its large plant. Other mines would do
well to follow this good example.*

Norman Godkin, of 31 Queen-street, Melbourne,
is prepared to purchase or develope, with option of
purchase, “big prospecting shows” (either gold or
mineral) in any part of Australia. Properties offered
must be subject to inspection. Cable address: “Option,”
Melbourne.*

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footscray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts,nuts, forgings, andallsortsofiron-work.*

London Goldfields, Ltd., Yic. : Five weeks ago
the London and Globe Finance Corporation attempted
this issue. Capital, 1750,000 in 150,000 £5 shares. It is
an off-shoot of the Victorian Gold Estates, and it was
claimed that £lOO,OOO had already been expended in de-
velopment and equipment on this part of the property,
and £50,000 additional working capital was provided by
the issue. The British Australasian said of it : “The
public are strongly fortified by the report of Murray, Vic.
Govt. Geologist, and Mr. Gore, both of whom affirm that
the ground contains gold in highly payable quantities,
averaging at least loz. per square fathom. The net
annual leturn of gold is expected to reach £280,980, and
with expenses at £75,477 there would be annual net profit
of £205,503. The purchase price is a big one (£700,000),
but then the property is a big one, comprising some
4500 acres.” The idea of estimating the value of a gold
mine from its superficial area !

The WILD CAT
COLUNM
The Bulletin has before it a document issued
by the National Mutual Life Association re a loan to one
of its clients on the security of the surrender value of his
policy. It sets forth that the interest on the loan is to
be 8 per cent., if said interest is paid each half-year not
more than 10 days after the due date. If it runs more
than 10 days in arrear the interest is to be 10 per cent.
The National Mutual lends out its total funds at an
average rate of less than 5 per cent. If a person who is
not a client wants to borrow on mortgage of his house it
will let him have the money at 6 per cent. ; yet there is
no absolute security about the mortgage business—in
fact, it is the business on which the life-offices have made
their largest losses. And when one of its own members,
to whom it surety owes more consideration than to an
outsider, asks for a loan on much better security—on
security which is absolutely beyond question, unless the
National Mutual’s policies are not worth their face value
—it charges him 8 or 10 per cent. And the National
Mutual would probably be quite hurt if this was called
extortion and usury.

* * * *
The prospects for Queensland are very gloomy.
The war-bill and the steadily-growing interest on the
public debt have greatly swelled the expenditure, which
is £251,159 larger than for the corresponding half-year of
1899. The natural result of £251,159 increase in expendi-
ture and £167,883 diminution in revenue is that things
are £419,042 worse than they were at this time last year.
If things go downhill at this rate till 30th June, 1901, it is
a fair inference that they will then be £838,000 worse
than at June, 1900, in which case Queensland will close
the year with a record deficit of nearly £BOO,OOO. Also,
the half-year’s loan expenditure (£585,708) is £35,187 more
than for the corresponding half of ’99. With less in-
come, more ordinary expenditure, more loan expenditure
and more interest to pay, things look bad for Queensland.
» * *
They also look bad in New South Wales. The
Lyne Government still persists in regarding the rents
from the resumed area as ordinary revenue, regardless
of the fact that when the price of the resumed property
is fixed the award will carry with it interest from the date
of resumption, which will probably absorb these rents
to the last penny. Therefore, they are merely trust
moneys. The remarkable delay in getting to work to
decide the price to be paid for the resumed area, and to
settle up the payment and the back interest, suggests
that the Lyne Government wants to keep these trust
moneys in hand as long as possible to make its accounts
look good, when in reality they are very bad.
And even reckoning these trust funds as
revenue, things look bad. The revenue is £341,218
more than for the corresponding half of 1899, and the ex-
penditure has increased by £381,324 —therefore things
are £40,106 worse than at 31st December, ’99. If affairs
go on at this rate it may be fairly assumed that the
deficit next June will be larger even than the shortage of
last June, and it was large enough. If the trouble arose
through a diminution of loan expenditure, which cast an
increased burden on revenue, it would be a thing to be
proud of; but it doesn’t. Instead, loan expenditure
(£1,310,302) is £356,455 more than the loan expenditure at
December, 1899. At the present rate of progress it looks
as if the year’s loan expenditure was going to be well on
towards £3,000,000. The O’Sullivan Ministry is having a
great time, and with only half the year gone its visible
shortage is £360,013.
Victoria and Tasmania publish no expenditure
accounts as yet, but with the war-bill still going on, Old
Age Pensions to pay this financial year (N.S.W. doesn’t
pay any until next financial year), its share of the Federal
Government expenses, and £35,000 extra interest through
the M’Lean Government’s borrowings, there is a suspicion
that Victoria is in a pretty tight place. S. A.’s half-yearly
expenditure account hasn’t appeared yet.
Westralia’s expenditure is only known up to the
end of November. Its situation then was : Revenue (5
months) showed an increase of £112,200 as compared with
five months to 30th November, 1899. Expenditure
showed an increase of £230,904, owing chiefly war-bills
and interest on new loans, so things were £118,704 worse
at 30th November, 1900, than at 30th November, 1899. For
the first five months of present financial year, Westralia’s
income just managed to keep £9305 ahead of outgoings.
Apparently Westralia isn’t going to have any surplus this
year. In fact, the only State which promises to have a
surplus on present appearances is Tasmania.

The profits are equal to £3 17s. 9d. per cent, on capital

tion to write off in so short a time in addition to the
ordinary provision for bad debts, but, considering the
present very gloomy outlook in drought-stricken Queens-
land, the wisdom of making every possible provision for
losses is unquestionable. In fact, the only fear with
regard to all Queensland institutions just now is that the
provision may not be large enough. If the present disas-
ters of drought, tick, huge borrowing and Philp mis-
government continue, something heavy is dead sure to
strike Queensland ere long, and make trouble for its
banks and financial establishments generally. At the
same time the Royal is a strong institution and can
stand a lot of hard times.

This looks very well until it is inspected. Then

Another beautiful element of fake is that the

When the without-bonus policies of Australasian offices
are thus placed alongside the without-bonus policies of
the 1.0. F. a good deal of the glory is knocked out of the
latter blatant institution.
. Another curious wile lies in the 1.0. F. taking a
£lOOO policy as the basis of comparison. Assurance
offices issue probably 20 policies of £lOO for one of £lOOO.
The average size of all the policies in force in Australasia
last year was only £272. And though the 1.0. F. charges
less premium for a £lOOO policy (without bonus) than the
Australasian offices do, it charges a lot more on a policy
of £loo—which is a great deal more important. The
figures are about as follows, it being assumed that the
average policy lasts ten years, and dividing the entrance
fee accordingly.

Yet the 1.0. F spends so much more out of its £2 9s. 2d.
in expenses and miscellaneous charges than the two Aus-
tralian offices do that, despite its higher charge, it has
much less left to accumulate towards paying the policy at
maturity than the local institutions have, and it is a fair
inference that its policies are so much the less secure.
Its huge visible deficit backs up this inference.
The Bulletin lays these facts before the public as a
further evidence of the urgent need for a State audit and
valuation of life offices and a rigid State standard of
solvency. If Australia was blessed with such a thing
such a truly remarkable institution as the 1.0. F. would
be practically impossible here.

A second re-arrangement was made in 1899, whereby the
debentures were equally divided into A debentures bearing
6 per cent, fixed interest and B debentures bearing 4
per cent, interest, payable out of profits only. As the B
debentures received no interest this year, the average
rate paid wa3 2j per cent., as before. But for the ex-
penses of this second reconstruction there would have
been a profit of £358 on the ordinary business of the co.
The Universal has a nominal paid-up capital of £129,143,
and, deducting accumulated losses, £105,273 of this is
still intact—on paper. The investments (not reckoning
arrears of interest as assets) are worth on paper £199,965,
and they produced last j’ear £3956 net, equal to almost
exactly 2 per cent. At this rate it is difficult to suppose
that they are worth over £lOO,OOO for immediate selling
purposes, and on present values it would seem that the
paid-up capital is all lost.
The worst feature of the new arrangement is
that future losses on sale of properties are to be charged
directly to capital, while any profits made on the ordinary
business are to go towards paying the 4 per cent, interest
to B debenture-holders. In other words, instead of the
B debenture-holders getting their interest out of any
profits that remain after paying expenses and loss on pro-
perty sales, they are to get it out of any profits that re-
main after paying expenses only, and the shareholders
have to stand the loss on property sales separately. The
best features are that the co. is now able to pay off Is. in
the £ on its A debentures, which will somewhat reduce
the interest burden, and that it is allowed to buy up its B
debentures at their reduced market price, and extinguish
them. It now proposes to call for tenders for the repur-
chase of some B debentures, and if it can find the money
to repurchase these on a large scale it may thereby do a
little towards replacing lost capital. One of the worst
troubles appears to be the £20,000 mortgage on the co.’s
office property, and though this has been reduced by
£5OOO during the past year, the outlook is not especially
cheering. About £llB,OOO of the paper value of the assets
consists of mortgaged properties abandoned to the society,
and this is a huge load of property to carry.

Girilambone : “ Last week 28 tons were raised
averaging by assay 6J per cent, copper.” Then follows
the usual long report showing where this 28 tons came
from—three tons from one spot and five from another,
etc. Altogether, more space is devoted every week by
Sydney papers to the Girilambone mine than is given to
the half-yearly report of the gigantic Mt. Morgan, largely
owned in Sydney. Writer is sick of reading Girilambone
reports, which only show how newspapers can be used as
a means of disseminating mining rubbish.
« * • *
Mt. Boppy, N.S.W.: “The assays from the
lode average 36dwt. gold per ton : width 12ft.” An off-
shoot of the Anglo-Australian Co., which floated the
Gibraltar and Gallymont, and lost a lot of money mining
in N.S.W. The assays on a big scale should, of course, be
taken with a great deal of salt. This co. deserves to find
a mine for its enterprise in different parts of N.S.W., and
writer is inclined to think it has at last found one at
Mt. Boppy—some 20 miles from Cobar.
* * * *
It does not seem that the mid-December set-
back in the lead market was serious ; anyhow, there has
been a partial recovery. One explanation is that tenders
were just then being called for the purchase of Block 10’s
lead output, and it was worth operators’ while to bear
the market. All the same, the tendency for some time
will be downward.
♦ * * *
Of all the Australian States Queensland alone
produced as much gold in 1900 as in ’99. Westralia’s
slump was from 1,643,8750 z. to about 1,577,3000 z. But
that is not half so bad as the slump in prices of stocks.
Taking the quotations just before Christmas in each year
(the last time in the year when markets are steady), here
are some comparisons :
1899. .. 1900.
Associated ..£8 7 6 .. £376
L.V. Consols .. 15 11 3 .. 15 0 0
Brown Hill ..9 7 6 .. 4 15 0
Horseshoe .. 16 0 0 .. 11 2 6
Ivanhoe .. .. 15 15 0 .. 9 15 0
Great Boulder .. 1 16 3 .. 16 3
The knock-out has been not the slump in output so much
as the slump in expectations. What did the damage was
the boom-rise in production from 110,000 oz. in January,
’99, to 205,1860 z. in Oct., same year ; they didn’t reckon
on the basis even of a 200,0000 z. monthly output—they
reckoned on a continuous increase. Hence the tribula-
tion in the Westralian market.
» * • *
The Collie (coal) Proprietary, lately put on the
London market for £200,000, is in the hands of Zeb.
Lane’s British-Westralia Syndicate. Latter takes £25,000
in cash and £75,000 in shares ; £50,000 is to be the available
working capital, and the rest of the shares are reserved. The
mine turns out now only 100 tons a day ; the explanation
is that trucks are not available to handle more. If the
deal comes off—well, the Collie mine is pretty nearly the
only thing in Westralia that doesn’t belong to Eng-
land, and it will then belong to England also. And the
sellers will probably go to England with the purchase
money, in which case W.A. makes just the same loss as if
it gave the mine away.
• • • *
Westralia ; According to Sir Jerry Smith—who
read a paper before the Royal Colonial Institute in the
middle of Nov.—“ The scarcity of water was the great
difficulty, but the completion of the Coolgardie water-
scheme would give a beneficial impetus to mining in that
district which, like many others, has not fulfilled its
early promise, chiefly from a lack of that indispensable
commodity.” Well, any mining man who knew Gov.
Jerry Smith could hardly expect him to know any better,
and his cheek in reading a paper on such a subject is
only endurable in London. Smith, however, took good
care not to name one Coolgardie mine shut down for
want of water ; he might have named a hundred shut
dow r n for want of gold.
* * * •
Ironsides North : “ The winze in the Eastern
crosscut at the 300 ft. level is now down 25ft. in fairly
good ore.” This sort of thing has been heard so often
about the mine that if it ever does strike a good ore body,
there will have to be a big crushing before the news is
believed. Once owned by Hannan’s Proprietary Develop-
ment Co., who most reluctantly sold it for shares. Located
outside the Golden Mile, partly worked by tributors and
surrounded by proved duffers —Hannan’s Central Ex-
tended, Boulder Central Extended, Kalgoorlie United,
Lake View and Boulder Junction, and other mines not
worth a rap.
* * * *
Orossus Proprietary: “Work on the Eclipse
lode will be commenced without delay.” The public has
had much good paper news of this alleged mine for five
years past. The shareholders have passed through one
or two reconstructions, paid many calls, never had a div.
or a crushing, and there is not the slightest prospect of
either. Adjoins the Eclipse lease on the north, and is
supposed to have a payable lode, but it is a long time
crushing. All the other blocks surrounding the Craasus
Proprietary are cats, and in any other mining community
the mine itself would have been abandoned a year ago as
a duffer.
* * • *
Hainault: “The surface is now a scene of great
activity, and the old battery, which was never known to
crush a pound of stone, and which stood so long as a
monument of somebody’s mismanagement in the early
days, has disappeared, and it is anticipated that the new
stampers will be dropping on March Ist.” The man res-
ponsible for the old battery either intended that it should
never crush, or if it did water from it should run uphill.
He gave the plates a slight elevation instead of a fall.
Anyhow, being by the roadside it has served the purpose
of many a joke. The mine has about 2000 tons of pay ore
at grass, but it has taken several years to get it.
* * * *
Hannan’s Star : “The extension of lease of the
plant by the Brownhill Co. has been refused, and in a few
days the mine will start treating its own sulphide ore
bodies per Diehl process. It is lucky to have any to
treat. It could not have had very much six months ago
or it would never have allowed its plant to be used by
other mines. This Co. has been reconstructed several
times, has spent over £lOO,OOO in development and
machinery, had many a crushing, but never a div. Veins
small, ore-shoots short and erratic. Located just south
of Boulder Main Reef and Chaffer’s, but too far away
from the golden zone to have any great or permanent
value. And enough work has been done on the mine to
establish that deplorable fact.
• • • «
Golden Ridge : Ten miles east of Hannan’s.
Floated in London some years ago, and after an attempted
reconstruction had failed, and many thousands been spent
in plant and mining, it was sold, lock, stock and barrel,
last year, for £7OO to a number of miners who have put
through 377 tons of stone lying at grass for 4500z5. And
the miners now begin to think they have really got a
mine.

SOCIETY
LEROY
LIQUEUR
BRANDY
N.S.W. has probably decided by this
time that a whole week of celebration was too much.
It was joyful the first day. It was moderately
joyful the second day. The third day it was de-
termined to be joyful, and was prepared to knock
down anyone who hinted that it wasn’t having a
good time. Fourth day it humped its load of
happiness around like an ancient Israelite carry-
ing bricks to a pyramid on Pharaoh’s birthday.
And after that it toiled in a sour and fagged-out
spirit from one public-house to another, and
wished it was dead.
* * * *
Kitchener’s request for the prompt return
to the Transvaal of those Australasian invalid sol-
diers who are now convalescent, and for sufficient
new men to raise the Australasian contingents to
their original strength, is a sign that the war-bill
is going to dwell on the Treasury doorstep a while
longer. All the same, the position is quite logi-
cal. War isn’t a contract where the soldier can
hand in his resignation when he grows tired. It
is a thing that has to be seen through to the end
if it takes 10 years, and when a country goes
light-heartedly into war then it is its business to
stay in it to the finish. The spectacle of Colonel
Tom Price, for example, stalking gloriously home
from the unfinished campaign is a lamentable
proof that this country hasn’t yet learned to take
its campaign seriously; and the withdrawal
of our soldiers from active service that they
might help to make a Commonwealth pageant
isn’t business. This country may be tired to
death of the war and the accompanying bill, but
unless its Jingoism is all a fraud it has got to
chase De Wet at ss. a day till it'overtakes him, or
drops on the road.
* * * *
After all the fuss it is significant that not
a single London paper is known to have sent a
special artist or descriptive writer to Australia in
connection with the inauguration of the Common-
wealth. There is evidently not much money in
Australia from a London newspaper point of view.
Had the locale of the celebrations been India,
what a rush there would have been !
* #• * *
At last S.M. Herald has unbent so far as
to publish illustrations —of the Commonwealth
street arches, &c. The experiment was not a
success, for unless illustrations intended to be
printed on a rotary machine are drawn by men
very skilful and well-used to the work, they do
not decorate a paper. Up to now, America is the
only place where daily-paper illustrations are
handled effectively.
* ¥ * *
Ye gods ! the Australian poet too finds
glory for the starry-eyed Commonwealth in the
gore of slaughtered Boers that smirches her white
THE LEADING FEMALE.
NEWSBOY (to another): “I thought yer sed yer
sister Kate ’ad a leadin’ part in the panto-
mime!”
TTHE OTHER: “So she has ; she leads the donkey
round the stage every ni hi.”
robe. Brunton Stephens’ first apostrophe is
addressed :
And ye, our dead, who at the call
Fared forth to fall as heroes fall.
He_ talks too of “the record-scroll whose bloody
script attests a nation’s soul.” So that the chiefest
pride of this voung nation is that she is fresh
from choking the life out of another young nation
weaker than herself. Take a map of the world,
mark thin red lines of kinship from Britain to
the Transvaal, and from all the countries that
sent their armies to Britain’s assistance to the
same centre. Then contemplate your “bloody
script,” and rejoice in your heroism in making
one of the thirteen nations that swarmed to the
destruction of the brave little republics. God
help Australia if she have only the chivalry of her
dingoes!
» * » *
Essex Evans has written a “ Masque ” in
honor of the Commonwealth celebrations. The
various portions of the Empire salute Australia in
rhythmical language, and South Africa proceeds
in this fashion
My veldt still reeks with blood and bitterness,
Yet am I come to speak the heart-felt word
Of ]3raise for her who heard my deep-felt distress,
And graspt the sword.”
Any Boer to any swaddy ! Just think of it !
* * m *
The . Commonwealth Review was a fine
show, though the 9600 troops on parade did not 1
come near Premier Lyne’s extravagant estimate.
It was short of the strength of one army
corps, and was not one-twentieth of the force
collected to subdue the little Boer Republics.
Yet, as a spectacle, the review was about the
most striking of the celebrations.
People who marvel over the Australian
outburst of militarism should not forget that the
original English who landed in this country were
attired in kharki (or “canary,” as it was known
in those days). Devotion to this color is only in
the natural order of things. Australia’s historic
birthstain was of the yellowish-brown which is
now so popular.
* • * *
The preparation of a Maori contingent for
S’Africa (after the assurance that the struggle
was to be a white man’s war) recalls that the
Taranaki Maoris are, or were, strong Boer sym-
pathisers. This is due to the fact that they have
had land squabbles with the M.L. Govt, at com-
paratively recent date, so at the outbreak of hos-
tilities they summoned the tribe together and
decided to support the Boers, if not by actual
drafts of men, at least by consignments of food
and fodder. But evidently the matter never went
beyond talk.
* » * *
A petty act of meanness was done to the
returning Victorians by the British military
authorities at Capetown, where they were re-
turned their old Martini-Henry rifles in place of
the Lee-Metfords they had been fighting with.
However, a good many of the men got even with
the British Govt., for the number of horses that
were mysteriously stolen by De Wet during the
few days preceding their arrival in Capetown was
appalling. The contingent also grew suddenly
rich about the same time, and sundry residents of
the locality became possessed of sturdy little cobs.
* * * *
The Commonwealth - celebration official
expenses were originally fixed by N.S.W. Premier
Lyne at £20,000. £200,000 will be nearer the
mark.
* * *
Not the least feature of the Common-
wealth celebrations was the small casualty list.
Though good and careful management may get
the credit of this, Sydney had more than a fair
share of luck. No madder idea was ever
originated than the letting-off of fireworks from
the Town Hall on Thursday night of the “ week;”
yet it was managed without accident. The dis-
play suddenly began with a tremendous rocket,
which started all the horses within earshot plung-
ing amongst the dense crowds in George-st. The
second rocket struck the parapet a few yards off
and spent its energy in the enclosure. Another
missed the tower on the markets by a few feet
and fell into York-st. A better-directed one
landed right on the market building itself, and
another burst on the roof of a George-st. shop.
Showers of sparks from the set-pieces fell amongst
the sheaves of wheat with which the portico was
decorated, and the Roman candles danced amongst
the bunting, but beyond singeing a flag or two and
scorching a few boys sitting almost on the fire-
works, everything escaped.

That it is a truly awful insult to confer an
Imperial title upon a brainy man even the most
“ loyal ’’could not deny if they took the trouble
to consider the question in all its aspects. Dr.
Quick, one of the Australians of the present day
who stand high above their fellows, is practically
shorn of the academical title earned by his own
intellect and perseverance, and is given instead a
knighthood, a thing flung to all the pot-bellied,
tinpot mayors and H-less aldermen of English
provincial towns. By a stroke of the pen, Quick
is placed on a level with Matt. Davies and Bingie
Benjamin—indeed, the fact of his title coming
along some ten years after theirs brands him as
their inferior. Young R. R. Garran is not even
accorded the dignity of this indignity. To him is
AN EASY ONE.

“ Oh, that’s easy. By the time she has a man

chucked a tawdry order, inferior even to that
held by the unspeakable Victorians mentioned.
He is bracketed with the awful sewer-bard Fitz-
Gibbon, and the well-named Jenkins, clerk of the
Vic. Leg. Council. The tardiness of the titles
where men of brain are concerned is one of their
most striking points. Little Surgeon Fitzgerald,
of Melbourne, was knighted but a year or two
ago, after almost a lifetime of distinction, while
the gilded Mayor M‘Eacharn, with three years of
plausible speechifying and the spending of much
money in champagne, gets his handle by return
post. Not only is the principle of titles vicious
and contemptible—their distribution is crooked,
illogical, and unfair, even judged by Jingo
standards.
* * * *
A year ago The Bulletin had something
to say about the untruthfulness of the Jingo press
re the reception given to Australian contingents
by the populace of Capetown. Cabled accounts
of the alleged “enthusiastic welcome” were
utterly at variance with statements to hand from
contingenters, who had noticed scores of scowling
faces, but no enthusiasm to speak of. There
seems to have been a similar conflict of assertion
among Melbourne newspapers as to the reception
given to Vic. troops in Sydney on Commonwealth
Day. The Argus said :
The people of Victoria will be gratified to learn that, of
all the troops which marched to the Centennial Park,
none were more enthusiastically received, and none gained
greater praise, than did the Victorians. As each succeed-
ing section was reached in the line of route, cheers for
the Victorians were raised on all sides.
Whereas the Herald-Standard said :
The display made by the British and Indian troops was
the finest military pageant that has ever been seen in
Australia, yet the applause was not general, the local
people cheering the local troops the loudest. The Vic-
torian troops passed almost unnoticed, save by their own
countrymen, though they marched better than any of the
State forces.
* * * *
A remarkable circumstance in connection
with the killing of Police-Inspector Bremner
(knocked down by a bolting-horse during the
swearing-in ceremony at Sydney on New Year’s
Day) was that he had virtually retired on a pen-
sion after 38 years’ service, and was, on the occa-
sion of his death, doing merely provisional duty
for that day only. But that day was the day !
The present warlike craze extended to Breinner’s
funeral, which was quite a military function.
Policemen, by the way, have always laboured
under the delusion that they are a kind of soldiery.
The Royal Irish constabulary are armed and
organised military fashion, yet the present writer
once heard Dowse, Q.C. (afterwards Judge), say
in court to a member of this body who happened
to refer to “civilians.” “Civilians, sir? Let
me remind you that you are a civilian, and that a
policeman is, if possible, more a civilian than
anybody else ! ”
» * * •
Melbourne Herald of Dec. 31, having
called attention in detail to the local programme
of entertainments provided for Commonwealth
Day, fell into a serious mood and said : “ The re-
ligious services of the day must not be lost sight
of.” Are the churches listening? Here was an
occasion when, by their own showing, they had
arranged to ensure the blessing of Providence on
the birth of a nation. There never had been a more
brilliant chance for the prayer of the Righteous
to operate for the public good. A People’s
Destiny was waiting to be made beautiful by the
combined effort of the parsons, and yet the
Herald didn’t trouble to give them a stronger
advance notice than “The religious services must
not be lost sight of.” What a lovely satire on the
pretensions of the isms !

On 4th October the Beacon Rock, a large
sailing vessel of 1789 tons, left Port Pirie (S.A.)
bound for Wellington (M.L.). Then her history
came to a sudden end, and it was long ago decided
that the Beacon Rock had gone down to be with
the prophet’s whale at the sea-bottom. Now
comes the amazing news that she has staggered,
much damaged, into the port of Concepcion
(Chili), only a few thousand miles off her track,
which seems about as remarkable a case of losing
the road as could be imagined. Even the usual
Rechabitish explanation, that it was all owing to
drink, fails here. A cargo tramp doesn’t carry
enough rum to keep her officers drunk for three
months.

left behind by patients. At the
Coast (N.S.W.) Hospital re-
cently the honorary librarian
discovered on the shelves two
of Zola’s raciest, and a verbatim
report of the Bradlaugh-
Besant trial for publishing

ligious donations were
limited to two of Sheldon’s
books, but there was no
demand for them, while
the others |were in con-
tinuous • use. If all the
libraries are treated as that
one is by Govt, there is
room for reform, for it has
had no official additions since
1892.
* * *
They are a practical
people in S. Australia; the
long grind between drought and
dust has worn off all the sentiment. So
when lately the boys at Adelaide agricultural
school decided to give the headmaster a
present, the usual assortment of toast-racks,
British poets padded, fish-slices, and so on,
was discarded in favor of a lemon-squash
machine, which, it was suggested, might
with advantage be left on the premises.
And there was even less poetry about
the sausage-mincing machine pre-
sented awhile back to the teacher of a
dancing class.
There is talk of a new door-lock that
will abolish keyholes. Judging by recent
Melbourne cases, it will also go far towards
abolishing divoice.

A lady views things from a bus-roof :
On the top of a ’bus one saw the illuminations well for
two shillings. The transparencies were curious. Above
an hotel in old Sydney was a picture of a girl holding
hard to a kangaroo’s tail, while an old man with hands up-
lifted gazed at the strides the kangaroo was making with
its enormous paws of four different sizes. The girl looked
larrikiny, and probably typified the Wexford-st. donah
who will keep Australia back in the march of progress.
Another atrocity showed the emu and the kangaroo
backed up on one side by a well-polished unclothed black,
and on the other by a gentleman in white knee-breeches
and blue coat eating a sandwich and holding a long beer
in his hand. Underneath was the admirable advice
“ Advance Australia,” with the date 1788 under-
neath the first figure, and 1901 under his
bell-toppered brother. Evidently the advance has
hitherto been in tbe. direction of drapery, sandwiches,
and long beers. Another place had hard, metallic waves
with a ship pitching uncomfortably thereon, and the
motto, “The Sea is Our Empire.” But most of these
transparencies were too symbolical for easy understand-
ing in a crowd where one was trampling over other per-
sons or being trampled upon oneself every second
minute. A large number of people, however, drove.
Anything that had wheels and would move behind a
horse was considered a suitable vehicle for the illumina-
tions. An enormous trolly decorated with Union
Jacks held some 45 adults and almost an equal
number of children. A furniture-removal van had
its square house - like roof plentifully strewn with
grown-ups and children, while in an obtuse sort of way it
essayed to turn itself into all the narrowest passages an
route. Fisli-carts and fruit-carts had boards placed across
for seats, with the inevitable flag to drape them, and
gentlemen carrying inverted bottles with candles in
them acted as cornermen. Spider-like gigs used for
breaking-in horses, and calculated only to hold one, were
stretched out to take in papa, mamma, and the twins. A
Commonwealth illumination procession is no respecter
of persons, and the coal-cart moved side by side
with Lord Hopetoun’s carriage. Some people lived

in the electric-trams, taking supper-baskets with
them, and spent the whole night in many
trips gazing up at one street of illuminations.
M.sP. drove pompously among their constituents in
carriages usually devoted “to the memory of.” A vege-
table-John took his missus along to see the harbour fire-
works in his manure-cart, but, with a view to extra style,
he placed in the vehicle a wicker armchair for the lady.
Everyone was patient. Even the horses breathed
earnestly into your face before trampling you down.
The Domain and round Mrs. Macquarie’s Chair formed
one large amphitheatre where people sat solemnly in rows
in the dust and ejaculated “Here she goes” when
fireworks ascended. “ You wouldn’t see fireworks like
these in Wagga,” said a voice out of the crowd. “No,
nor in Murrurundi,” was the reply. A mild gamble was
being carried on between the flights of rockets by a man
who had envelopes with numbers, which he sold to the
crowd for sixpence. It was alleged that there was a
remote chance of someone getting a pound. A flaring
grease lamp illuminated the proceedings. Many people
were enquiring where the advertised “ continental ” was.
At last it was discovered in one corner of the Domain,
where it had disentangled itself from other bands, and
had secured a raised platform with, for a background,
something that looked like the inside of a gigantic walnut
shell. There were no tables, no beer, no lights, no soft
drinks, and no seats—but everyone lay down in the dust
among- the dogs and listened to the music.
* * * »
The decorations at Sydney Mint were not
a success. Outside in the street glowed great
pearls, and the people went underneath with their
mouths open as though they wished some would
fall down. Inside the Mint fence a man with
patched pants went about with a match tied to
the end of a window-hook trying to light up cer-
tain emblematic gas-jets. Gradually he got one
lit, and it turned out to be St. George riding a
steed which had cast its hind-quarters and had a
strangely-unfinished appearance about it. Also,
he seemed to be lunging with an unfinished fist at
nothing in general. On the other side it was even
worse —for there Queen Vic. blazed away merrily
without her back hair. The loyal man with
the match on the window-hook perspired and
struggled, but the back hair of the Queen of
Britain and Empress of India resolutely refused
to flame for more than ssec. continuously. It
looked as though the stars and the breezes in their
courses fought to proclaim to a disgusted city that
their sovereign had no back hair of her own.
N.S.W. coal-owners and exploiters erected
a coal arch just inside the Domain entrance and
yelped wildiy for £2OO from Govt, for doing so.
When the arch had been up about two whole days
it showed signs of bulging, and fissures appeared.
People made bets as to when it would come down,
and two big policemen were put on to keep the
people back, also barricades were erected bearing
the warning remark “dangerous!” But they
wouldn’t keep away. People swarmed up and
asked the “ cop ” if the arch was really dangerous.
Being told it was dangerous they usually
walked underneath it, and looked up at it,
and said they thought it was dangerous and
would probably kill someone yet, and generally
maundered on till the police managed, at the risk
of their own lives, to drag them out. On Sunday-
night the arch came down with a run, and seri-
ously smashed up several people. Whether the
injured persons had read the notice “ Dangerous,”
and then rushed in at the wrong moment to see
how dangerous it was, or in what other way they
got on the spot, doesn’t appear.
* » * *
Cardinal Moran was not so successful in
his precedence campaign as was his uncle, Car-
dinal Cullen, in Dublin, when the Prince and
Princess of Wales visited Dublin Castle. A
State banquet was given and the precedence
question arose between the Cardinal and the Pro-
testant Archbishop. The decision was against
the Cardinal, and he notified that he would not
attend. But it was principally to placate the
R.C. Irish the Prince had visited Ireland, and in
the end the Cardinal’s claim was admitted.
Amongst many other good things done by
Kingston during his long reign in South Australia
was the squelching of public disputes re ecclesias-
tical precedence. Weary of the clergy’s constant
squawk, the Premier altogether struck the clerics
out of the official list—remarked that their
kingdom was not of this world ; and out
they all went. Gov. Kintore, who was quite sick
of the wrangling of the holy men, entered heartily
into the spirit of the coup, which was effected by
authority of a statute giving the Executive power
to settle such questions subject to the approval of
the Queen in Council.
Failing the consent of the N.S. W. clergy to
rank according to seniority, the present trouble at
Sydney should be settled by some such cutting of
the Gordian knot as that adopted at Adelaide.
As matters stand, the independent public must
sympathise with Cardinal Moran inasmuch as
the official recognition of the precedence of the
Anglican Primate and his prayers at the Common-
wealth ceremonies amounts to a colorable attempt
to set up a State Church in a country which,
to use the vernacular, has given the Church
“brusher.” Bishop Smith should have no more
pull in the secular state than Cardinal Moran or
Pastor Howlman of the Little Ebenezer. The
State shouldn’t know any one of them from a
crow.

Victoria practically started paying her
old-age pensions on Ist Jan., yet the very day
after three old women—two in very poor health—
were gaoled for fr.om three to twelve months for
begging. If the Pensions Act isn’t to be a mere
farce it must be applied honestly and thoroughly
to all aged poor whether previously convicted or
not. Moreover, gaoling such is a most extra-
vagant way of keeping them while they can main-
tain themselves easily on 10s. a week and enjoy
their liberty too.

“ Gunner ” on the recent artillery accident
at Sydney, whereby one Imperial soldier had an
arm blown off and others were badly hurt: —
Ed. Bulletin.—ln dealing with the occurrence S.M.
Hebald says that “the breech of the gun became
heated.” Which is absurd—seeing that the thing is
expected to fire five shots a minute, and keep up that
for an indefinite time. Then the Eveninu News talks
about a hypothetical spark in the breech ; hut doesn’t
explain how any such spark could have fired the charge
through its case of rolled brass. (The cartridges of these
quick-firing cannon are enlarged copies of the ordinary
breech-loading rifle cartridges.) Pending the promised
enquiry, however, comment is undesirable ; though it
may fairly be asked why there was a breach of the im-
perative service rule that no firing shall take place unless
a commissioned officer is in charge, and a surgeon pre-
sent? Whether said 0.0. was on hand does not appear ;
but it is certain that the surgeon who ought to have been
on the spot had to he ’phoned for when the accident
happened.
* V * *
A Melbourne tradesman, on almost the
last day of the year, shot his wife with a gun,
killing her instantly. The slayer, who suffered
severely from sunstroke several years ago, and
appears never to have thoroughly recovered,
seems to be a product of too much iron roof. The
family, which included four small children, lived
in a three-roomed iron-roofed hot-house in a small
street in Richmond, and the tiny, stuffy rooms
and the tiny roof, and the squalid backyard, com-
bined with the effects of a sun-heat of lOGdeg.,
seem to have been too much for the weak brain.
Verily, the Municipal Councils and Boards of
Health that allow people to build alleged dwel-
ling-houses on pocket-handkerchief allotments in
10ft. lanes, and roof them with galvanised iron in
a,climate as hot, and in a country as wide as this,
deserve to be grilled for all eternity.

success in the Tyson-estate busi-
ness draws attention to the British
Government’s successful onslaught
on the estate of the late Baron

tried, without much success, to
establish Jewish colonies in South

millions allocated in England, for
the purpose of carrying out his
schemes for the benefit of his co-
religionists. On this enormous
sum the British Govt, has just
succeeded in making good its claim
for the sum of 1£ millions. How-
ever, they want it all just now.

The Sydney statue of Australia’s alleged
idol, Capt. Cook, was treated with great disre-
spect, and not decorated with a single light during
the celebrations. Governor Phillip’s effigy, on the
other hand, had fountains playing over it, with
limelight effects. It is not quite clear whether
this was meant t o symbolise Australia washed of
her past, or whet her Phillip was personally in
need of a bath, or a cool shower was expected to
please his ghost when the latter found the heat
and glare a cruel superfluity.
m * * •
South Australia celebrated the Common-
tvealth by knocking one-sixth off the sentences of
prisoners in gaol for a first offence, and making
smaller remissions in the case of old offenders.
Yet, as the Government can’t knock off one-sixth
of the black eye or the broken head which the
first offender had given to some harmless citizen,
and which led to that first offender being in gaol,
it isn’t quite so fair a deal as it looks. The public
has some right to be considered as well as the
criminal class I—as 1 —as there is more of it, it has more
right to be considered. Personal safety is miser-
ably insecure already in Australia —largely by
reason of the inadequate sentences dealt out to
assault-and-battery men ; and it doesn’t need to
be made any more insecure by hastening the
release of the few assaulters and batterers that
have got to the place they so well deserve. If
the Commonwealth had been celebrated all over
Australia by adding five-sixths on to the
sentences of all persons in gaol for crimes of
violence it would have been a much more kindly
act to the great, peaceful majority. Said majority
will be assaulted again with comparative im-
punity as soon as the push gets out of gaol, and
to hasten its release, so that it can batter the
public all the earlier, is calculated to bring the
Commonwealth into disrepute. If this famous
Ist of January could be remembered hereafter as
a time when life and limb were made more secure
by a special discouragement of the brick-throwing
industry many decent people would look back on
it with gratitude.
* * * *
The ghastliest thing done in the name of
the Commonwealth was a gigantic daub hung on
the outer wall at Govt. House, Melbourne. Who
perpetrated the evil thing heaven knows, and no
doubt his name is entered in the book of deadly
sins; but it was supposed to be a _ cartoon repre-
sentative of the Young Queen, as invented by R.
Kipling, being blessed by the old Queen, as
adopted by the same R.K. Australia (the Y.Q.)
was pictured coming up out of nothing with in-
flated nostrils and a glare of wild surprise in her
eye. In her hand was a naked and horrid sword,, and
she looked like a female pig-sticker touting for
business. In the background were swarming
swaddies shaking their fore-paws in the air. That
was all. Politicians, poets and parsons gush about
the peaceful aspirations of the Commonwealth,
and the Government of a leading State can findno
better emblem for her than a boozy-looking
amazon burning to disembowel something, cheered
on to her fell purpose by professional slaughterers.

One democratic point about last week’s
Sydney festivity ; it broke down the railway clas-
sifications. “Second-class ” swarmed over “ first-
class ” carriages; and “ first-class, ” when it came
late, hung on the platforms like anybody else, and
was glad to hang.
Time was when closely to his side
Man wisely chained his chosen bride.
And if he wearied in due course
Death granted him a sure divorce.
To-day the chain is with us still,
But drawn so long wives roam at will ,
And love their bondage for the sake
Of little liberties they take.
