A WOMAN’S LETTER.
_ ~ Sydney, January 8, 1901.
My Dear Myek.-
Every night for a week Sydney has been an
enchanted city. Steeples, domes, parapets, all
were rimmed with fire. The Post-office tower
was a contour of light drawn with a pencil of
name against the sky. The Domain was a sea of
glitter, beyond which the moon looked sickly and
dim. From the Palace Garden fence, across the
irregular line that marked a regular sea of heads,
was to be seen a concave stage, a tiny affair fresh
from a toy-shop, upon which gyrated and danced
timer puppets. Sometimes one puppet waved its
arms in the stifling air, and sounds of music arose
from l lie other puppets below. In the fierce,
steely glare from the great are lamps every move-
ment was visible. Then, too, the Milky Way had
fallen into Hyde Park avenue, and lay there,
glittering with all the colors of the rainbow.
Down Macquarie-street North, draped and draped
with the Dutch flag, opalescent lights glowed like
pearls set amidst red and blue sparks till
the fairy street culminated in the arch at
Govt.-House gates, glowing with soft golden
lights. All was beautiful.
But the picture of pictures was in Palace
Ga djn. There the flower-beds were outlined
with colo e ! lights. Away beyond, on glit-
tering waters, rode ships of fire. Governor
Phihips statue towered up, and streamed
with sprav. The figures were mere shadows
m the lu uinous darkness, but the spray was
real, and it swayed, and drifted, and turned
miny hues and fell with a murmuring sound on
the recumbent figures beneath. Up in the sky
blazed a c-own of silver light, sprinkled with ruby
sparks. Its beams lit up the outline of the dome
of the Colonial Secretary’s office, and across it all
ever and again drifted thin, shadowy clouds of
smoke from the electric-light engines. These
high, pale drifts lent a suggestion of immense
height and grandeur to the picture—a picture
from the Austral Nights which far outshone any-
thing in the Arabian tales. There are a few
things in life worth remembering, and the memory
of the fountain, the iridescent spray, the dome
and the crown of light with the dim, beautiful
landscape beneath, as seen from Palace Garden, is
calculated to light up Eternity—if, as we pray,
there’s any scarcity of illumination there.
The Hotel Australia claims to house the Most
Distinguished. The large expanse of John Forrest
and his blonde-haired wife are put up there. Also
Chief Justice Way from Adelaide, Miss Blue (his
stepdaughter), and his wife, the tallest of all the
official women, and, many people say, the most
charming.
A curious thing happened to one lady’s linen-
box. Its label was, in some mysterious fashion,
rubbed off, and another affixed bearing the name
of a bald-headed legislator. It went to his rooms,
wa3 found by his pretty young wife, and—
tableau ! The things were sheeted home to the
right owner after tremendous explanations on
both sides.
At the great State Banquet on Federation
night the galleries glittered with decollete women,
mostly in black brightened with jet or silver
sequins, and looking impossibly important as their
menkind unrolled the Federal napkins. Mrs.
John See and Mrs. Sam Hordern foregathered
with a train of pretty young See and Hordern
girls. The new Mayoress, Mrs. Graham, was in
black and specs. We have never had a Mayoress
in specs before, and, considering whom a mayoress
has to look at, hardly know whether to envy her
or not. The past Mayoress, in black and turquoise,
sxt with her daughter, Mrs. Carty Salmon, in a
low-necked blouse. Mrs. Toby Barton wore
b ack with a big pink chou on her shoulder, and
continued to hold up her reputation for being the
smartest local political woman. The wife of M.L.
Chief-Justice Stout and Mrs. Besant-Scott, Annie
Bssant’s daughter, were also in the galleries.
The Govt, nobly laid on tea, coffee, and the
fumes of fizz for the galleries. The result was
that the gallery thought the roses on the table
much too thickly arranged, and reckoned that the
decorations generally lacked brilliancy.
By the way, the menus of that Banquet have
already become objects of value. People are
offering a guinea apiece for them. The card is
rather good to look at, with wreaths of delicately-
drawn native flowers round the Tortue Claire, the
"Vol Vent ala loulouse, the Dinde Farcie ala
Perigord, and the other French language. The
outer cover is a blaze of crimson and pale blue,
and is effective, if not novel.
The ladies’ gallery had its enjoyment in watch-
ing how much a certain distinguished personage
ate. They sav he refused nothing. Poor 1
I hear it loudly stated he even chewed crumbs
between courses, but if women have really dis-
covered that, they have discovered that he isn’t
the gourmand his country painted him.
The man who eats bread between acts is as the
man who would eat an apple half-an-hour before
dinner, and that’s the final test of a good man, you
know. The man who does either is no gourmand,
be.ieve men.
William Zeal added a pictorial note to the ban-
quet by going out in a blaze of rage and regi-
mentals. They didn’t put him at the top table,
so he left; but it is an open secret that he now
regrets his zealous action, and would like it hushed
up and forgotten as quickly as possible.
Bananalander Griffith made a wonderful, mean-
dering, interminable speech. In spite of the ban-
quet s forked interruptions, he went steadily
through seventeen years of history. In looks he
resembles George Bibbs, with a beard. He rolled,
waved hopped bowed, jumped till the gallery
thought—well, 111 stop short here. Little Samuel
YVay sat beside him, and looked like crying several
times when his companion bumped his chair.
George Reid made “an entrance.” He came
iate, and had a reception. He is clever
at that kind of thing, but, even if he had
been early, no one could have overlooked
him his Froggy Would a Wooing Go” in
white tight trousers and a tight cut-away orna-
mented coat. Lyne and Barton sat side by side,
solemn as owls, with their big heads slowly
moving a little from side to side as the brilliancies
they would let off presently agitated their con-
sciousnesses. Attorney-General Wise looked like
a beautiful policeman in fancy dress, and went
away early. The banquet opened dully. Every-
one looked boiled-eyed and bored. But the scene
changed pretty quickly. I watched the editor of
a high-souled daily sitting silently beside a comic
artist. But, next time I looked, they loved each
other like brothers. It was wonderful how soon
that whole banquet loved each other like brothers.
Yet all the brotherly love and what led to it
(federation I mean) didn’t evoke one single bril-
liant speech. Premier Lewis, from Tasmania,
was the feeblest spokesman of the lot; Samuel
Griffith the longest; the Admiral the dullest;
Lyne or Barton the heaviest; Colonel Wyndham
the best pronounced; Chief Justice Darley the
tamest; and Reid the only one who made himself
heard; and having said the last, I might have
left the other unsaid, perhaps.
The exhausted Excellency was in rare fettle at
the choice and select banquet at Government
House last Wednesday. When complimented on
his improvement the pale little Gov.-Gen. re-
marked dryly that he guessed he was growing
something like his photo, again.
Hie great thing on Thursday hight was to get
a Government invitation to “A Slessage from
Mars.” All the d.c.’s were taken by the Govt.,
and the theatre tickets distributed without pre-
meditation, so they say. However—without pre-
meditation, of course—the Palace held the fashion-
able audience; though, I believe, “Monte
Christo” and “The Hew Barmaid,” and the
Panto., and the Opera all had a title or so allotted
to them.
In the Palace d.c. was the Madden party. The
Westralian Forrests sat in the front row, and be-
hind them were John Downer, his Sydney wife in
white satin and turquoise blue, and her papa,
Mr. H. E. Russell. A pretty woman in black
supported a superb diamond necklace in the lower
box. Her long opera jacket of rich white silk,
brocaded in big bunches of violets, hung on a nail
in the box, and was much admired. Sydney
society turned up in large crowds ; also it wore a
sulky expression. The stalls were a brilliant mass
of our own Extinguished Guests, cut out by the
Melbourne and other visitors. Mr. and Mrs.
Henry Dobson from Van Dobsonland were in the
stalls. It was a brilliant house but abnormally
quiet. But by> that time the week was at Thurs-
day, and a little pensiveness was inevitable.
You will be glad to hear that the bright, sweet,
wholesome play—does that sound dull, the play is
never dull—is catching on. It went slowly at
first, just as in London, where it afterwards
settled down to a two years’ run.
Sydney en feting leaves many people “off.”
For instance, the Union Club’s spree on Common-
wealth night. Heaps of women have mentioned
to me that they were there; but, when asked
what happened, they all alike reply vaguely—
“ Champagne.” That’s all that’s to be got from
anyone—in a speaking sense, that is.
Melbourne visitors are narrowly watching the
brightest gem of their Sassiety, basking day after
day in the boyish smiles of the Sydney beau who
made the running in Melbourne during the Cup
junkets.
Apropos of nothing, everybody is observing the
altered looks of a high ecclesiastic, whose round
red face has gone haggard to a pitiful degree ail
of a sudden.
Talking of looks, this Ad. from the S.M.H. will
not be the most run-after thing of the week I
imagine:—
E N'TLEMAN would like to meet refined, educated
V young lady, not good looking, talented and lady-
like. Advertiser in good position, and has some
capital, and only genuine noticed. X. A. M., G.P.O.
The sudden demise of fine old Edward Knox
has been a shock to a large section of local society.
Though he had long been in feeble health his
death was unexpected, the day before seeing him
at the Cathedral service, apparently in good
spirits. The Union Club is postponing its ball
indefinitely out of compliment to its highly-
esteemed President, whose charming Presidential
manners will not be easily replaced.
The Society of Artists’ Commonwealth Show
lacks but one thing to its brilliant success—big
crowds to fill its rooms and buy its pictures. All
the States have sent in. Result: one of the best
shows ever seen here. Entering, Blamire Young’s
“ Music Master” captivates and keeps the eye, so
droll, so brainy. Two youngsters kneading a
duet, and the Music Master listening. But, ay di
mi, the Music Master is musician all over his big,
loose feet, his young, blue eye, his tired, flabby
old chin, and his lank, black hair. If you can
digest your humor and pathos mixed, this
clever poster will meet the demand refresh-
ingly- Near by, Norman Lindsay hangs two
of his marvellous pen-work illustrations for
Bocaccio, but as they are shown thus publicly you
will understand they are not quite typical of
those sensational Boccacio Pictures that art
people rave over, and which will, no doubt, ulti-
mately be published for “private circulation.”
Turning to big works, Julian Ashton covers more
than lots of wall-space with his “ Jewelled Margin
of the Sea,” a tenderly-painted seascape, the sort
of picture one yearns to dip into this hot weather.
It has been bought (for, I am told, £230)
for the Queensland National Gallery. His
“Sacristan,” a very strong watercolour head,
has a double interest—or would have had a few
weeks ago—for the man in the cowl does so look as if
he knows something. Can it be about a cause
celebre? Sir Julian Salomons bought that Julian
Ashton study with the pink rose in her hair, and
a tumor on her left chest. The head and face
are affectionately handled, but the tumor —!
And the perky Q.C. ordinarily buys the
chocolate-box sort of picture—some stout
ladv in black satin and cream roses.
If he is now forming a museum of anatomical
deformities you will admire his start. Howard
Ashton seems to have been lying on the sands of
time somewhat long, and somewhat often. Three
of his exhibits, “The Hunter,” “The Beach,”
and “The Sands at Bondi,” all clever, but weak-
ened with their sameness, warn the rising young
artist to get off the sand pretty soon. His charm-
ing little “Aurora” shows a woman’s head, with
floating hair and a background of young rose-
clouds.
Anyone who knows Henry Lawson may be
forgiven much raving over Longstaff’s portrait of
him—in my opinion the best' portrait in the
exhibition. You will be glad to hear N.S.W.
National Gallery has bought it.
Apropos, when Henry Lawson sat for this he
came along in smart johnnyfied white shirt, with
great, white, starched cuffs, pulled right down
over his skinny knuckles. He looked so com-
placently at those cuffs that the artist
hadn’t the heart to utter an objection. He
painted the portrait, and painted the cuffs, faith-
fully, if in pain. But as soon as the long, bony
poet had departed, out came the big, white
patches so foreign to Lawson’s nature, and in
went the pathos of those bony, angular wrists that
are as much Lawson as his face, and his poetry,
and his doggy eyes that follow you all over the
show.
Longstaff’s portrait of Mr. F. B. Suttor only
wants hoofs and a tail to accentuate the pleasing
likeness to a universal gentleman friend. I was
going to spell friend without an “ r,” but that
might cast a cloud on my genuinely pellucid com-
pliment. Next it are the red robes around the
sweet old Chief Justice. In the cause of Justice,
why is one hand half as large as the other? Sid.
Long comes out with two portraits of two good-
looking women who are evidently friends of the
artist, or they might have objected. His treat-
ment lacks graciousness. However, the public
will like Sid. Long better this year, for he knows
what he means, and so will they, and he makes a
big success with his pool-and-gum-tree picture,
livened with comprehensible sheep instead of
Pans. The little president’s “Frosty Sunrise”
shows big river banks and a rather tired, rather
hard, stream. That might be one of the weariest
rivers that flow somewhere safe to sea. If this
picture is cold, so much the truer.
The Misses Hambidge, Adelaide artists, send
some charming miniatures, full of life, even of
diablerie, not a usual quality in this sleek, smooth
kind of work. Mrs. Muntz-Adams, from Queens-
land, erstwhile a Melbourne girl, shows a faithful
portrait of a young French doctor, and a full-
length girl who might have been better named
than “Portrait of a Lady.” Another strangely-
named canvas is called Madlle. Blank, by Violet
Teague, where you would expect it to answer to
the name of ’Arriet. Alice Muskett has a clever
symphony in green in her “ Day at the Morn,” a
long, thin girl in green gown and a sash, with an
illumined and artless smile against green tree
leafage. And speaking of women’s work, artists
find much to admire in Lilia Reidy’s “ After the
Bath,” but the uninitiated may reckon that,
despite all the nudeness, one calf badly wants
re-vealing—to borrow Brunton Stephen’s sparklet.
Further down the street the Art Society has
opened its Commonwealth Show. Many of the pic-
tures here are well-known old favorites, but, from a
crowded wall, Englishman Wainwright’s portrait
of Dr. Riches stands out with a compelling vigor
and force, full of psychology as well as art. Mrs.
Edmund Barton opened the show on Monday.
Both Shows have been disappointed at Lord Hope-
toun’s refusal to perform their opening ceremonies.
He has promised to attend during the Exhibitions,
but that’s all. The first taste of Governor-
General.
The Art Society’s walls abound in ten-acre pic-
tures, something noticeably large being Lister-
Lister’s “ Crossing Hunter River.” The trustees
wanted to buy this, but couldn’t, because Lister is
a trustee and couldn’t buy his own work, but it
gets there all the same ; the artist has presented
it to the trust. Bernard Hall’s clever “Andante”
might be added to the Museum of Anatomical
Curios above-mentioned. The nude woman,
with yellow and green flesh, lying on delicious
grass, presents a hip that might be anything from
stuffed turkey to a Xmas ham. Walter Withers’
gallery oil, “Nearing the Township,” is sombre,
tender and shadowy, with a strong, strange list
to starboard, the road, gum-trees, the telegraph-
poles and the paddock-fences all sloping leftwards.
“A Street in Spain ” is a Maorilander’s work, a
water-color by Ohas. Worsley, clean and animated
enough, but “we know it of yore.” Frank
Mahony’s magnificent chestnut-horse picture has
been lent by Mr. F. B. Suttor.
But see what comes of asking other States to
hang with you !
Sydney Art Gallery has made five Melbourne
purchases, but none from Sydney. Phillips Fox’s
“Lady in Black” and “Autumn Showers”;
Longstaff’s “Lawson”; Walter Withers’ “Land-
scape (Art Society’s Show); Helen Peter’s
“ Fireside Scene,” and Florence Fuller’s “ French
Village.” It was left to the Brisbane Gallery to
buy^Julian Ashton’s “Jewelled Margin of the
Sea. ’ - Samuel Griffith lost no time in snapping it
up as soon as he saw it.
After all these pictures, one thinks of others.
Honestly, and taking a high standard, there’s not
one picture in either show that comes near Great-
ness. What is lacking ? Is it brains, is it force;
or is it a lack of sympathy with the primal force
of greatness “Simplicity ? Out of many of our
canvases a giant would clear screens, mantel-
drapes, figures, side-issues, superfluous frills—all
unnecessary.
With simplicity goes what George Moore calls
the inevitable. I wonder have I missed the one
picture among all these hundreds from beloved
fellow-countrymen—the one picture that had to be
painted, and could never have been painted any
other way—the one picture that hides The Inevit-
able like a jewel in its breast !
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ This year the arrival of a. new baby is quite a
fashionable form of Xmas entertainment. Foung
Mrs. Stephen Rowan, formerly Miss Eileen
O’Hara, has just welcomed her first-born—a
daughter. Nowadays, Mrs. Rowan’s home is
‘Talleyrand,’ a station near Longreach (Q.), but
she has been for the last few months in Melbourne,
where, before her marriage, she had as gay a time
as might be expected of the dashing daughter of a
fashionable surgeon, more or less closely con-
nected with many notables. Mrs. Frank Travers’
Noel baby adds another great - grandchild to
the quiver of old Mrs. Stephen, mother of
the Lieutenant-Governor’s wife. Mrs. Percy
Chirnside’s little stranger is a second son. Baby’s
pa revels in the role of Lord of the Manor, and a
year or two back, when the first-born arrived,
great rejoicings were set on foot among the Werri-
bee tenantry and vassals. ‘Mrs. Percy,’ nee
Fenner, a pretty little fair woman, has enough to
do to hold her own socially against some of the
more assertive matrons of hubby’s clan. Not
that the Fenners count themselves less distinctly
hall-marked than surrounding metal. Quite the
contrary. The Percy-Chimside heir is weighed
down by his string of aristocratic names derived
from Ma’s ‘ Cavalier ancestry.’
“ Rumour hath it that blustering Colonel Tom
Price is going to marry again. His first wife died
some _ months before he went to kill Boers in
S’Africa, and his daughter (who was under the
matronly eye of Mrs. Baronet Clarke during her
pa’s absence) is likewise engaged.
“On Saturday (Dec. 29) all the ‘Florodora’
stars glittered in a garden by the sea—the soene
of a fair for St. Agnes Church (Blackrock). Pro-
moters boast a fair amount of ‘neck,’ don’t you
think, dragging stage entertainers all that dis-
tance, especially as the Church would probably
not see its way to respond if asked to contribute
a dozen crack clergy to plead the cause of some
needy theatre or underpaid actor. (
“The belle of the Williamson bunch, which
arrived in a gleeful four-in-hand, was Grace
Palotta. Her eyes look bigger and bluer, her
complexion fresher and her cheeks more dimpled
than they do on the stage. She wore dainty
white muslin flowered with black, grey suede
purse-bag fastened to her belt, and cream feathers
in her hat. She sang a pretty coquettish
little thing called ‘A Lesson with a Fan,’ and
when encored gave ‘lf you can’t afford a
Hansom, take a ’Bus,’ to her own accompani-
ment. Carrie Moore, in a little white muslin
and black hat and white veil, put ever so
much fun and feeling into her coon ditty.
She’s a gifted youngster, and looked very
pretty on Saturday, with her white cheeks and
liquid black eyes. Carrie had with her her chum,
Cesca Vollugi, whose voice was perhaps the best
on tap. It’s a pity the tuneful Signorma doesn’t
push forward faster. Besides her voice, she is
blessed with lovely golden hair, a sweet smile and
an agreeable face, but though she’s a biggish,
buxom girl, she suffers from a dowdy deDortment.
Cecil Englehart looked better than she* sounded,
in blue-flowered muslin and a hat turned up in
front. Maud Chetwynd, in simple pink muslin,
affected all-black hat like every one of the theatri-
cal ladies. The party included Dorothy Vane
(Mrs. Norman Menzies), in greeny-blue Eton suit
with white silk applique trimmings, who seemed
enlivened by the company of her old chums, and
who, with Miss Menzies, shared their homeward
drive.
“ Suppose that six curates and six pretty dea-
conesses could bring themselves to help some
shivoo held in a theatre, I wonder how shocked
they’d be if they found the approach to the har-
monium and hymnbook stage was through a sleep-
ing-room with an ostentatious bed in the middle
of the floor. It was rather Arcadian to see Ken-
ningham, Lauri, and Co. gallantly handing their
fair colleagues through, and absolutely refusing to
be aware of that bed’s aggressive presence.
Yours affectionately,

One of the dangers of Sydney during the holi-
day season will be the burglar and sneak-thief. The
Federation festivities will attract all the rascaldom of
Australasia, and plate and purses will be constantly
menaced. Those who arc leaving Sydney, and visitors
to Sydney, therefore should deposit their plate, cash
and valuables in the Sydney Safe Deposit for security
Access can be had to them at any time during* business
hours. The vaults are situated behind Paling’s, off
George-street, and are always open for inspection. They
are in themselves a sight worth seeing.*
PERSONAL ITEMS.
After his experience at that “ State ban-
quet,” Chief Justice Griffith will be likely to
remember that Sydneyites don’t like a long, dull
speech just after their food.
George Maurice O’Rorke, the M.L.
Speaker, has succumbed to the strain of the Syd-
ney Federal festivities, and has retired to a pri-
vate hospital suffering from bronchitis.
Edmund Barton, in his opening speech at
N.S.W. Society of Artists, expressed the hope
that art would advance in “ this colony.” To say
“State” is apparently as hard as writing 1901.
London Times mentions that M‘llwraith’s
estate is valued at £1778 net. He died intestate.
It wasn’t worth while making a will. In
M‘llwraith’s latter days nothing was worth while.
Alfred Deakin’s name is conspicuously
absent from the list of ennobblements flung about
on Jan. Ist. Does the emotional orator from the
Garden State propose to remain true to his early
democratic instincts ?
The knighting of softgoods man M'Millan,
if a tribute to the mercantile section, is looked at
askance by a couple of bigger Sydney merchants ;
and, if a tribute to political labor, is scowled at by
a score on the John See level.
When was the Agent-General for N.S.W.
knighted, or do coming events cast their shadows
before ? Cutting from Letts’ Diary, 1901, No. 46 :
New South Wales.— Sir Henry Copeland ; S. Yardley,
C.M.G., Secretary , 9, Victoria-street.
Where is that title for D.T. boss-director
Major Randal Carey—for which we were naively
prepared by a Sydney D.T. leader? And the
fire-snorting N.S.W. Colonial Secretary, John See,
is also still cruelly left in a state of expectancy.
'Adelaide Critic says that Rupert Clarke
will soon cease to be “our only Australian
baronet,” as Madden of Victoria is to be shunted
up to that honor. In the name of all that is
wonderful what has become of Baronet Way ?
Sculptor Bertram Mackennal has arrived
in Australia just at the right time. His marvellous
Circe, of Paris Salon fame, he has brought with
him and will exhibit complete unless the
Scotchbyterian crowd bar the reliefs on the
pedestal, as was done in the English Academy.
When Lyne was commissioned to form a
Federal Ministry the Australian Star exultantly
said : “ Our Premier is not a man to let the grass

Thoinas Glassey gravely assures the pub-
lic, in a letter to Brisbane Courier, that now
Federation is a fact he is perfectly content to
leave the Glassey family settled in Australia.
The great heart of Bananaland heaved in joy at
the announcement, and the country settled con-
tentedly down to its customary calm.
Over the front of the Divorce Court,
Sydney, hang various Federal-Union mottoes:
“One People, One Destiny,” “The Crimson
Thread,” and so on. Above the court’s main
entrance is a crimson heart labelled “ Wedded.”
This is interpreted as a delicate compliment to
Mr. Justice Stephen, now away on his second
honeymoon.
Mr. G. T. Loder, who died at Singleton
(N.S.W.) last week, aged 78, was a native of the
Hawkesbury. His grandfather was a soldier in
the 102nd Regt. (the N.S. Wales Corps) and his
father was born in N.S.W. in 1796. Hume, the
explorer, was the maternal grandfather of the
recently-deceased, and Loder accompanied him in
many of his expeditions.
Lieut. E. A. Roberts, M.P., the chirpy
little Labor member who went to the war from
S.A. with the Imperial Bushmen, says that the
young British officer is a reckless and overbearing
nincompoop, and that no courtesy can be expected
from any Imperial commander under the rank of
a lieutenant-colonel. The generals, however, are
considerate and polite always.
Dickson, who as this is written lies seri-
ously ill in Sydney, is the only member of the
Federal Cabinet who is not an Australian native.
He is its patriarch, too, with 68 years at his
credit, while Deakin is the infant, his age being 44.
Each of the States has a son in the Govt., save
only Queensland, as Lyne was born in Tasmania.
The honorary Ministers, O’Connor and Lewis,
are omitted from the age calculation, for Lewis is
but 42.
An indomitable law lighter was Henry
Ricketson, the 75-year-old Riverina and Victorian
squatter who recently died near Melbourne.
Land-suits were his specialty; Ricketson v.
Barbour is among the best-known leading cases
decided by Privy Council. It was G. C. Tompson,
who a few years ago died of heat-apoplexy on a
track outside Bourke, that heard the case; his
decision was repeatedly over-ruled until the
Privy Council was reached.
In the distribution of Commonwealth
“honors” the man who comes off worst is Symon,
Q.C., of South Australia, who can no longer dis-
guise the fact that his name is Josiah. Sir Josiah !
Formerly he was Mr. Symon, or J. Symon, or
Jos. Symon, and most people took it for granted
that his front designation was nothing worse than
Joseph. And now his Josiah rises up against
him like a buried crime whose bones refuse to be
overlooked any longer.
It would really appear that J. H. Grey,
the absconded Geelong solicitor, who robbed that
dreary hamlet of everything but the holes in its
socks, still remains within easy call of the scene
of his crimes. A correspondent assures The
Bulletin that one energetic victim-in-a-small-
way bounced his belongings out of certain parties
by giving them the choice of forking out or having
the secret of the missing solicitor’s retreat pub-
lished within 24 hours.
The Federal pyramid for which Premier
Holder aims is the Chairmanship of the Inter-
State Commission, which carries a fat salary and
has more promise of permanency that Cabinet
office. No doubt circumstances will rule his
choice of the role of awkward friend of Barton or
open ally of Reid according to which is most
likely to secure the end in view. One thing is
certain —Holder will win election to the House of
Representatives easily, and Kingston is the only
man who has a chance to beat him at the polls.

date for the Senate. When he had his 2-col.

he was a Freetrader and an Imperialist; but most
people expected that in another day or two he
would be a Single-tax Protectionist and a Repub-

for Yosper’s versatility. He died early on Sunday

Josh Symon, the new Adelaide knight, is
independent of his law-practice now ; he is owner
of about the biggest and most up-to-date vineyard
in the province. Like Kingston, he is a glutton
for work. But the one is Scotch ; and the other is
partly Irish and a little Portuguese and a good deal
Australian, also Kingston doesn’t own any vine-
yard. And yet he had the chance. Por his
father, for years Speaker of S.A. Assembly,
owned what, in his day, was one of the best vine-
yards in the province, at Marino ; but it has been
utterly neglected, and is now a tenantless ruin,
hardly a vine remaining.
The notorious Jabez Wright, Broken
Hill’s Labor Mayor, didn’t mince matters
in his reply to the formal invitation to the
Commonwealth celebrations: “Thanks for offer
(of free railage, &c.,); but I have more important
work to do than attending the national drunk.”
But there is an outcry against Jabez—he scorns
the “Mr. Mayor” or “ Your Worship ” form of
address—and for once in his career he wavers. If
he does not have a bad fall meanwhile, he will
probably be the Labor candidate for one of the
Barrier seats when the sitting members “go
higher.” Though very illiterate, he is an effective
speaker and a born fighter.
In N.S.W. Chief-Justice Martin’s time
one Plumb, who was chief court-keeper, applied
to him for a fortnight’s leave of ab-
sence to recuperate on the moun-
tains. “ Better take a month,
Plumb,” said Martin, “and come
back a new man. I’ll find someone
to fill your place in the meantime.”
But before the month was out Plumb had to re-
turn to assist in black-draping the Banco Court on
account of the C. J.’s own death.
Everyone is asking how the doughty Jos.
Symon, Q.C., of Adelaide, came to get a knight-
hood, seeing he fought so hard against the altera-
tion of clause 74, and in the battle used such
vitriolic language re Lieut.-Gov. Way and other
magnates. Most people seem to think that some-
how Kingston worked the oracle across the sea for
his boyhood’s friend and latter-day ally. All the
bitterness of a few years ago—Symon would not
shake hands with Kingston when he returned
from England after the Jubilee celebrations —have
now been buried deep.

Ihe death of Bishop Salvado, of New
Norcia, W.A., at the age of 86, is reported from
Rome, for a trip to which city he left his Wes-
tralian monastery a year ago. He began training
W.A. aborigines in 1846, and has worked at the
business ever since. Early in his missionary
career he was told by the Bishop of Perth that his
enterprise couldn’t possibly succeed, and that he
had better abandon it; but he believed in his work
and started to raise funds by a musical programme!
which resulted so well that he was enabled to buy
a waggon and load it up-and return as his own
bullock-puncher.

A.P. supplies trom Milparinka, N.S.
Wales, some facts concerning the “ Major Pelly,”
whose death at Perth and mis-identification with
the imaginary bushranger, “Starlight,” have
been chronicled
About six years ago a military-looking man of about 60
years of age arrived here with two good horses, heavily
packed ; and put up at the local hotel for some months
He styled himself Major Pelly, and spent a good deal of
his time sketching. Several of his sketches are to be
seen here still. One day the landlady of the hotel, hap-
pening to go into his room, found the “ Major ” busily
diately pulled his sleeve down. On another occasion he
engaged in an altercation ivith an old man called “Dad,”
when dinner was called. The old chap sat down to
dinner and was hardly seated when the “ Major” slipped
to the door, and, covering him with a revolver coollv
said he’d blow his brains out. “ Dad,” however wm
equally cool, and remarked, “Fire away, old man'’
The publican grabbed the weapon and found it loaded in
all chambers. Not being able to get his revolver back,
“Pelly” vowed he’d follow “Dad” to Tibooburra and
blow him up with dynamite, which he said he carried.

G. Y. Smith (Vic.) recently deceased, was
Postmaster-General in the second M‘Culloch
Govt., and retired from politics about 20 years
ago, living on slender private means. Before his
election to Parliament he was a miner. He is
remembered as the inventor of the term “ iron
hand ” applied to the closure adopted by the
M‘Culloch Govt.
A Gippsland surgeon went out with the
Cameron Scouts, and, although a surgeon, boasts
of having fought, rifle in hand, right through the
business, and can tell how many Boers he slew up
to the time when a Boer potted him with a slug
that cut the heart and pierced the lungs. But he
made a miraculous recovery, and now talks of
returning to Africa to shoot some more.
In Jan. ’B6 Gladstone offered Chamber-
lain the post of First Lord of the Admiralty, but
the pacific Joe declined the tempting favor on the
ground that it was not congenial with the feelings
of a Radical, or consistent with his professions, to
be the head of a department whose vast expendi-
ture was lavished upon the means of making war.
Now the same man wants to paint the world red
by force of arms.
Colonel Champ, afterwards Inspector-
General of Vic. Prisons, formerly Colonial
Secretary of V.D.Land in the Leg. Assembly at
Hobart, was one night championing the con-
tinuance of Transportation. As an instance of the
benefits of the system, he gave the case of his own
farm, which, he said, could not be worked at a
profit but for cheap convict labor. “ Look,” re-
plied Member Gregson—“ here is a man who con-
fesses that he couldn’t get a living had God made
all men honest! ”
Fitchett and Stead have parted company.
They settled-up during Fitchett’s recent trip to
London. The two men couldn’t “ hit it ”at all—
Stead as the Liberal, Fitchett as the flaming
Imperialist. So the Australian Review op
Reviews now belongs to Fitchett. He sloshes
out what he chooses of Stead’s democracy, and in
goes the other thing, well warmed-np. "Fitchett
also owns the Southern Cross, the Presbyterian
organ, though he is a Wesleyan. He began on
the Wesleyan Spectator, but the fools there—2o
years ago—let him go, because he would not work
for nix, and wanted a trifling remuneration,
merely on principle. Nathless, Pastor Lorimer
Fison, a genial Laputan, edits the Spectator
with better stuff than the Cross. But Fitchett’s
forte is anything but theology.
On 30th December, 1900, Bigjohn Forrest
finished his tenth year as Premier of Westralia.
Forrest’s is the record Ministry of Australasia.
The long-distance Governments of the Seven
Provinces to Ist January, 1901, are as follows,
everything that lasted over five years being in-
cluded :

Aboriginalities.
“Nell Cook” : Was camped in the bush
on Queensland border lately, cooking for ring-
barkers, and one morning there hove in sight a
weird little leathery-looking woman, with two
small boys, and carrying in a small cage a morose
cockatoo. Apparently she had come to pay a
morning call, and firstly she remarked “ Hullo ! ”
in a creaky, small voice, as though rusted for want
of use. She said she lived “ over there,” with a
vague wave of the bony, brown, little hand. She
carefully placed the cage on the ground, and from
the way she and the children prostrated them-
selves around the gloomy bird it almost looked as
if they were bird-worshippers. Nor would they
have anything to eat until the cockatoo had been
refreshed. Under the stimulating influence of
tea and cake the woman delivered herself of
a few items of information concerning herself and
family, but the small, tired voice only became ani-
mated when alluding to the parrot. She was the
wife of a boundary-rider living near. Had lived
there a long time, didn’t know how long. Did
not know how old she was, or where she was born,
but (triumphantly) knew she was born somewhere;
had once had a little girl child that died, but for-
got how long ago. The children were at first
like small wild creatures. Their first shyness
wore off, examining everything with a monkey-like
curiosity. The clothing of the boys was of the
most elementary sort, while the woman looked as
though she might have been dragged through
countless wire-fences for several centuries. She
might have been any age. Her hair was burnt the
color of rusty iron, and done up in a knob in size
and shape like a reel of cotton. Finally they
drifted off down creek, all of them again so intent
on the parrot that they neglected to say good-day
to me, and on inquiry I found that this female was
not considered a peculiar type in those parts.
• * * *
“ Alces” : While at work on a crossbred
jumbuck a Casterton (Vic.) shearer cut in half a
lively brown snake concealed in the fleece about
the animal’s neck. A Queensland drover recently
told me that once, in skinning a ewe, he found a
death-adder coiled up in a neatly-made nest in the
belly-wool, which bore traces of lengthy occupa-
tion. The snake was very sluggish (it was in
winter), yet how the ewe escaped being bitten was
marvellous, as the reptile must, from its position,
have been subjected to a deal of squeezing when-
ever the involuntary hostess lay down.
• • * *
A Queensland lady writes to London
Spectator apropos a reference, in the fanciful
travels of Sir John Mandeville, to the long-apple,
which is soft, soon goes rotten, and shows the
mark of the Most Holy Cross when it is cut open.
A good many of Mandeville’s descriptions, sup-
posed to be fictitious, turn out to be thinly-
wreathed realities. This lady says his long apple
is the banana, and when a ripe one is cut across
there is an appearance as of a cross.

muchee that pfeller?”
HE: “ Oh — pennyweight!”

(Deal promptly effected.)
“R.W.S.” : A man recently told me that
he was paid by three different candidates to per-
sonate them in the riding-tests for the S.A. Con-
tingent. Got ’em all through, too ; but, when it
came to his own test, he got a nag that wouldn’t

“ Suffolk ” writes re the question whethe*
the dingo is or is not indigenous to Australia
(“P.H.C.,” 8., 13/10/’00): —
In Moree School of Arts library is a slab of sandstone
(the top bed of the foot-hills) on which is imprinted the
pad of a dog, 4in. x 4in., also the right foot of a human.
Also what are considered to be Phallic emblems (casts of
which are in the Australian Museum under the title of
“ Aboriginal doctor stones”), shaped out of a carbonate of
lime. These stones were found in a hollow tree on the route
taken by the Bellinger blacks on their annual excursions
to celebrate their Bora rites. Now, this human footprint
in the carboniferous sandstone and the Phallic emblems
tend to prove respectively that Australia was inhabited
whilst the rocks were in process of formation, and that in
his journey from Asia the early inhabitant of Australia
brought the emblems of his religion with him. All over
Moree district, away from the river system, the various
wells sunk prove it to bo a vast mausoleum of the remains
of extinct animals —fossil bones of the nototherium,
diprotodon, and smaller animals being invariably met
with at depths varying from 12ft. to 80ft.

“T.S.”: Re paragraph (8., 27/10/ ’00)
about the dingo feigning death in a time of diffi-
culty, the habit is by no means peculiar to that
animal. I have heard of a bank-manager doing
the same thing while the accounts were being
overhauled. The bank manager is a rather
lengthy animal with a smooth black covering and
a black cloth tail, and is of a burrowing disposi-
tion. Some of your readers may have seen him.

“ D. 8.” on a matter of hospitality :
“Geo. T.” says (B. 20/10/’00) that he has never met the
station-hand described by me as wanting in civility to
strangers, but abounding in servility to his “ boss.”
“ Geo. T.” can’t have travelled much of late years. There
was a time when hospitality and civility to a stranger out
West were the rule ; but, as an effect of closer settlement
and incidental competition, they are to-day the excep-
tion. I reiterate that the average station-hand loathes
the approach of a traveller in any form —perhaps, because
he instinctively feels that his billet is thereby menaced.
This is too well-known by those who have travelled
through station-country to be refuted by anybody vege-
tating in the dreary Thompson locality. 1 should know !
—having called at over 60 stations in the last six months.
I make, however, an exception of shearers, whom I found
(with some exceptions, of course) to be a fairly-enlightened
and sociable class.

of 33051b5. (29fjcwt.). Same par. says that in 1854

“ Lar ” : When I was overseer on a cattle-
station worked by niggers at the head of the
Warrego in the early days, the
manager, who had gone for a
spell, suddenly wrote up that
. he’d got married and was bring-
ing the missus along shortly.
The niggers didn’t wear a
tack, so I and one of the
jackeroos reckoned we’d
rig out the three gins that
worked about the home-
stead. We looked out
some stiff white bed-
chintz, with red flowers
on it, got a pair of shears,
some sail needles, some
strong thread, took a
sugar-bag as a pattern
and started dressmaking!
When we had got the
“dresses” fixed up they
were too long, but were
so narrow that, as the
gins were in good condi-
tion, we cautioned them
to play light or the
things would bust. The
day the missus was to
arrive we dressed the
gins, ran a piece of green-
hide through the hem-
things round their necks
and tied it behind, and
when the gins put on their
old belltoppers and stuck
their pipes in their mouths
they went about the place
yelling with delight every
time the chintz crackled.
When the missus arrived,
I told the gins to run up
their horses to the back-
door and show white missus (a town girl) how
well they could mount and ride. Up they
came, yabbering and stumbling over their gowns
till they got angry, when they ripped out some
steep oaths, tucked their “dresses” right up
under their arms, jumped on their horses,, and
went whooping off pelt for leather over the rise.
* * * *
“ Kinirral ” : Niggers on the Warrego
(W.Q.) are always made “plenty sorry” by a
death. The corpse’s nearest-of-kin are banged on
the head with a nullah-nullah till they bleed, and
then dragged over and around the new-made
grave.
* * * *
A. bushman in a journey of 60 miles
recently between two Queensland stations had
twice to crawl over a causeway of dead and putre-
fying beasts, then skim the surface of the floating
filth to get a drink. His horses refused the first
hole, but were eager for the next.
“ Bios ” : In the report of the ’96 Royal
Commission on the fishes of N.S.W., a cer-
tain peculiar white pellicle always found in
FIAT EXPERIMENTUM.
1ST. DOSSER: “’Ave a drop, mate?”
2ND DO. : “ What’s in it?”
1ST. DO. : “I dunno. I found th’ bloomin’

the cod is gravely stated to be the actual picture
of a tree near where the young fishes are spawned
—produced by some sort of prenatal influence.
The appearance, however—as I know from dis-
secting scores of cod—is not pictorial but struc-
tural ; being, in fact, a system of arteries essential
to the life of all Australian fresh-water fishes.
The “trunk” is the basal artery; the limbs
ramous arteries ; and the anastomosing branchlets
form the rest of the “ tree.” Bushmen and fisher-
men still swear by the “natural photograph” ;
but it is regrettable that so vulgar an. error should
be perpetuated in an expensive publication which
has been distributed to scientific institutions all
over the world.
Many of the regular highways in Queens-
land are at present closed to traffic through the
drought, and numerous station homesteads are
deserted for want of water. The other day a
Govt. officer after a holiday in Clermont had to
take train to Rockhampton, then on to South
Australia per sea; train again from Adelaide
some hundreds of miles, and lastly per camel to
his destination, “ Oontoo,” a town on the borders
of Queensland and S. A. A glance at the map will
show what such a journey means.
“BF.”: When I was a raw new-chum
I had’ occasion to take a coach journey. We
stormed at a little roadside house at dead of
night. I had a large flask of whisky with
me in my bag, and, thinking to do the
right thing, produced same and gave
drinks all round, including the sullen-
looking owner of the wretched hole.
I wondered at the time why every-
body should regard it as such a
good joke, but since then I
have often wondered more
what were the actual remarks
made about me by the un-
licensed grog-shanty keeper
after we had taken our de-
parture.
“Gilaomi”: In an
old “Sands’ Directory”
Burrowa, N.S.W., is des-
cribed as built on both
sides of Ryan or Dissen-
sion Creek. Can anyone
say how the watercourse
got the name ? It is jocu-
larly said that the big
recreation-ground lying be-
tween the two parts of the
town andthrough which the
creek runs was put there to
keep the Ryans and Dwyers
apart. Putting up at a Burrowa
hotel recently I chanced it and asked an atten-
dant if her name was Ryan. She replied, “No,
sir, it’s Dwyer; but it was Ryan.” So perhaps
the feud is healed.

“O.” : Cavern mentioned by “C.F.”(8., 20/
10/’oo)has evidently at onetime been asubterranean
watercourse, entering the Murray just below
Swan Reach. Length of cavern unknown, as
nobody has yet got to the other end. Present
writer, at all events, was quite satisfied to return
after about a mile on all-fours and in intense dark-
ness with innumerable bats fluttering around.
From a large gum-log about 200yds. from the
mouth —carried there probably by water —some
suppose the cavern to have been formed by a
channel leaving the Murray somewhere bet ween
Overland Corner and Pyap and taking a short cut
across the N.W. Bend (underground).

“ Suffolk ” : lie size of iguana. One Sun-
day in 1883, whilst on mounted police patrol near
Burragorang mountain (N.S.W.), I was having a
pitch at a settler’s when one of the children sang
out, “Father, there’s a goanna running away
with one of the sucking-pigs ! ” The old man got
his gun, followed the reptile, and shot it. It was
6 feet long and thick in proportion. Several
“suckers” had been previously missed.

then you could talk.”

been 20 years ago?”
TYSON: A BUSHMAN’S YARN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He’s my ruddy off-side leader,
And a skulking swine at that—
He’s as lazy as an M.L.0.,
And as cunning as a rat;
He was always d n well eating,
And he never seemed content,
And “ Hungry Buddy Tyson ”
Was the name by which he went.
If you turned him into clover
He would guzzle till he’d cough,
And he’d burst upon a haystack,
If you didn’t hunt him off ;
He had very little conscience
When he felt inclined to dine, .
And the women wouldn’t trust him
With the washing on the line.
We.ll, one day it chanced to happen
I was rocking down the Gap,
When someone came behind me
With a rattling, blanky trap ;
And when the polers rushed a bit
He heard the waggon jolt,
And Mr. Ruddy Tyson
Laid his ears back for a bolt.
I spoke to him and soothed him,
Then I jumped in front and swore,
And I chopped him with the greenhide,
But he gave a ruddy roar
And sent me back and spinning,
And he galloped down the track
With fourteen ruddy bullocks
And the waggon at his back.
They were going very nasty,
With their heads laid to the creek,
My mouth so full of dust and oaths
I couldn’t make it speak,
When just as smash seemed imminent
And everything to H—ll,
The blanky off-side leader
Tucked his blessed head and fell.
Then, the others in behind him
Took some headers on the road,
But the brakes were on the waggon,
And they stopped the ruddy load,
And when I poked the bullocks
With the whip-butt from the dirt
I couldn’t find a cripple,
Or a single bullock hurt.
And I found the ruddy reason
Why the ruddy leader stopped.
He saw a bit of butter-scotch
Upon the road and propped,
And now whene’er I think of it,
And see the ghastly sight,
I thank old hungry Tyson
For his ruddy appetite.
FREE LANCE.


Trials of an M.F.
No legislator is better known in Queensland
than Mr, Andrew Lang Petrie, M.P. for
Toombul. Mr. Petrie was elected by that con-
stituency in 1893, and his supporters’ appreciation
of his ability and integrity is shown by the fact
that he still represents it.
Speaking to a reporter recently Mr. Petrie
referred to the fact that his life had not been
without trials. Questioned further he said :
“ For many years I have been troubled with
rheumatism, which caused a dull, aching pain in
my shoulders and limbs, and stiffness of the
muscles. For years also my digestion was im-
paired. My appetite quite deserted me, and
although I forced myself to eat I could not relish
my food ; after meals there was a sense of weight,
discomfort, and sleepiness. At times there was a
bitter taste in my mouth. Headaches now and
again troubled me, and my ill-health seemed to
take away the activity which formerly character-
ised me. The medical skill available to combat
my complaints seemed to give only slight relief.
Having read good reports of Dr. Williams’ pink
pills I decided to try them. After using them for
four weeks an improvement was noticeable, and
thence onward my progress was rapid. Before long
I was enabled to discontinue Dr. Williams’ pink
pills, as they had cured me completely both of
rheumatism and indigestion. As it is over two
years since I used them there is no doubt about
the permanency of my cure. Several of my
friends and relatives have obtained great benefit
from them, and I can personally recommend Dr.
Williams’ pink pills to all sufferers from rheu-
matism and indigestion.”
Mr. Petrie’s recommendation endorses what
thousands have said about the merits of Dr.
Williams’ pink pills : by enriching the blood and
retoning the nerves, they cure anaemia, debility
and insomnia, paralysis, rickets, St. Vitus’ Dance,
fits, erysipelas, dysentery, effects of typhoid, etc.
Sold by chemists and storekeepers, and the Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s Place Sydney,
two and nine per box, six boxes fifteen and'three-
pence, post free. Write for free pamphlets.*

SCHWEPPE’S DERATED
SARSAPARILLA
AT A WESTERN PUB.
THIRSTY TRAVELLER (spitting out -water): “ Holy
wars—soap !”
MRS. PULICHOUSE: “I’ve told that wretch of a

“ Alpar ” : When I was spreading refine-
ment and culture at Possum Hollow a big bush
larrikin came one day to the gate of the select
bark seminary, and wanted to know why I had
whacked his little brother Jerry. Also, he in-
vited me out to see if I could whack him as easily
as I did Jeremiah. It was hot, and I wasn’t feel-
ing good that day, so I didn’t go out. Instead, I
loosed on him the two biggest girls in the school
(sisters), about 12st. each, who had a grievance
against the hulking object at the gate for speak-
ing disrespectfully of their mother. The pro-
ceedings were short and unscientific. Last I saw
of the bush lout that day he was covered with the
footmarks of the two girls, and was trying to get
a large quantity of mud out of his ear.
* * • •
“ H.S.” Me the dingo-question :
About Kimberley, W.A., there are some very big native
dogs of a dark brown color, as well as the smaller, fox-
like species common all over the continent. In both
species the forehead is flat, with “ pricked ” ears, having
generally a forward lean. The dingo, when travelling
fast, always cirries the head high, the tail extended
horizontally. The aboriginals catch the young puppies
and take them to the camp, where they are reared at the
breasts of the gins—suck and suck' about with the
picaninnies ; some of these gins become almost as much
attached to the canine as to the human. About the
most remarkable characteristic of the dingo is the amount
of battering he will survive. As to his sagacity, a
friend of mine once left a box of fowls outside the camp
at Cambridge Gulf, the box having strips of hoard nailed
securely over the top. In the morning every fowl was
still in the box, but all were headless ; the dingoes had
quietly lain in wait, and at daylight snapped off each
head as it popped up between the b ,irs. Excellent cattle-
dogs are often found in a cross of dingo and collie—
somewhat severe, but very hardy and fast, and nearly
always with that great desideratum in cattle-dogs—good
feet.
» * • * "
“Dodger”: Me tight corners. Billy
Y , a crack camp-rider of the Dawson, was
once “ shouldering ” a big bullock, hoop,
horns, when by some chance the bullock got
one horn through the ring of the bit; but
after going thus for a couple of hundred
yards at top speed the beast got his horn
free.

“F. 8.”: A friend of mine noticed
near his camp a trap-door spider run in front
of him and pop into its hole, pulling the
“lid” down as it disappeared. The lid
seemed so neat and perfect a circle that the
man stooped to examine it, and found, to his
astonishment, that it was a sixpence ! There
was nothing but silk thread covering the top
of the coin, but underneath mud and silk
thread were coated on and shaped convex
(as usual). The coin had probably been swept
out of the tent with rubbish.
It is announced officially that about 45 per
cent, of the sheep in Hughenden district have
died. A drover took away about 1000 sheep to
save them. They arrived at country containing
whitewood shoots, ate thereof voraciously, and
died to the number of 850. The drover took 17
hours to travel seven miles, and only kept himself
alive by drinking the blood ¥ a sheep he killed.
* * * •*
An English paper re the Governor out-
rages :
Advices from Australia state that the blacks have risen
against the whites in the outlying distncts and massacred
great numbers of the inhabitants, who were taken by
surprise. A large proportion of the victims were women.
They have not yet learnt in Hingland that the
whites have retaliated by organising a head and
scalp hunt in the scrubs and gullies of Mt. Erebus.
“Bluebell” : We had on the station a
blind old gin known as “ Boko Mammy,” and once
when she fell sick the tribe thought she had had
a pretty good innings, and were on the point of
burying her alive—they’d got her sewn up in
her ’possum rug and all —when we rescued her.
However, she “tumbled down” soon after, and
we saw them doubling her up and cracking her
knees. I’ll never forget the sickening sound !
“ A. A. A.” ; Re crystallised hollow pebbles
(8., 27/10/’00). It was not water that those
pebbles (most probably quartz) contained, but
carbonic acid which had been taken up during the
process of crystallisation, and liquefied under
immense pressure. The liquid generally fills
about two-thirds of the cavity, and pebbles con-
taining it are not very rare.
“A.”: “Tomahawk” (8., 13/10/’00)
mentions as an uncommon exploit the scaffolding
of big timber by means of the “shoe.” It is an
every-day occurrence with the splitters in the
Tasmanian gum forests. The “shoe” is a
piece of timber roughly shaped, with the
axe, unshod, and driven into a narrow
hole previously cut into the tree about 7in.
Have seen a Tas. axeman fix a length of
round, green sapling not more than 3in. in
dia., at a height of 20ft., and stand there in,
seemingly, perfect security. Exhibitions of
“shoe scatfolding” have been given at
various times in Tas. The “ shoe ”is gener-
ally about 4ft. long, and made of split
timber.
Re liquor adulteration (B. 13/10/'00),
you don’t need to go to Queensland to
get liquor that will “bite all the way
down. In ’Bl I called at a pub in
N.S.W. back country and stayed 3
days to spell the horses. We limited
ourselves to six nips per day and
yet I left hurriedly on the third day be-
cause I could see white, transparent faces
all round me. Was told by the publican’s
neighbor that the last load of spirits—including,
amongst other items, 15 gals, rum —had arrived two
years before. The publican had been selling
“ rum ” all the time, and at the period of my visit
had 60 gallons in stock ! Talk about the widow’s
cruse !—Heimsdal.
* * * •
“Ston”: Re snakes climbing wall
(“ A.S.”, B. 24/11/’00). I have often killed snakes
in a hay-loft—whither they had doubtless gone
after mice—and was puzzled to know how they
got up, till I one day found a snake ascending the
ladder by coiling itself successively round the
rungs and then reaching out for another. How
they got down I can’t say. [Rats ! —Ed. B.]
“ Vril ” : To “ A.S.” (8.*24/11/’00.) There
is a green tree-snake in Queensland that can climb
anything. I have seen one go up a weatherboard
wall, ziz-zag fashion.
“Eller” : Re “ Steele Rudd’s ” “Dad”
and the cow with blind teats. In a recent N.S. W.
South Coast police-court case it was deposed that
when the buyer asked “Is she a good milker? ”
the seller replied, “Oh, she’ll simply astonish
you ! ” And she did —for she had blind teats ;
and the buyer was “ astonished ” some more when
the beak dismissed the case.

“ Bullfrog ” : I was ploughing about two
miles from the house, and saw the storm coming,
so I let two of the horses go and jumped on the
other to get my coat. The loose horses started at
a gallop for home, and the brute I was on went
after ’em just as the hail started. I had on a
button-at-the-back shirt —the wind kept it open
and the hailstones poured in. They were no
bigger than peas, but by the time the horse got
home with me I had Sin. of ice all around inside
my shirt, like a lifebelt. I was doubled up like
Tod Sloan, and the cooky was laughing, but when
I rolled off, tearing at t':e shirt, he thought it was
full of snakes, and had sense enough to fetch the
whisky.

Goombargana station, N.S.W., has this
year cropped 8000 acres of self-sown cereals, the
wheat of which has yielded 10 bushels per acre —
equal to the previous year’s retui’n from a regu-
larly-sown crop.

SYDNEY.


A Lambton correspondent sends a pointer
for the Animals Protection Society, which, in
such a matter, should surely be able to claim the
co-operation of the Govt, coal-mine inspectors :—
Editor Bulletin.—Isn’t it a curious thing that while a
carter depending on his one horse and cart for a living
must not work his horse with a bad shoulder or a sore
knee, wealthy N.S.W. coal-companies can with impunity
work anything that can keep on its feet. At one pit I
know of a horse whose head was so battered that you
could scarcely see the bridle for hard lumps of skin and
flesh overhanging it. The wheelers always placed him a
C f U P e hundred yards to leeward at crib-time because
of the fearful stench from his wounds. Have seen horses,
out after a hard shift, get to the last danger-board where
a gang of men were waiting to be let in, and then have to
go in again with the gang and work “a doubler.”
It is pi yiful the way the poor animals look at their
more fortunate companions on their way out, and try to
lift or kick the barrier down. Even when they have
travelled a couple of miles down, they will try to get out,
in crossing from one travelling road to another, by turn-
ing up the engine-bank. A feed for the poor beasts, on
these double-shifts, is too often a secondary consideration
—considered lucky if they get a drink. Water-bailers’
horses very seldom carry a nosebag. They generally
leave the top at 10.30 at night and get back about 8 in the
morning, and then they often have barely time to get a
feed ere they are taken for boundary-riding or odd jobs
on top. The wheelers often brutally use a horse ; but
then, on the other hand, it amounts to this—a certain
quantity of work must be done —or 14 hours’ notice.
“ Quercus ” re “ tipping ” :
Re “ Diogenes” (B. l/12/’00)and the “ tipping” system,
The impecunious Lord Rossiyn had recently an article on
the subject in an English magazine. It seems to me that
the real offenders are not the recipients of the tips, but
the employers. A regulation prohibiting tipping is posted
in all first-class London clubs, but the rule is, of course,
ignored, notably even by «;he committee which framed it.
As long as hotels and steamers are overcrowded with
waiters and others of the unorganised labor class (who
cannot demand, as a right, any minimum wage) so long
will the public be a party, not to the pauperising of the
employes, but to the assisting of big hotel and steamship
co’s in underpaying and sweating men, who, through
want of organisation, are apparently without remedy.

POLITICAL
POINTS
We have the Commonwealth. Now we
only want a Cromwell !
Mary Jane Gould will not lace Toby Bar-
ton for the Hunter. Gould’s money-bags can’t
materially help him in this case.
Jingoism chuckles. It has already, it
reckons, accomplished three-fourths of the task of
rivetting upon Australia the shackles of an
aggressive militarism.
Chief-Justice Griffith at the Common-
wealth banquet: “The State from which I come
is one of six, yet it is as large as France, Germany
and Austria combined.”
“ I do not like to see too many lawyers in
Parliament. They dip their hands too frequently
into the Treasury. Jessel, the Jew, was the only
exception to this rule that I know.”—Gladstone,
in “ Tollemache’s Talks.”
Queen Victoria cables Lord Hopetoun :
Accept my heartfelt congratulations for the new year,
and for the welfare of my new Australian Commonwealth.
“My Commonwealth ! ” Ha, ha ! Her Gracious
must surely have been thinking of “my”
Dominion !
Queensland has a sort of hybrid old-age
pension scheme already working that gives relief
in some cases, but the authorities salve the Tory
conscience by taking from the applicants any
house or property they have and vesting it in the
Govt. Of course this isn’t Socialism.
Sydney Telegraph now calls Dry Dog
Reid the “low tariff leader.” Considering that
the Dry Dog man’s fiscal arguments consist almost
wholly of statistics that are either straight-out
falsehoods or falsehoods by inference, he does
seem about as low a leader as any party could
have.
LED LIKE A LAMB.
PHRENOLOGIST: “By your bumps, Mr. Casey. I
should say that you -were easily led.”
C ASEY: “ Begorra, Mr. Phrenologist, shure
indade, ye are roight; Oi’ve been married
three toimes.”
Again the irony of politics ! Barton was
sworn-in as Federal Premier within the bound-
aries of the State electorate of Randwick. Rand-
wick, by prefering to elect Bagman Storey and to
reject Edmund Barton, in 1894, gave the first solid
indication that Sydney intended to fight against
the federation of Australia.
Maoriland’s penny post commenced on
Ist January, and there are already strong demands
that the Federal penny post shall follow. But to
carry a letter for a penny 1100 miles from one end
of Maoriland to the other is one thing, and to
carry it for a penny 2400 miles from one end of
Australia to the other isn’t quite the same.
Tasmanian papers swear a good deal at
their island only getting an unpaid Federal
Minister who holds no office. But after all there
are probably 10,000 years ahead, and the present
Ministry is only fixed for four months. When
the Federal Parliament meets, Tasmania has only
to produce a man of sufficiently commanding
ability to get the Premiership, and if it produces
two men of sufficiently commanding ability it can
have the Treasurership as well.
Victoria's Houge of 48 Rich Landlords
threw out the Scripture Lessons Referendum Bill
le'uctantly and by a very small majority. That
Upper House is pious in its own sour and useless
fashion, and it would gladly have passed a
measure for introducing the Bible into State
schools, but the idea of a Referendum to
decide that or any other question was
abhorrent to its narrow soul. Why
doesn’t the Assembly, if it really
wants to get the direct opinion of
the people on Woman Suffrage
or the Bible in schools or
any other subject, simply
take a Referendum on its
own authority, and thus
introduce the principle
whether the Council is
willing or not ? Kingston
did it in S.A. Is Victoria
prepared to admit that it
hasn’t the same courage and
energy as the State nextdoor?
This is a matter on which it
needn’t be kicked by its
dreadful tipper House un-
less it really likes being
kicked.
A well-known N.S.W.
legislator was once address-
mg a meeting which a straw
would have turned either way,
when a big miner arose and began
reading from a volume according to
which the candidate had formerly
expressed views directly opposite
to those enunciated by him that
night. The platform supporters
turned sick with fear, but pre-
sently the candidate rose and de-
manded, “ What’s that you’re reading ? ” “ Han-
sard, ” was the reply. “ Pooh ! ” contemptuously
returned the candidate—“a thing issued by a
Freetrade Government!” Then the crowd whooped
enthusiastically, and the Hansard man had a
bad time while being vigorously passed along to
the door.
Melbourne Leaded reckons that it knows
the kind of Cabinet Reid wanted to construct —
one consisting of George Houstoun Reid, George
H. Reid, G. Houstoun Reid, George Reid,
G. H. Reid, G. Reid, and Reid. Alas ! even that
wouldn’t satisfy him, for it would leave Dry Dog
Reid out in the cold—unless he was made an
honorary Minister.
Sydney D.T. in an attempt to injure
Federal-tariff-formulator Kingston, states that he
“ would never brook a strong man in his Cabinet.”
Yet the only Cabinet Kingston ever formed con-
tained Playford, Holder, Cockburn, Gordon and
Jenkins, who were, when they joined, absolutely
the strongest men in S.A. Parliament, and the
combination lasted six vears.
“ N’lmporte ” on the subject of naming
the Federal capital :
Dear Bulletin. — lie Hori Grey as the introducer of the
clause which subjects revision of the Federal Constitu-
tion to the popular assembly, would it not be best to
name the Federal capital after him? “Greyburgh” or
“ Grey town” looks and sounds well, and we would have
it all to ourselves ; the memory of the “ great pro-consul ”
is surely more worthy of preservation than that of the
purely local “ Wentworth.”
The utter absence of reference in any of
the Commonwealth celebration speeches to the
name of that sound Federalist, ex-Chief Justice
Lilley, of Queensland, suggests something more
than forgetfulness. It may have been prudent to
omit the name of late Premier MTlwraith, but
when speakers were searching history back to
Wentworth, at least Griffith should have thought
of Lillev.
Though the whole postal and telegraph
business of Australia and Tasmania has passed
into Federal control, there is a great silence
among the State Postmasters-General in the
matter of resigning their billets. There is even
some talk of a conference of State Postmasters-
General a month or two hence. Yet, long before
that time, there should be no Postmasters-General
to confer.
Bulletin recommends every democrat in
Australia to get his name on the Federal vote-rolls
at once. Witii manhood suffrage over all the coun-
try, the Federal elections give a splendid chance
to sweep the Commonwealth House clean of mili-
tarism, servility, jobbery, and thievery. The issue
is in the people’s own hands ; and if they pickle
the rod for their own backs—serve ’em right ! So
up with the Gum-Tree Flag, with the motto —
Australia for Australians, and Australians
for Australia !
The question already raised whether
a Federal Minister should get his salary of £4OO
a year as a private Federal member in addi-
tion to his Ministerial salary of about £l7OO a
year. The Bulletin says YES in a tremendous
voice. Good men can’t be got, as a rule, without
paying for them, and even with both salaries com-
bined the Federal Treasurer will get less than
many a bank-manager, without the bank-manager’s
life billet. And a good Treasurer needs to know as
much as three good bank-managers.
“ Freetraders must speak out,” says the
newly-knighted M‘Billion, ex-Treasurer of N.S.W.
“We must now strenuously fight for the principles
of our fiscal creed.” Pity the M‘Billion didn’t
fight a little for his alleged fiscal creed when he
was Freetrade Treasurer of N.S.W. He certainly
took care that there should be no duties on cloth-
ing, and manfully discouraged the local clothing
trade which might interfere with the importing
business. But he protected most other industries
more or less. The M‘Billion would make a good
member of a Protectionist Ministry which be-
lieved in Freetrade in drapery.

The adjustability of Premier Holder’s
political conscience is proved by the fact that after
being ready to join Lyne, and after having hoped
to enter the Barton Cabinet, lie ( exclaimed with
simulated glee that he has now an unfettered
hand to advocate a Freetrade policy—which will
be useful as assisting him to a remunerative
alliance with Reid. Never in State politics has
Holder’s Freetrade been deeper than Holder’s
lips, for all the Ministries with which he has been
associated have been definitely Protective.
Premier Lyne’s translation to the Federal
sphere will leave a vacant portfolio, which rumor
reports will go to F. B. Suttor, Vice-President of
the Executive, now representing the Govt, in the
Leg. Council. No salary is attached to this latter
billet, the custom being for the salaried Ministers
to each make a grant to the Vice-President. With
threatened reduction in State Ministers’ screws
the probability is that this arrangement, never a
commendable one, will be dropped for the future.
The P.M.G’s salary will be available for the
Upper House representative.
Tasmania’s Great Western private-railway
syndicate, which only started, after five years
delay, when it was kicked hard by the Lewis
Government, is pottering along building its line
at the rate of £2OOO a month. At this rate, it will
take probably 48 years to finish the work, and it
only spends even this amount under protest, and
asks twice a week to be allowed to hang up the
job indefinitely while still retaining the conces-
sion. And still Hobart clings to the myth that
handing over the work to a private syndicate with
a big land-grant is the only way to get speedy
communication with the West Coast.
“ Fairplay ” to The Bulletin :
Sydney D.T. printed a par. t’other day with a flaring
headline “ Bombala surrounded by flames” —this no
doubt to make it appear that Bombala is an unsafe place
for the Federal capital. I was at Bombala a few days
after the alleged conflagration, yet not even the vestige
of a fire was perceptible within two miles of the town.
What fire there had been was confined to a fifty-acre area.
Can it be that the par. was printed with the object of
prejudicing the superior claims of the locality to the
Federal capital? Apropos, a Govt, engineer was sent up
to Orange a few weeks ago to report on the local water-
supply, but his report has not yet seen light. When it
does, it should make interesting reading when placed
side by side with the report furnished by the League’s
paid official.
J. H. Symon, Q.C., declared at a recent
Adelaide meeting that D. H. Deniehy was the
real father of Australian Federation, and anyone
who cares to delve in far-back political literature
will at once see that the assertion is maintainable.
But of course Deniehy only sounded “the Aus-
tralian note,” and the Australian note is not popu-
lar just now. Hence Deniehy has been forgotten,
and the name of Dailey given to a N.S.W.
Federal constituency on the ground, as set forth
by an Irish M.P. of N.S.W. Assembly, that he
was “the first Australian statesman to strike the
Imperial note ” and the first to be “ summoned to
the councils of his Queen.”
That New Year’s distribution of food to
6000 “ needy families ” —apparently needy
bachelors and spinsters “on their own ” were not
reckoned, and the relief was confined to families —
in Sydney and suburbs is still awaiting explana-
tion. If a family is reckoned at five persons, that
distribution means that one-twelfth of the whole
population of Sydney and its environs is in want
or on the verge of it. A truly awful state of
things is herein foreshadowed, and if four years
of “ Freetrade ” has brought this to pass, what a
grim joke it is at the expense of the Jolly and
Fat policy ! The 6000 Jolly Skeletons —it sounds
like the name of a public-house, by the way.
A belated hunk from George Reid’s last
great Freetrade speech :
While, during the five years preceding 1899 the number
of male operatives in Victoria had decreased, showing that
“ the tree was rotten,” the number had increased in
similar establishments in N.S.W., &c., &c.,
Possibly this is another of the facts which the
guileless and phonographic George took on trust
from Max Hirsch. According to the published
information on page 47 of the “Abstract of the
Statistics of Victoria, 1899,” the number of male
employes in the protected State increased in the
five years previous to 1899 from 35,467 to 44,041.
It seems to be George Reid that is rotten —not the
tree.
Last Friday the first Australian Federal
Ministry was only four days old, and the three
Sydney Calico Jimmy dailies were all demanding
to know its fiscal policy, and threatening to sool
the dog on it if the information wasn’t instantly
forthcoming. Not one of them inquired about its
borrowing or non-borrowing policy, its Old Age
Pension policy, its policy for preserving the
Federal territory from the grasp of syndicates and
land-grabbers, its public debt conversion policy,
its railway policy, its land-tax policy, or anything
else. The Freetrade dailies of Sydney wear a
very thin veil of democratic sentiment, and the
raw bones of their Calico-Jimmyism stick through
it in all directions.
In passing its Federal Senators' Elections
Bill t’other day, Vic. Assembly fixed the hours of
polling from 8 a.m. to 7 p.m. all over the State in
place of Btos, as for the State elections. Where-
upon the Argus arose in great haste to protest
against such an' entirely unnecessary innovation,
and pleaded tearfully for sundry hapless returning
officers, who “might have to find their way
along country roads in the dark ” (!) The Argus
poses as the friend of the country as against the
city, but the heavy journal is, of course, unaware
that the country worker is nearly always engaged
between 8 a.m. and 5 or 6 p.m., and that the
extra hour or two gives him just the desired op-
portunity to record his vote.
Attorney-General Isaac Isaacs claims the
Victorian State Premiership as Turner’s successor.
The stock of Kookaburra Peacock has long been
steadily rising, but Isaacs has always ranked as
second man in the Cabinet, and his inheritance
appears sure. Isaacs has been frequently described
as furnishing the brains and the strong democratic
instincts of Vic. Cabinet. The Kookaburra man’s
friends put in the same claim for him, and the
Kookaburra man’s enemies say that be is an able
and wily schemer who has no settled instincts of
any kind, and that his fearful laugh is an acquired
habit carefully worked up for purposes of advt.
Probably, however, Turner supplied his own
brains and his own democratic ideas, and was
dependent on neither man. People who want to
appear well-informed have a habit of looking for
a power behind the throne even when there isn’t
any.
The Commonwealth Ministry of seven con-
tains three laymen (Lyne, Forrest and Dickson)
and four lawyers (Barton, Turner, Deakin and
Kingston). The great William Pitt Cabinets of
England never numbered more than seven.
Modern British Cabinets sum up to nearly a score,
a matter Constitutionalists regard as a source of
weakness. Honorary members of a Cabinet are a
dangerous, when not useless, excrescence. Parlia-
mentary sanction should be insisted on before
a new Minister, honorary or otherwise, is
attached to a Cabinet. In Australia, in Victoria
particularly, the practice of making a
man a Minister without portfolio has often been
badly abused. In England, during later years,
the practice has amounted to a form of bribery.
The inclusion of R. E. O’Connor and Lewis will
over-strengthen the lawyer element in the first
Federal Ministry.

It seems strange that the highly-trained
Mid splendid horses at the George-street, Sydney,
hire. Station could not have been saved when the
nags and greenstuff of the Commonwealth decora-
tions caught fire on procession day. Had the
alarm come from the furthermost part of the city
the poor animals would have been galloping down
the street in a few seconds, but with the fire at the
. static® itself they were helpless.
The Benevolent Societies’ Fete—one of the
features of the approaching Commonwealth festivities—
should command the ready support of all. The object is
good, the price of tickets, Is., is reasonable, and the enter-
tainments to be provided- three concerts in Sydney Town
Hall and a monster sports meeting at the Agricultural
grounds—will appeal to all classes. Further particulars
from Mr. W. Moffitt Burns, hon. sec., 35 Oastlereagh-st.*
