Touching natural remedies that flow from
rocks and are used for both drinking and bathing
purposes. The Melbourne agents for Hepburn
Mineral Water lately obtained a careful analysis
of its virtues fiom Mr. A. de Bavay, who found
• carbonates of lime, magnesium and soda in solu-
tion to the amount of about 30 grains of each per
gallon. Also grs. 2.24 of carb. of iron, not to men-
tion sulphatesand things thathaveassistedto perpe-
tuate the youth of J. S. Butters. The best testi-
monial the Hepburn water has ever received was
Butters allegation that lie was laid on to it long
long ago by his old friend, Dr. Diamond Beaney’
and believes it to be the main secret of the splen-
did health he enjoyed last century. But to resume.
Analyst de Bavay said something which should
interest many who go down to the Hepburn
Springs armed with bottles, cans, and other
utensils wherein they carry away doses of the
famous water for home consumption. “In the
event,” says Mr. de 8., “of an escape of the
natural carbonic acid gas with which this water is
charged, the salts of lime, iron, &c., become pre-
cipitated. Th acid gas makes the
chemical properties soluble, and should the acid
disappear, or become materially diminished from
any cause whatever, the qualities of this fine
water will completely disappear.” In short, the
crank who, after drinking as much as he can carry
m his stomach, fills a whisky-bottle with mineral
water every night, until at last he has colored the
bottle like a meerschaum pipe, gets no more tonic
from the contents than he could get by swilling a
matutinal quart of ordinary water from the bed-
room jug. It is the bubbles do the trick. The 80
or 90 years that Butters has (thusfar) added to his
mvaluable life are due to his having bought his
.Hepburn elixir by the 5-gallon cylinder, from the
Melbourne Agency. These cylinders are charged
with an extra quantity of natural gas at the
Mineral Spring. Taken fresh from the Spring in
a pannikin the temperance liquor is gassy enough,
of course, but after a short stay in a hand-corked
bottle or can it becomes flat, stale, and foolish.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
23 of this issue.*
Premier Seddon has appointed a Com-
mission to collect evidence as to the desirability
of Maoriland entering the Commonwealth. As
the Commission, however, consists mostly of anti-
Federalists, it looks rather as if it was appointed
to fix up a foregone conclusion, and to find
excuses for Seddon’s separatist policy.
The bell-ringers visiting Sydney for the
Commonwealth revels have been picnicked by the
local janglers, with the result that N.S.W. is now
menaced with a “Campanovian Association.”
On second thoughts, however, The Bulletin is
inclined to approve of the step. It will pro-
bably some day be found necessary to extermi-
nate the whole fraternity of bell-bashers, and an
opportunity may thus be afforded of slaughtering
the lot at one “ go.”
On New Year’s eve, Newcastle (N.S.W.)
had a big fire, which raged for 10 hours, and
finally went out “on its own,” after destroying a
big bulk store with contents worth about £70,000.
Now, Newcastle and suburbs have 17 volunteer
fire-brigades, with 300 members, but up to an
hour after the fire was first seen, not over 20
“ firemen ” were on hand, and not for another
half-hour did Newcastle’s only steam fire-engine
come on the scene—taking yet a further half-hour
to get steam up. It is explained that about 250
of the firemen were away “attending fire-brigade
competitions.” Apparently the volunteer fireman
exists more for the sake of such competitions and
the showing-off of his much-polished trappings
than for the putting out of fires.

Dear Sir,—For Twelve Years I was a sufferer of
Bleeding Polypus in the Womb, and was treated by the
best medical skill in Ballarat and Melbourne without
avail. I have been in Three Hospitals, but derived no
permanent benefit from the treatment I received.
I was advised to try Yitadatio, and after taking a
course of the Medicine lam completely cured. The
Medical Men who attended me expressed their astonish-
ment at my recovery, and stated if they had not attended
me they WOULD NOT have believed I had suffered as
stated. I write this for the benefit of similar sufferers,
and you are at liberty to publish and make what use vou
like of it.—Yours truly,
(Signed) M. BECKMAN.

Some Samoan postage-stamp dies were
destroyed lately at Wellington (M.L.) by Mr. J.
Davis, until recently postmaster on that blood-
red island. Several stamps were issued during
Mr. Davis’s term, including the “Black Error” —
so called because Malietoa’s noble phiz was
printed in ebony. This stamp was withdrawn, it
being deemed a reflection on his Majesty's colour.
Bishop Webber wrote to London Times
lately, saying that he needed £13,000 for the Bris-
bane Anglican Cathedral. His friends advise him
to go to the old country and try to raise the
money himself. The Bishop doesn’t like the idea,
but says that if the money is not raised in three
weeks he will actually depart on a collecting mis-
sion. Does the worthy cleric fancy that the
British public will actually raise the money to
prevent his coming ? Surely modesty couldn’t go
further.
Some sayings of Oscai; Wilde, who died
the other day in Paris
Education is an a l.nirable thing. But it is well to re-
member from time to time that nothing that is worth
knowing can be Taught.
To be really mediaeval one should have no body. To be
really modern one should have no soul. To be really
Greek one should have no clothes.
The English are always degrading truths into facts.
When a Truth becomes a Fact, it loses all its intellectual
value
The criminal classes are so close to us that even the
policeman can see them. They are so far away from us
that only the poet can understand them.
Art is the only serious thing in the world. And the
artist is the only person who is never serious.
The only link between literature and the drama left to
us in England is the bill of the play. [No wonder his
fellow-dramatists loved him.]
The only thing that the artist cannot see is the obvious.
The only thing that the public can see is the obvious.
The result is the Criticism of the Journalist. [No wonder
the critics loved him.]
The 19th century dislike of Realism is the rage of Cali-
ban seeing his own face in a glass. The 19th century dis-
like of Romanticism is the rage of Caliban not seeing his
own face in a glass. [No wonder the puhlic loved him.]
There is no such thing as a mor d or an immoral book.
Books are well written or badly written. is all.

Sydney Commonwealth celebrations might
very well be summed up as half-a-million harbor
souls and a million barber’s poles.
Lord Tennyson, at the Adelaide celebra-
tion on Jan. 1, strongly advised the people of S. A.
to preserve their State rights, to fight hard to
maintain their individuality, and to keep up the
status of their Governors. To help them in these
laudable efforts, he said, he had refrained from
attending the big demonstration in Sydney. All
his Cabinet Ministers but two, most of the legis-
lators, and shoals of other prominent public men,
however, temporarily emigrated to Port Jackson.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Opening of S.A.-N.9.W. inter-State cricket
match supplied a genuine surprise—l6,ooo people (less
the usual percentage of deadheads) paid £550 at the
gates. Fact of the Wheat State, a few weeks ago, beating
N.S.W. by an innings and 35 runs suggested that, at
least, present struggle would be close, but it was very
one-sided. The wicket left nothing wanting, yet S.A.
went in first and were all out for 157, while the home side
had only one down for 224 when stumps were drawn.
“ Noss ” Iredale (not out) had 107 in front of his name.
Aboriginal Marsh downed five wickets for 34 runs.
Saturday’s feat makes the twelfth century notched by
Iredale in first-class cricket —four of them were hit up on
the Sydney ground. Last time Iredale scored three
figures was when he compiled 111 at Lords in ’99. His
total against S.A., up to Saturday, was 1231—the highest
made by any player.
* * • *
Monday’s play in S.A.-N.S.W.
was remarkable. N.S.W. bats reached best form, and
pasted weak bowling unmercifully. Day’s score 499, and
when stumps were drawn the board showed 6 wickets for
723. This reads like a record. Four individual centuries
(Iredale 118, Noble 153, Gregory 168, and Duff 119) in one
innings parallels the achievement of Yorkshire against
Warwickshire five years ago, when Lord Hawke, Jackson,
Peel, and Wainwright each placed three figures in front
of his name. On that occasion Yorkshire’s total was 887
—the best performance in a first-class match up to time
of writing. N.S.W. (opposed to S.A.) last year at Adel-
aide notched the highest score (807) in inter-State cricket;
then Gregory, Trumpet - , and Noble hit up hundreds. Pre-
vious best was N.S.W.’s 775 against Vic. at Sydney in 'B2.
Murdoch, Jones, and Garrett totted up the centuries, and
the first-named’s 321 remained a record till Olem Hill
wiped it out t’other day in Adelaide.
* » * *
In the end, N.S.W. men were all out for 918,
the record for an inter-State match. Poidevin scored 140
(not out).
* * * »
Remarkable how successful left-hand bowlers
have been this season. Victoria’s triumphs have to a
great extent been brought about by the effectiveness of
Saunders. Then there’s Travers (of Adelaide) who has
done a lot of good for his side. M'Beth’s performances,
too, call for special mention. Clem Hill is a left-handed
bat; so is Stuckey, who had such a big say in Victoria’s
recent triumphs over N.S.W.
Victoria’s three substantial cricket victories
have much lessened interest in the premiership, but the
return match with N.S.W. should provide a draw if the
selection committee is wise in its generation. To get
two drubbings from Vic. in one season is not quite a
novelty', but it is most unpleasant. A judicious selection
on form and not reputation may save the mother State.
* * * ♦
Fred Hack, the long stonewaller of S.A.
cricket-team, has left his Adelaide billet, and hopes to
settle in Melbourne or Sydney as a coachbuilder.
Although not in great form this year, Hack was top of
last season’s inter-State batting averages.
• » • •
C. T. B. Turner recently captained a team from
Gympie (Queensland) against a' combination from Bris-
bane.
• * • *
Commonwealth athletic sports crieket-ball-
throwing competition at Sydne.v Cricket Ground (Friday')
produced these performances : Vic. Trumper 120.yds. lft
6in., and aboriginal Marsh 92yds. Unusual conditions
prevailed. Throw was measured from a mark which the
competitors couldn’t step ever either in delivering or
following up. This makes rumper’s feat particularly
good. Last Feb. he did 124.yds lOin. C. H. Gordon, on
the old Albert Ground in Elizabeth-street, Redfern, cast
the ball 122yds, while “ demon ” bowler Spofforth’s best
was 120yds. The world’s authenticated record is 140yds,
(really 142Jyds) accomplished at Clermont (Q.) by an
aboriginal (“ Billy ”) nearly 29 years ago.
* * * *
Mo. was very much got at over Khaki, winner
of Sydney Tatt.’s Cup t’other da.v. He couldn’t be drawn
the night before at the card-reading, and remained dumb
as an oyster while the list was being gone through until
the nag named was reached ; then he took a chance and
launched out, with the result that the waiting stable
grabbed all offers available at a reasonable price. One
extra-careful firm dropped something over a thousand.
Khaki is the second foal of Godlike, recently sent to Eng-
land. He was thrice unplaced at 2 yrs. Backed for a
mile welter at Moorefield recently he cut up badly, but a
week later nearly sprang a surprise in Warwick Farm
Handicap. His showing in Boxing Day’s Summer Cup
was good, but few noticed it. However, he got home
when very specially wanted, and that’s the main thing.
* » • ♦
Fleet Admiral was the hero of the New Year’s
Day - meeting at Flemington. With 8.10 to carry, the
old dandy won the Bagot Handicap in attractive
style. The opposition didn’t look very - strong on paper,
but all the same the performance went to show that
Fleet, with a fair run, should have nearly captured last
Caulfield Cup, in which he had the luxurious weight
of 7.7.
* • * »
Aquarius, the V.R.C. National winner, scored
another bracket at Flemington on the Ist. This was one
of the occasions when Aquarius wasn’t “ expected,” and
he went out at 12 to 1 in a small field. The result set
people thinking of the nag’s performance in the 3-mile
Hurdle Race on Cup Day (when he was the hottest of
street-corner tips) and again on Oaks Day. There were
interesting whispers in the air re the doings of Aquarius
at Melb. Cup meeting.
* • » •
Second trip of Bananaland crack nag Ohio to
Sydney promises as little remuneration as on the previous
occasion, and emphasises the generally accepted fact that
Q. horse-racing form is comparatively poor. Piles of
coin were heaped on him at Randwick, but he didn’t even
shape well, and his display in N.S.W. Handicap at Rose-
hill, Saturday, showed no improvement.
« * * *
Etneiuraf (Farniente backwards) is the latest
infliction in N.S.W. turf nomenclature.
* * * *
Captain Scott, the glorified veterinary surgeon
who married the rich and very mature widow of the late
Hon. James White, of N.S.W., and who succeeded to the
lady’s handsome fortune when she died, soon after the
marriage, has, with his trainer, been “ warned off ” the
Irish turf because of the crooked running of his horse,
Raven’s Plume. It is enough to make old James White
turn in his grave.
• * * •
N.S.W. crack nags, Parthian and The Tola,
were among starters in w.f.a. Final Plate at recent Man-
chester (Eng.) meeting, humping 9.5 and 9.6 respectively.
Spectrum won in a canter, with Parthian second and Tola
last; 6to 4 was winner’s price, and 100 to 8 could have
been had about Parthian. London Sportsman expects to
see Parthian win a good race this year, but says ’tis Mr.
Tom Muir’s intention to have him trained for w.f.a.
events only, ignoring handicaps. Apropos, T.M. has
written that he came at Parthian in the Queen’s Plate
(2m.) at Derby to the extent of £5OO because the crack
nag met a “ rather weak field.” Australians who know
Mr. Muir will have some difficulty in assimilating this
tale, especially when ’tis stated that he explains the nag
was only 7 “ half fit.”
* • » *
The “sport ” at some of the oldest of the English
racecourses is very cheap. The Nottingham Steeplechase
meeting, for instance, is a two-days affair, and yet the
total amount of stake-money offered on the second day
was only £550 for six races, including a principal event
of £250.
A newsclip: A gamble when under Govt,
supervision, is alleged to be inviolable, and is always
supposed to be one of the most valuable guarantees of
“ Tattersall." Yet extraordinary happenings will some-
times occur, even more mysterious than those of the
famous V.D.L. Bank lottery, which left, or rather
sent back, by a chain of coincidences, certain valu-
able Hobart properties into the hands of the original
owners. Thus the other day the Maoriland Govt, balloted
3 7,325 acres of second-class land in the King Country
The marbles were drawn by a Mr. W. W. Hardy , and five
of the Hardy family secured selections amounting to oner
3815 acres , comprising one of the choicest bits of the blocks.
Another family named Dunning was equally favored by
“ blind chance,” securing a complete estate with adjoining
boundaries of 2263 acres. Of course, the result is that
some people are dissatisfied, and want Seddon, whose
desire to secure honest land settlement is unquestioned,
to investigate these remarkable coincidences.
* * » •
Feature of Saturday night’s cycle racing at
Sydney Cricket Ground was Bilmartin’s brilliant sus-
tainment of his marvellous mana. He got away
with the two scratch races after desperate
struggles with Lawson, Forbes, Beauchamp, Lewis and
Morgan. Final of Sydney Plate (cut out in 2.71-5)
brought Martin, Morgan, Lawson and Forbes to the
mark. American Vanghan paced. First-named three
seemed to cross the line simultaneously, but they were
placed as given. Later, Plugger Bill annexed Sydney'
Plate (second distance—ten miles), beating Beauchamp
by half a wheel, with Lawson close up, in 22.46. Martin’s
sustained spurt in the last round was an eye-opener, the
packed crowd cheering him continuously.
* * • •
Tas. rider Beauchamp built up a big reputation
by downing Plugger Bill in a heat of the mile supremacy
and the final distance (five miles) of Sydney Plate. Each
time the bulky “ Murkan ” was fairly overwhelmed by
the Tas. top-notcher’s brilliant burst of pace at the finish.
Both struggles were hot from the jump.
v * • *
Mile cycling championship of Australia at
, Sydney Cricket Ground, Monday night, produced heaps of
excitement. Recently arrived U.S. front-ranker Lawson
got the third heat by a wheel from Morgan, and then won
the final—a length ahead of Beauchamp, with still
another Yankee (Chapman) third and the Sydneyidol Joe
Megson fourth. Time recorded, 2.16 2-5.
♦ * » *
Yankee crack sprinter, Ivor Lawson, now in
Sydney, shows great improvement with each fresh
appearance, and, atop of big things already done, is ex-
pected to do considerably better on Wednesday and
Saturday' (this week), and Monday next. Thursday (last
week), in his heat of the Mile Scratch race, he appeared
surely beaten half a lap from home, and everyone thought
the finish a “ moral ” for Lewis, but Lawson came as if
shot from a catapult, and got over the line half a wheel
in front of Lewis and Walne. Lawson had a big reputa-
tion in America, and looks like living up to it here. He
sits his machine well, and pedals with clockwork accuracy.
May not be generally known that, at Ballarat, on Boxing
Day, Lawson secured the Five Miles Scratch (£35) and
the Half-Mile Handicap—Martin finishing behind him in
each.
* * * •
Lyne wheel race (run over two miles and worth
£200) was the big thing of Sydney night-cycling carnival,
concluded Monday. The pick of riders in Australia
lined up. Bilmartin started favorite for the final —t’other
Yankee (Lawson) being next —and won by half a length
with a spurt that fairly crazed the spectators, who
cheered madly. Morgan (second in recent “ Austral ”)
emphasised the general opinion that he comes closest to
the great plugger by finishing on Martin’s heels. Beau-
champ (Tas.) was third, and Fay fourth. Time (4.10) was
very fast.
* * * *
Time (2min., dead) of S. Australian cyclist
Champness, in final of the “ Martin ” Plate at Sydney
Cricket Ground (Saturday) is the speediest ever achieved
in a mile competition, and brings him into line with the
best we have.
* * * *
“Trap”: At N.S.W. Gun Club big shoot,
Friday last week, no less than 72 noms. (including 8
“stars”) showed on the board. When these were
reduced to 12, president Faithfull vented a suggestion
that the lot left in should take a fiver each out of the
£lOO offered, but two or three weren’t on. Next round
saw five go out, and t’other seven closed on a tenner
each, and continued until only four stayed; these
whacked the remaining £3O. Writer can’t understand
sportsmen doing this sort of thing. Why not go for the
lot or none? That’s the practice in every other game.
Why is it different at pigeon-shooting ? Have often seen
swell shots take £2 each, then £l, and so on till they’ve
distributed £5O or £lOO among a dozen or fifteen. If
competitors (professedly sportsmen) won’t act up to the
character they assume, clubs should legislate to compel
them, especially as there are some who’d rather shoot for
the lot, but will not stand out against the cutting-up
proposals for fear of getting disliked.
* * -K *
Struggle between Australian top-notch swim-
mers, Dick Cavill and Fred Lane, in the 200yds. Common-
wealth supremacy at Cockatoo Island (Sydney), Wednesday
last week, was a treat. Not at any time right through did
more than a yard separate them. Lane finished first, with
barely the length of his arm to the good, in 2.22 —just
2sec. slower than the world’s best (2.20) held jointly by
Tyers (amateur) and Nuttall (prof.).
* * * *
Crack swimmer Fred. Lane has given out that
he’ll swim in races no more ; his constitution won’t stand
it. Writer expected to hear this ever since Fred came
back from England, as reports previously reached Sydney
that on at least two or three occasions he fainted after
extra-tough struggles, and, altogether, seemed to be in-
juriously affected by the constant strain.
• * *. *
Boy Dick Cavill’s win of the 1000yds. Common-
wealth Championship at Cockatoo Island last week in
14.31 l-sth showed beyond question that he is unapproach-
able from half-a-mile up, though Reid and one or two
others might finish very near him over any less distance.
The 1000yds. is rarely swum now, but it was much
favored of old. Once at Robinson’s Baths (Domain),
“ Bill ” Corbett, the champion of his time at all distances,
put up 14.34 for 1000yds., turning every 50, without a
“ kick-off.” Conditions of last Wednesday’s event were
much easier, as the turn was only necessary at the end of
every 100yds.
* * * *
Most promising youngster in the swimming
game just now is 15yr.-old Claude Corbett, who’ll be top
of the heap in another year, if he doesn’t get there before
this season ends. At Cockatoo Island Commonwealth
swimming display last week he won the two big events
open to all Australasian Schools. The Half-Mile was first,
and this he paddled over in 13inin. 32 2-sth sec. ; then the
100yds. fell to him in I.9—only 2-sth sec. slower than
Lane’s time for same distance on same day and at same
place.
* * * *
A challenge from Englishman Barry and Aus-
tralian Jim Wray to row any two in the world for £2OO
a-side created lots of talk and ink-slinging in
England early last month. Most people thought it was
directed at M.L. Tom Sullivan and Australian George
Towns. When asked about the match, it being sug-
gested that he and his chum Towns were probably meant,
Sullivan said, emphatically, “In no possible way shall I
identify myself personally with anything with which
Wray is connected.” Australian scullers at ’Ome are evi-
dently not a happy family.
* * • *
Low as the standard of pug. ability is in
N.S.W. just now ’twould seem to be worse in Melbourne—
judged by the manner in w’hich bruisers from the latter
State have been shaping in Sydney of late. Thursday
(last week) at Gaiety A. 0. “ Cocker ” Tweedie and Cullen
(Melb.) supplied a farce that filled every onlooker to
t/he brim. Cullen couldn’t or wouldn’t fight, and Tweedie
was little better. Former seemed to want to get away
from it, but “Cocker” wasn’t able to send in one hard
enough to provide an excuse for being counted out.
Often Cullen went down determined to stay, but the
crowd wouldn't let him. “ Gerrup ! ” they yelled, and
he did, but not too willingly. The thing ended after the
fifth round when the “ wipe ” was skied in the Melb.
man’s corner. Saturday night, at Golden Gate A.C., some-
thing similar was provided ; Sydney waterman Johns met
“ young Corbett ” (Melb.), and had it all his own way
from start to end, sending Corbett out in the fifth 3min.
* * * *
Referee Wally Weekes, after the second round
of the Tweedie-Ned Cullen fight at the Gaiety A.C, (Syd-
ney) last week : “Gentlemen, I know both of them can
do better 1 ” Two rounds later : “ Gentlemen, it’s a
genuine fight; I know Tweedie is doing his best, but I
don’t know the other gentleman.”
“Jab”: See Australian Jim Richardsonis atwork
in England again, and wants another match with Ohrisp,
who beat him twice before. Richardson hasn’t been heard
of for a long time, and, as he is ready to back himself well,
the inference is that his chance is thought good. Writer
remembers Richardson fighting the goriest fight imagin-
able with “ Pheasant ” at the old Darlinghurst skating
rink eight or 10 years ago, when things were so rough
that they carried the ropes and posts of the ring with
them, and fell down 3ft. among the people. Richardson
won, but got a severe drubbing. He has been in England
some years, and, though a game and fairly clever fighter,
seems to do little good—more through bad luck than
aught else.
* * • #
Tom Burrows, whilom Australian crack wrestler
and axe-swinger, and ex-holder of the world’s endurance
club-swinging record, is in S’Africa with the British
troops. Recently Corporal Day, of the 16th Lancers (3st.
heavier and 3in. taller than Burrows), was backed to fight
Tom and stop him in 8 rounds. Burrows came out atop.
* * * *
Yankee mailboat Sierra brings news that
world’s champion bruiser, Jim Jeffries, has got down to
business ; he is matched to meet Akron giant Ruhlin in
May, and Sailor Sharkey 30 days later. Prospects are
that Fitzsimmons wdll take on the huge boilermaker if he
gets through with the two contracts now in hand, and
they look fairly sure things for him ; but he’ll know he’s
been fighting when they are done with.
* * * *
U.S. scribes tell Clioynski’s story of how he
saved M.L. Bob Fitzsimmons from Deing licked by the
erstwhile Dublin brewer’s carter, Peter Maher, In the
initial round Maher had Fitz. on the ropes dead to the
world when the gong clanged and saved him. There
were still 30sec. to go. The timekeeper had his hand on
the bell-rope as freckled-faced Bob got the punch on the
point. Choynski struck the timekeeper’s arm, the bell
sounded, and he rushed in and carried Bob to his corner
limp as a rag. After that Fitz. was more careful and
didn’t give the Irishman another chance. Choynski
claims that he should have had a big slice of the 10,00dol.
purse Fitz. won, as but for the bell dodge ’twas any odds
on Maher getting the money.
* * * *
Mile Australasian Championship, held on Syd-
ney Cricket Ground last night, was won by Lawson on
Dunlop tyres.
The arrangement appears to be that Lyne
will hand over *he N.S. W. Premiership to See,
and that in return See will help Lyne into the
representation of Cowper (Grafton) electorate in
the House of Representatives.
Up to now, no Australian politician has
publicly flaunted the Windsor uniform. When he
had it on, he darted into a cab and pulled the
blinds down till he arrived at his destination,
which was always Govt. House, to meet others of
his own kind. Now, the brass-bound Imperial
agents are seen in hotel vestibules, swaggering
round with cocked hats and swords. Seddon, for
instance—and Wise.
The latest sensation comes from Mathinna,
Tasmania, where James Whyte, a married State-
school teacher with a family, shot his brother-in-
law, Sydney Jackson, and the latter’s sister, Miss
Rhoda Jackson, and then committed suicide. All
the parties were highly esteemed locally. The
jury found a verdict of murder and suicide
while of unsound mind ; but had Whyte
merely wounded himself they would doubtless
have (as in a hundred similar cases) verdicted
wilful murder, and a Supreme Court jury would
have convicted Whyte, who would, doubtless,
have been hanged. So much for the logic of the
law ! The root cause of the trouble seems to have
been that Whyte _ loved Rhoda Jackson, and
feared she was going to marry another man.
True Love again—real, red-hot, sizzling, savage,
uncivilised love !
Cardinal Moran barks up the wrong tree
in claiming precedence at public functions over all
other local clerics. His contention that his claim
was allowed in Lord Jersey’s time doesn’t hold
water—what was then arranged was that, at Govt.
House dinner-table, he, as the senior in office,
should go before the Primate. Since then, if the
two happen to be present, the Cardinal says grace
before, and the Primate says grace after the meal;
but it was distinctly laid down by Lord Jersey
that the old rule as to precedence on public
occasions of the C. of E. clergy was to remain.
Independent people can only sympathise with the
Cardinal to the extent of objecting to the Angli-
can Church having precedence as a Church, for
there is, thank Heaven, no State Church in Aus-
tralia. But the Cardinal does not merely protest
against the Anglican Primate having precedence;
he (the Cardinal) insists on having precedence
himself. Evidently the only way out of the trouble
is to ignore all the Churches, leaving them to
attend public functions or not, just as they please;
for there is no reason why the parsons should get
any more official notice than the members of the
plasterers’ union.
Harwood, M.L.C. (Victoria), on the
Woman’s Suffrage Bill, just rejected by the
House of Landlords for the sixth time :
Until the Council is thoroughly satisfied that the
Assembly is absolutely hacked up by the community, the
fact that the Assembly continually sends this Bill up to it
should have no weight whatever with members.
The people have elected three successive Assem-
blies containing a majority of Woman Suffragists
—the present one contains nearly a three-fourths
majority—and still the Council isn’t satisfied.
The Assembly has offered to let the matter be
decided by a direct vote of the people, and the
Council isn’t satisfied with that. Will Harwood
explain exactly what evidence will satisfy the
Council ?
The fact that Lyue and Turner have ac-
cepted Federal office without immediately resign-
ing their State Premierships complicates things,
and Sydney Telegraph thus figures out the situa-
tion ,
Mr. Lyne, acting strictly in his capacity of State
Premier, has agreed to allow the Ministers some rooms at
the Treasury and in the Colonial Secretary’s building.
As Minister for Internal Affairs, he accepts the quarters
that Mr. Barton provides for him. As State Premier, he
allows Mr. Barton all the accommodation that he needs.
If Mr. Barton should not allow him sufficient room, he
will get Mr. Barton to apply to himself as State Premier
for more accommodaf ion.
Mr. Lyne will transact the business of the Home
Department at the State Premier’s Office. It is improbable
that, in the course of his business as Minister for
Home Affairs, he will have to communicate with himself
as State Premier. That lie will do through Mr. Barton as
Prime Minister. But if it should be necessary, as Minister
for Internal Affairs, to send himself a message as State
Premier, he will get Mr. Barton, as Prime Minister, to
conduct the negotiations. He will not be able to com-
municate with himself by telephone. He must first tele-
phone to Mr. Barton what he desires to ask himself.
Then Mr. Barton will ring him up and inform him
what it is that he wishes himself to be asked. Should
Mr. Lyne, as Federal Minister, desire to remon-
strate with himself, as State Premier, he must secure
the assistance of Mr. Barton. He cannot accuse
himself of any wrong. He cannot suggest to himself that
he should take action in regard to any matter that might
relate to his Federal Department. But as a Federal
Minister he can request his chief to send himself a strong
remonstrance at his failure to perform the act that he
ought to have done as State Premier.
With Mr. George Turner the complication will be still
more interesting. As Federal Treasurer he may have im-
portant relations with himself as State Premier and
Treasurer. He, too, will probably not communicate
with himself direct. But if there should be a difference
of opinion between himself as the Federal Treasurer and
himself as State Premier and Treasurer, he will have
to open an extensive correspondence with himself through
the medium also of Mr. Barton. As State Treasurer he
may find that the payment of the Customs surplus has
failed to have regard to an item that is essential to his
accounts. He will immediately as State Treasurer ring
himself violently up as State Premier requesting himself
to communicate with Mr. Barton as Prime Minister,
urging Mr. Barton to demand of himself as Federal
Treasurer why this apparent discrepancy has been allowed
to occur.
If the response that he makes to his Federal self
through Mr. Barton is not satisfactory to his State self,
he may request himself as State Premier to invoke the aid
of the High Court to issue a mandamus against his
Federal self, or some other form of legal process, compel-
ling his Federal self to act squarely by his State self, and
make gcod to himself the amount that he has not allowed
himself. Altogether the prospects before Mr. Turner
are infinitely more touching than they are before
Mr. Lyne. The only danger in regard to Mr.
Lyne is that while the relations between himself
as State and Federal Minister may be acute he may as
State Premier some day meet himself face to face as
Federal Minister. Then, being a man sometimes of quick
temper, it is impossible to predict the horrible conse-
quences that may result.

Standard Exploration Co. : Cabled last week
that this co. had been unable to meet its obligations be-
cause of the Lake View collapse. A little over two years
since, the Westralian “ cat ” mines floated by Whitaker
Wright, in conjunction with Kaufmann’s and others, pre-
sented such a horrible spectacle in the presence of the
Ivanhoe and Lake View mines that Wright conceived the
brilliant idea of separating the sheep from the goats, and
decided to put all the cats in a bag and allow them to
slowly drown themselves under the name of the Standard
Exploration Co., which has the modest capital of a million
and a-half. They consisted of the Austin Friars Syndicate,
Golden Crown. Hannan’s Golden Group, Hannan’s Golden
Treasure, Mainland Consols, Paddington Consols, Thames
Hauraki, the Wealth of Nations (or “ Expectations ”), and
the Wealth of Nations Extended. When the shareholders
of these practically defunct co’s were called together,
Wright told them that if the London and Globe had not
promoted the amalgamation they would have had much
trouble from want of capital, but the bull having been
taken by the horns “ as a matter of pure philanthropy ,”
they would, in all probability, in the near future recover
what they had lost in the past. The Marquis of Dufferin
remarked at same meeting that the mines controlled by the
Standard co. were amongst the best in Westralia outside the
famous Kalgoorlie properties. In addition to taking over
nine mines, not one of which had paid a div. and all of
which were bankrupt, it had an option on something
situated on an island in the Pacific and shares in some
Westralian co’s, the names, price, and number of which
were not stated. Shares soon went to a premium, soon
afterwards to a discount, and they have stayed there ever
since. Most of the mines have since languished from
want of gold and from want of money, although the Stan-
dard started with half-a-million working capital. No
doubt it has been largely used in trying to “ boost ” Lake
View shares, and has been lost for ever in the attempt.
And this is the result of Whitaker Wright’s “pure
philanthropy.”
* * * •
The British Australasian on Whitaker
Wright: “ Casually as I have known him I have always
entertained for him that admiration which one cannot
withhold from a plucky fighter. Mr. Wright is one of
those rare characters who show up best when sorely be-
leaguered and when their back is to the wall. The late
Sir William Robertson used often to say : ‘ If only Wright
was as good a business man as he is a financier and fighter
he would indeed be invincible.’ Sir William omitted to
realise that business men can be bought by the score for
£lOO a year, whereas for the man of pluck, of individuality,
and of magnetism you may pay a fabulous figure and yet
get him cheap. 'Of such is Mr. Whitaker Wright,
and he well deserves the unique eulogium passed
on him by Lord Dufferin, himself a master of
men as Mr. W'right is of affairs. He is essentially a
market man. One never knows in fact whether he is a
“bull” ora “bear;” thus he obtains the might which
attends mystery.” There is a lot more similar hogwash,
published just one month before the cable announcing a
drop of millions in Lake View Consols, the ruin of the
London and Globe and of the Standard Exploration cos ,
and all on account of this great man Wright. Yet the
British Australasian will not kick itself for this mistake
—it will go on to soap the next Australian mining
boomster. The constant greasing of the Hooleys,
Bottomleys, Kaufmans, Wrights, and other boomsters is
the most deplorable and disgusting feature of London
financial journalism.
* * * •
Golden Arrow : The pallid 0. E. Hogg, con-
sulting engineer, company promoter and director, has
issued one more circular about the Golden Arrow, and
deals it out to a shareholder because he has asked to have
the property examined by an unbiassed expert and to
take such action as would ensure better management.
London Critic gives a list of the Westralian
mines in the promotion of which Hogg played an
important part. Nearly all have been reconstructed,
not one of them ever paid a div., and the list is long.
Hogg once estimated that the Golden Arrow down to
75ft. had a reserve of ore valued at £126,000, and as it
was increasing in width it justified the conclusion that
the quantity w T ould be doubled—giving upwards of
£400,000 as the value of ore in sight available for treat-
ment. Since that time the co. has been reconstructed
three times, and the liquidation in each instance has
been carried out in Hogg’s office by one of his nominees.
* * * *
Lake View : Shares between £8 and £9, and not
likely to slump until the “ bears ” have taken their profits.
Last week announced that Dufferin had resigned ; now
stated that he has withdrawn his resignation, and is re-
turning to England to meet the shareholders. As the
London and Globe Corporation finds it impossible to
realise on its assets at present, a special meeting to con-
sider resolutions for liquidation has been called. Under-
stood that the mine is being thoroughly re-sampled by
Hartmann and Nicholson, the last two managers. It will
be long ere the public will be re-sampled.
* * * *
“Miner” sends The Bulletin some more about
Donnybrook, N.Q. : “ Donnybrook is the most flourishing
mining camp in Queensland. It has two crushing mills
in course of erection, besides ten winding plants and
other machinery, ail within one mile. All the reefs to
the south and S.E. of Donnybrook were worked 30 years
ago and abandoned, and afterwards fossicked by hun-
dreds of fossickers up to the present. But none of the
claims were systematically worked until Mr. A. L.
Wilson took up the block off the Donnybrook P.C., and
found payable gold below the sulphide zone. The
last crushing of 200 tons gave Boz. to the ton,
and now he (Mr. Wilson) is sinking on another block
(No. 2), which he expects to bottom at 700 ft. The Donny-
brook P.C. crushed about 300 tons tor 6oz. per ton, and
the Erin’s Hope is bagging all the prize ore and sending
it to Illawarra for over 20oz. to the ton. This s3 T ndicate
is putting up a mill to treat its own stone. The Erin’s
Hope cuts the granite and porphyry in two ; the foot-
wall is granite and the hanging-wall porphyry. Same
may be said of the block, as the granite takes a dive
under the porphyry and no granite appears again for
miles. So, when sinking a block shaft, it is a great guide
that you have not bottomed until you strike the granite.”

“W. 8.”: “Pickaxe” (B. 17/11/’00) knows
little about the Richton property if he says “ the gold is
not there.” And lam far from being a “ long way out ”
in my statements. I said the mine was “within” two
miles of the Burdekin, and £60,000 was spent on a rail-
way, machinery, and a 40-head battery ”; which is cor-
rect, with the exception of the distance from the river,
but I said “within.” Now I am again in Charters
Towers, and have some more facts. Bremen and party,
who have a tribute in the Disraeli mine, lately crushed" 9
tons for 350 z. gold. James and party 7, also tributors, in
Nov. puVthrough 78 tons for 1260 z., and Thompson Bros,
had a good crushing lately—2Joz. per ton. Bremen’s
show, in the bed of the river, is improving. The first reef
found was about 18in. thick, then a footwall reef Ift. thick
was opened up, and another 2ft. The two footwall reefs
are flatter than the one first got, and it’s probable that
the three will join at a depth. They had a good crushing
from the reef first found. Martin and Grace, who had a
claim on the Englishman, have crushed 75 tons stone for
102 oz. gold, besides which their sandstone will bring all
round between £2 and £3 per ton. Martin and Grace
have more stone in sight, and are on a good thing. This
shows how little “Pickaxe” knows about the district
when he says the stone wasn’t there. Ere long you will
have a good deal more of the Richton.
• • • *
Three miners, including a Maorilander, have
managed to steal £4OOO worth of gold from the Russian
coast, opposite Cape Nome. In the course of the venture
they killed a sentry. The Russians know of their de-
posits, but impose too severe working conditions. There
seems to be auriferous sand right across, the Straits. The
further it is wrought from the beach the richer it
becomes. Profitable dredging is therefore expected. It
is a blackish sand, and Maorilanders compare it to their
ironsand.

Dear Bulletin.— Re that North Lyell par.
Surely you must give the Crotty-Lonergan-Madden crowd
credit for financial skill in steering the N. Lyell co. to
success. They are certainly wise in sending 1000 tons
weekly to the Lyell smelters—so as to obviate borrowing,
or a refloat, to perfect their reticulation, tramway, aerial
way, and meet their surveyors’ and other expenses ere the
smelters are erected. Again, the natural outlet of the
Lyell field is Kelly Basin—good railway topping Mts.
Dukes and Darwin with easy grades, plenty of timber
and flux, grand smelter-site between two rivers, (the King
and Governor). Whereas Lyell co. has a cumbersome Abt
system of railway ; is short of water in summer ; has to
haul ore over a mountain on aerial tramway, and
haulage would cost thousands to get near Jukes or Darwin.
The North’s 30 miles of rail is trained in 75min. as against
Lyell Co.’s 20 miles in lOOmin. See 1 The N. Lyell can
put ore into its train easier than into Lyell smelters. The
Lyell co. can take its own ore easier than anybody else's.
Transhipment means running a “ dummy ” after the N.
Lyell co. has pushed its ore half-a-mile past its own
aerial. Re combination— Surely you know that the clever
crowd incorporated most of the outside shows, such a 9
S. Lyell, Comstock, Consols, Jukes, Darwin and other
shows, to make their rich ore a successful smelting-
proposition. I predict the combine will emanate and be
effected by the North people taking in the Lyell co., with
Stieht as general boss.—J.O.
* • • •
N.S.W. Agent-General Copeland has replied to
Warnford Lock’s indictment of the N.S.W. Mining
laws. Says they need no defence, only a little explana-
tion ; that Lock had been barking up the wrong tree ;
that he had imported a private quarrel into the debate ;
that his management of Wentworth mine had brought
about a crisis, and, as he could not have everything his
own way, and the' N.S.W. Govt, would not alter the
laws to suit his own eccentricities, then everything
connected with the local mining law was everything that
was bad. He also charged Lock with treating working
miners with indignity in compelling them to be searched
and making them spies on one another. Solemn fact is
that the energetic Lock was not fit to deal with the free
and independent Australian miner. He would do all
right with nigger labor, or with whites in a forelock-pull-
ing land.
» • • «
The Victorian Parliament recently passed an
amending Companies Act which most carefully avoided
the question which, so far as banks are concerned, called
for most attention. The old Act was most specific in de-
manding that bank balance-sheets should contain full in-
formation, which was very good in theory. But the Com-
mercial Bank of Australia cheerfullj' dodges this by
putting £3,400,000 of its liabilities and a corresponding
amount (in paper-value) of its assets (the very worst
assets, on which the whole question of the bank’s
stability appears to depend) into a nominally separate
co.—separate only in name, for it really belongs
to the bank as much as the bank itself does. And about
this co. it gives no information —neither as to earnings,
profits, losses, depreciation, surplus, deficiency (which
i may be a million for all that the balance-sheet shows), or
anything else. Apparently there is nothing to prevent it
, going further, and putting the whole bank, all except £6
! or so, into an Assets Trust, and then giving no informa-
j tion at all about anything. And either the letter of the
| Vic. Companies Act allows this audacious evasion of its
spirit, or else the Vic. Companies Act isn’t enforced.

I Dear Bulletin. —Re answer to “ E. 0.” (B.
24 8/’00). Cash is the true coinage of China, the ex-
change at present being about 800 to the dollar (Mexican).
Sycee is simply silver bullion, either in the form, of shoes
(worth about 67 taels) or small irregular-shaped pieces.
The tael is valued at about 1 1-3 dol. ; exchange from 72
to 78 taels per 100 dol. The tael is not coined, but notes
of 10, 5, 1 taels, &c., are issued by the Chinese banks.
The Mexican dollar exchange at present is about 2s.
Money' matters in China are further complicated through
the use of the Russian rouble ; very like the old Mexican
valued at 10 per cent. less. The British dolk r
of the Straits and Hongkong is accepted in Shanghai
and the North at a small reduction, and the Hupeh,
Peiyang or Tientsin and Kwangsii dollars pass at a dis-
count in the bank. There are also lots of subs’diary
coins—2o and 10 cent pieces of the various province s—ail
at present at about 17 per cent, below face value. Thus
a Mexican dollar at a small Chinese bank can be changed
for 1110-cent pieces and some cash. A Russian dollar
for 10 10-cent pieces only'. The dollar has sterdiiy de-
creased in value from 4s. 6d. to 25., at about whie h figure
it has remained for nearly five years.—Cheenah, Shanghai.
* * * *
So far from electricity having, as was predicted,
rendered gas completely obsolete as an illuminant, and
made all gas-scrip into waste-paper, the use of the older
illuminant seems to be only now reaching its proper de-
velopment. A notable feature of the current Sydney
■Commonwealth illuminations is the Australian Gas Co.’s
installation, at Wynyard Square, of 18 gas-.umps, fitted
with three VVelsbach burners each, and giving a total
18,000 candle-power of a beautifully pure and steady
light. The principle involved is a trebling—by means of
automatic water-compressors—of the ordinary gas-pres-
sure.
The Powers have announced that they
won’t allow Turkey' to send any Mussulman mis-
sionaries to comfort the faithful in China. Does
this portend the shutting down on the missionary
business generally or is it only a move to chec :
any spreading of the influence of Russia’s old
enemy—the Sultan ?
If the “ gentleman ” (unnamed) who has
been relating his experiences as a prisoner with
De Wet is telling the truth, De W. aforesaid must
have "been pulling his leg to some purpose. The
gentleman says the Boer General had 60C0
men and 18,000 horses —which would need a
daily 12 tons of food and .180 tons of forage 1—
and this in a country laid-waste. Looks simply
as if the Boers had been at the campaigning
dodge of stuffing a prisoner with lies, and then
turning him loose, or allowing him to “escape.”
The nameless one says De Wet “ admitted that
he. was beaten.” But being beaten in war is
being forced to do what your adversary wants you
to do, and so far De Wet has apparently not
been forced to do that. De Wet’s game is evidently
to avoid close fighting, in that he can’t afford to
lose men, so when large bodies of British come in
his way he simply leaves behind a few to keep
them amused and “gets” with the bulk of his
men. Petty British entrenched posts (like
Dewetsdorp) and small convoys are his objec 4 .
He can master them, and they supply him with
ammunition, food, horses, and waggons.
Edward Knox, of the Colonial Sugar Re-
fining Co., and chairman of the Commercial'
Banking Co. of Sydney, died suddenly last Mon-
day, in his 82nd year. He was a native of
Denmark, of English parents, and was educated
at the Danish school of Soroe, and in Germany.
He arrived in N.S.W. in 1840, and m
1843 became manager of the Australian
Sugar Co., afterwards merged in the pre-
sent C.S.R. Co. The deceased, who was
known throughout life as the straightest of men
and the most considerate of employers, was pre-.
sident of the Union Club of Sydney, and was in
a very wide circle probably the most popular man
of his generation in N.S.W. His eldest surviving
son, E. W. Knox, is manager of the Colonial
Sugar Co. ; other sons are T. F. Knox, managing
director in Sydney of Dalgety and Co., and
Adrian Knox, of the equity bar; he leaves also
four daughters—Mrs. W. O. Gilchrist, Mrs.
E. H. Mackay (Q.), and two unmarried.

Government Notices.
The Treasury, New South Wales,
Sydney, 24th December, 1900.
TREASURY BILLS ACT OF 1899.
rpENDERS will be received up to and not later than Ist
February, 1901, for the purchase of £500,000 worth of
Treasury Bills, to be issued under the authority of the
Treasury Bills Act of 1899, which provides that, for the
purpose'of carrying out certain Public Works, the raising
of a sum not exceeding Four Millions sterling is
sanctioned.
Such bills to the amount mentioned above, viz.,
£500,000, will have a currency of five years, and will bear
interest at the rate of 31 per centum per annum, payable
half-yearly, said interest to accrue from Ist February,
1901, and be repayable at the Treasury at par.
A deposit of 10 per cent, on the total amount of Tender,
in Sydney Bank drafts payable on demand, cheques on
Sydney endorsed by banks, or Sydney Money Orders,
must accompany each Tender, and the balance of
accepted Tenders will be payable at the Treasury, Sydney,
within three days of date of allotment.
Bills will be issued of the following denominations, viz.,
£lOO, £5OO, £l,OOO, £5,000, and where possible will be
allotted in the values applied for by successful Tenderers,
but the Treasurer will not in any instance guarantee
division of values to Tenderers.
■ fApplications will be receivable for sums of (£100) one
hundred pounds and upwards.
Forms of Tender and all particulars can be obtained at
the Treasury, Sydney, on and after Ist January, 1901. i
Tenders to be addressed “ Under Secretary for Finance j
and Trade,” and endorsed “ Tender for Treasury Bills.” j
The highest or any Tender not necessarily accepted.
2a7. WILLIAM JOHN LYNE. I

Taken I'll In The Street.
On the 26th of January, 1898, a young lady,
passing through Burton Street, in Darlinghurst,
was suddenly taken ill. Fortunately, she was
seen by the people of a house near by, who am-
ducted her within doors, administered a stimu-
lant, and cared for her until she was able to go
home.
This, you may say, was hardly an incident
worth putting in print. That depends on what
it signified. To a besieged and starving garrison
the sound of a distant bugle is a matter to make
the heart jump.
On arriving at her house, we are sorry to say,
the young lady had a second seizure, and a doctor
was at once called in.
No, it was not the nerves, or a rush of blood to
the head ; the symptoms pointed to a thoroughly
disordered state of digestion ; ulcerated stomach,
probably. So spoke the medical man, after a
careful diagnosis.
Such being the doctor’s opinion, he prescribed
on the lines suggested by the facts. The sequel
proved him right, albeit his treatment had but
little beneficial effect. For this he is not to be
blamed, as there are few diseased conditions more
difficult to correct than the one he met with in
this instance. Many physicians decline to touch
such cases—sending the sufferers to hospitals.
In a letter dated August 20th, 1899, the young
lady describes the events alluded to here, and
informs us as to the final result.
“It was early in 1897,” she says, “that my
health, previously good, began to fail. In spite of
all we could do, I got worse, and in the middle of
that year my state was dreadful. Not only was
my appetite wholly destroyed but I abhorred the
very siyht of food. From the most savoury and
attractive meals that could be put on the table I
turned away in disgust,
“That this was unnatural and alarming, of
course, I realised ; but what could be ’done ?
Hardly a day passed without my suffering agony
with a twisting, griping, gnawing feeling in the
stomach.
“ The pleasure and comfort went out of my life
as completely as the sparkle vanishes from a lake
on a cloudy day.
“At length I acted on my mother’s urgent andr re-
peated advice, and began the use of Mother Seigel’s
Syrup. She said it had wrought a wonderful
cure on her ; and why should it not on me ? So I
said yes, and we sent for it. Before I had taken
four bottles the trouble passed away, and I have
been a strong, healthy girl ever since—and that is
now eighteen months.” —Maud MeGuinness, 158
Burton Street, Darlinghurst, Sydney, N.S.W.*
A Voice from Gath and Ashdod.
In self-defence the “ separated brethren ” of
Melbourne have resolved to manifest themselves
(modestly) in a clubbic manner. The articles of
faith and membership are appended :
THE PHILISTINES.
This is the Confession of Faith :
Whosoever will be of us, before all things it is necessary
that he hold the Philistine faith.
And the Philistine faith is this—that ws worship Genius
and Friendship.
Neither confounding admiration with affection nor
dividing Ability.
For there is one person of the Friendship and another
person of the Genius.
But the heart and the head be all one—the Affection
equal, the Genius eternal.
The Friendship not necessary to be comprehended but
only to be returned—and the Ability incomprehensible.
And Genius has a triune Godhead—the first the mind,
which is the act of a man ; the second the soul, which is
the emotion of a woman in the mind of a man ; and the
third the spirit, which is neither man nor woman, but of
both.
Yet there be not three Gods but two Gods.
The first God is Love, and the second God Genius.
The first not born but create, the second neither born
nor created but proceeding.
And to each and both his angels—Belial, Baal and
Moloch; Rimmon, Dagon and Azrael ; Mammon, Agag
and Goliath ; Beelzebub, Endor, Ichabod and Doeg.
And of these not one nor another is greater, but co-
equal in all ways and manners.
And unto each his sign. To Belial the sign generant—
to Moloch the blood-gout—to Baal a tower of his high
places;
And the sign of Dagon a fish, and of Azrael a wing ;
And the sign of Agag a boot with holes in the sole
thereof—and the sign Of Goliath a bat of brick ;
And the sign of Beelzebub a house-fly—and the sign of
Rimmon a terrace;
And the sign of Mammon a farthing—and of Endor a
broom of birch ;
And the sign of Ichabod a setting sun—and the sign of
Doeg, the Edomite, a hornet.
And unto each his house and habitation —unto Belial,
Philistia and the Palace of Black Wrath—and unto
Moloch, Gehenna —and unto Baal, Askelon ;
And unto Dagon, Gath—unto Azrael of the Wing,
Adullam ;
And unto Agag, Lehi—and unto Goliath, Gilboa ;
And unto Beelzebub, Tophet—and unto Rimmon,
Suburbia;
And the house of Mammon is Gaza—and the house of
Endor is Ajalon ;
And the house of Ichabod is Timnath—and the house
of Doeg, the Edomite, is Tarsus.
And the souls of these, even from Belial unto Doeg, the
Edomite, enter into the bodies of such as are found faith-
ful, and the names given unto them by their fathers are
for ever lost.
He therefore that will be one of us must think thus of
us.
And the right faith is that we believe and confess—
that there are but two Gods —Love and Genius.
And yet there is but one God—for Genius producth
Love, and Love begetteth Genius.
Perfect God as to the Genius, and perfect Man as to the
Love.
And any that would be of us must acknowledge us as
his Angels.
And at the end all Gods and Men shall go unto judg-
ment ; yea, even we that are Philistines.
And they that have done good shall go into the tenth
edition, apd they that have wrought evil shall not be sold
on the bookstalls.
* * * *
THE LITANY.
Remember not, oh Lord of Love and Genius, our
offences when we did write tripe—spare us our just
punishment for the line that did not scan.
Spare us, good Lord !
Prom all painting of poster girls in a crimson meadow,
and from the necessity of writing for Punch—
Good Lord, deliver us !
Prom all blindness of heart, from pride, vain glory and
hypocrisy, from envy, hatred and malice, and all un-
charitableness—
Good Lord, deliver us !
Prom all dukes and princes—from shoddy titles,
mayors, councillors, and magistrates, and from all credi-
tors who have hardness of heart and contempt of OUR
WORD and commandment —
Good Lord, deliver us 1
That it may please thee to bless and preserve all such
as buy our copy, or our drawings; to illuminate all
editors with a true knowledge and understanding of OUR
WORTH so that both by their payment and publication
they may set forth and shew it accordingly—
We beseech thee to hear us, good Lord !
That it may please thee who alone art able to work great
miracles, to endow McKinley, of Punch, with grace,
wisdom and understanding—-
We beseech thee to hear us, good Lord !
That it may please thee to keep all Philistines—and to
comfort all such as are of us —and to fill the pipe and glass
of them for ever and ever—
We beseech thee to hear us, good Lord !
That it may please thee to preserve for posterity all who
write, to help all authors laboring of book, and all artists
in cross-presentation of picture—to comfort all such as
play the bassoon, and to show thy pity upon all that
listen thereto —
We beseech thee to hear us, good Lord !
That it may please thee to give unto us the whole
world in a bag, so that the people shall crown us with
garlands—and to bring back the golden age upon earth —
We beseech thee to hear us, good Lord ! _
That it may please thee to give unto the Great Public
a heart to love and dread us, so that they may ever-
more have affiance in us and seek always our honor and
glory—
We beseech thee to hear us, good Lord !
* • * »
LET US PRAY !
Oh, Thou Great God of all the Arts, who sitteth at the
right hand of Shakespeare ! Oh, Thou to whom Darwin
is acolyte, and Mozart and Asaph chorus, and Velasquez
the gonfalonier. Oh, Thou who inspired the first steers-
men of the rafts of Lebanon to the havens of Joppa—Oh,
Thou who hast translated men from dust and made the
stones find a voice—Oh, Thou who sendest true Love and
true Genius to travail in the Four Ways of Poverty, and
to tarry in the streets, of Hunger, Cold, Waiting, and Con-
tumely that they may catch a beam of thy great light,
and by it win their own—Oh, Thou who liveth in all
things, and wert old or ever the chimneys of hell were
hot, and yet art ever young—give unto us the vagrom
spark—the lacrum of that divine fire that burneth some-
where everlastingly.—Amen !

Government Notices.
Land and Income Tax Office, Custom House,
Sydney, 18th December, 1900.
LAND AND INCOME TAX ASSESSMENT ACT
OF 1895.
IST OTIOE is hereby given, under section 30 of the above
Act, that Returns of Income received during the
year 1900 for the purposes of the assessment of Income
Tax for the year 1901 are required to be furnished to the
Commissioners of Taxation, at their office, on or before
the second day of February, 1901, and all persons who
are in receipt of income, and all persons liable under the
provisions of the Act in any representative capacity to
furnish returns for the purposes aforesaid, are hereby
informed that the prescribed Forms of Returns are now
available, and may be obtained (except those for Com-
panies) on application at any Post Office, Police Office,
Custom House, Railway Station, and the Office of any
Clerk of Petty Sessions in New South Wales, Stamp
Duties Office, Phillip-street, Sydney, or at the Taxation
Office, Custom House, Sydney.
Forms for Companies can be obtained at the Stamp
Duties Office, Phillip-street, Sydney, or at the Taxation
Office, Custom House, Sydney.
All persons carrying on any business, trade, or profes-
sion on their own account are required to furnish Income
Returns irrespective of the amount of their income ; but
no return of Income is required from any person in receipt
of salary or wages only if the gross annual amount of such
salary or wages does not exceed £2OO.
All employers of labour, whether they themselves are
liable to make Income Tax Returns or not, are required
to furnish to the Commissioners of Taxation the name,
occupation, and addresses of all employes whose salaries
or wages for the year 1900 aggregated £l6O and upwards.
Public attention is called to the fact
that a penalty not exceeding Twenty
pounds is imposed on any person neglect-
ing to furnish the Return required of him within
the time above mentioned, and that persons failing to
furnish such Return are not relieved from liability to the
penalty by reason only that they may not have received a
notice to furnish the Return, or that the prescribed Form
may not have been delivered to them.
The attention of owners of taxable lands subject to
mortgage is invited to the necessity for furnishing the
requisite particulars, in order that they may derive the
benefit of the deduction to which they are entitled in
respect of the interest on the mortgage on their lands,
including improvements thereon. Without these par-
ticulars the Commissioners will be unable to make the
statutory deduction from the tax on the unimproved
values.
Forms of the prescribed Return may be obtained on
application at any of the abovementioned offices, and
should be furnished on or before the second day of Feb-
ruary, 1901, before mentioned.
N. LOCKYER,
R. N. JOHNSON,
L. S. SPILLER,
Commissioners
of
Taxation.
N.B.—Particular attention is called to the observations
and directions with the Forms of Return. 2clo

COMMON WEALTH.
At Poverty Point.
fid. Bulletin. —J. A. Delany dropped a bombshell into
a perfectly self-satisfied mutual admiration society when,
at the recent “ send-off” to Hazon, he fired off the cold,
hard fact that Australian musicians, no matter how
talented, are thought little of in their own country until
they have received the hall-mark of European approba-
tion. J. O. Williamson, who was present, cited, in oppo-
sition, the relations which existed between his firm and
Melba, but only managed to strengthen Delany's hands,
by stating that he (J.C.W.) had recently offered Melba
20. times more per night to re-visit Australia than he had
paid her a week prior to her departure. Sydney D.T. fol-
lowed with a leader on the subject, in which, of course,
the inevitable Marchesi cropped-up. Now, it may interest
musical Australians to know that Mine. Marchesi’s
“ world-wide fame ” exists here, and here only. She is
very small potatoes in Paris. Her pupils are nearly all
Australians, with a few Americans and English. French,
Italian or German pupils she has none. Melba has boomed
Marchesi as Albani boomed Lamperti. Lamperti could
boast of numerous eminent artists as pupils, but, from
personal experience, present scribe can affirm that these
same artists, in hardly any case, took away from Lam-
perti anything they had not brought to begin with.
Printer’s ink, judiciously used, works wonders.— Spogato.
Melbourne’s boss basso, W. G. Barker, has been in a
bad way lately. Many people, including Tiie Bulletin,
used to say of him that he would be altogether admirable
if he could only put a little devil into his work. With a
grand voice, good vocalisation, and a rather striking
appearance, Barker was, nevertheless, an unsatisfactory
artist. He looked and sang as though he had some
trouble on his mind. Poor fellow ! it now appears that
he was afflicted with a cranial tumor, which explains the
want of vivacity in his platform style. Several pieces of
diseased bone were taken from his head the other
Saturday, and it is hoped that the operation will make a
new man of the estimable “ W.G.”
Re Arthur Styan’s acting in “ With Flying Colors,” a
Melb. daily said “ he reminds one very forcibly of Mr.
Titheradge at his best.” Styan is a clever artist, but how
does he suggest a Titheradge performance, except in
being quietly effective ? The criticism reminds one that
the Australian stage of late years has had only two quiet
“ heavy ” actors who could play emotional parts in strik-
ing style—Titheradge and Styan. Other successful lead-
ing men have been quiet and light, or heavy and robusti-
ous when they were not vehemently heroic. These
ranged from Boucieault and Brough to Wilson Barrett,
Julius Knight and Cartwright. By the way, Styan did
some admirable work as a member of Cartwright’s co.
His Lord Jura, in “ Moths,” made a pathetic decease, and
his.Loftus Roupell, in “ The Tree of Knowledge ” was a
convincing sinner of the hard, reposeful type.
Le Guide Musical, Brussels, on one of Lalla Miranda’s
recent performances : “ ‘ Romeo et Juliette ’ had a genuine
interest from the very personal interpretation given to
the character by the heroine. It was no longer the
country Juliette with manners of last year. What a dis-
tinction, what reserve, what candour and piquancy, with
sometimes a little coyness. Her attitudes and very aris-
tocratic appearance have an exquisite charm.” And of
her singing, the same critic says: “ Mdlle. Miranda’s
brilliant execution of the ‘ Valse Song ’ brought to her a
great ovation. In the sentimental passages her voice has
very caressing intonations; the cres. and dim. are
admirable.”
Mrs. Brown-Potter recently stated that she had no in-
tention of returning to Australia, although she had
received handsome offers from both Williamson and Mus-
grove. By the way, she is said to have made money at
mining. Many stage charmers get the real straight tip.
For instance, Melba is said to have made a haul of
£40,000 out of Spanish copper-scrip. ,> . . . -
On the day after the funeral of Sir Arthur Sullivan, ten
of his songs were sung at a ballad concert at St. James
Hall, London. The Morning Post criticism said that
“ Orpheus with his Lute,” which was “ one of the earliest
and certainly most beautiful ” of Sullivan’s vocal com-
positions, was “ very sweetly rendered by Miss Florence
Schmidt.”
Maud Ohetwynd, the bright little woman showing con-
spicuously as the millionaire’s daughter in “Floradora,”
at Melb. Majesty’s, is quite anew recruit, and has come
to the front with a rush. She was married to a son of
Dr. Singleton, the big Vic. philanthropist, and is a St.
Kilda girl.
The company of the Savoy Theatre, identified of late
years with Gilbert and Sullivan opera, contributed the
prettiest of tributes to Arthur Sullivan’s funeral show.
A circle of white flowers framing a seven-stringed harp
with the fifth string broken—the composer having died in
his fifth decade. And somebody wrote a couple of “In
Memoriam ” verses which were not too bad
Sleep softly. We who live shall hear alway
songs that you gave us while you walked the earth ;
Some exquisite voice shall echo every day
Music you made for sorrow or for mirth.
Sleep quietly, but hear, as in a dream,
Magical music of the enchanted night;
Sleep on, and, as you listen, may it seem
They, too, were yours, the songs you did not write.
“ What kind of reception did I get in Dunedin?” says
Donald Macdonald, the returned war-lecturer. “ Well,
every third man there is a Macdonald and every'tenth
man Donald Macdonald, so you can judge for yourself.”
The only new thing under the sun is the outrageously
funny scene of Bland Holt’s home, built on a right-of-
way. Just as Bland is cuddling his new wife a chaw-
bacon coolly walks across, then a coster, a man comes on
with a donkey, another with a bulldog, which chases Bland
round the furniture. The whole is a piece of impossible
gTptesquerie, but it tickles the diaphragm with its memo- I
ries for a week after.
Nellie Stewart gets a startling effect with her final cos-
tume in “ Cinderella.” The shapely form of the principal
boy is encompassed from neck to toe in one close fitting
silK garment of the same glowing pink as her naked arms.
Ovpr this is the coat of crystal— a garment nothing more
th|n a yard-and-a-half of scintillating jewels, with a hole
fos the wearer’s neck. The sides are completely open,
affording more than glimpses of Nellie’s sides, and it ter-
minates just below her broad hips.
That extremely decollete costume of Mine. Darto, in the
Princess’s pantomime, got out of order one night last
week, and the public were treated to a most unusual
vision. Mme., however, pluckily kept on and finished
her dance, but the crowd lost its head, and demanded
encores before it would permit the performance to go on
and Mme. to adjust the errant elastic.
The imitators of Charles Godfrey are badly off for songs
these times. If they had all the natural endowments of
poor thirsty Charley—voice, talent, and magnetism—
they couldn’t make much impression with the clumsy
hunks of bathos they heave at Rickards’s audiences.
George D’Albert, the latest and most forcible of these
misguided grafters, would be acceptable enough if he
sang some of the best of Godfrey’s ditties. Why not ?
Geo. Ridgway gets a show as clown in Musgrove’s
“ Cinderella” matinees. George thought he had washed
the grease-paint off altogether. He has long been known
as a Melb. master bill-poster. He was the star trapezist
of the Lenton troupe, and learnt the business from
Leotard, at London Alhambra, with the use of Leotard’s
ropes.
According to London Daily Chronicle Sir Arthur Sulli-
van’s new opera, in active rehearsal at the time of his
death, is never likely to be performed.
“ I backed a horse at ten to one and he came in at a
quarter to four.”— Funny man in “ Cinderella."
Marshall-Hall is going to do a big business with his
Conservatorium, and will yet thank his lucky stars that
he was canted out of the University.
Ed. Bulletin. —Can you tell me why the
Sydney town clerk publicly appeared on the day
of the swearing-in, adorned with a barrister’s
wig ? I have never heard that the ingenious
gentleman had any connection with any branch of
the law. Was it merely an advt. ? —B.C,L.
From Argus, 27/12/’00 : —
Yesterday afternoon’s trains were both well filled, but
not crowded. Among those who left Melbourne were Mr.
Alfred Deakin, Mr. Frederick Sargood, M.L.O., Mr. George
Bruce, and Sub-inspector Ward, and Detectives Mac-
manamny, Bannon, and Fryer, who are despatched to
watch the Melbourne criminals during the celebrations.
Which is particularly rough on Deakin.
Rev. David Laseron, who was so badly
wounded in a Sydney railway-carriage some years
ago by a bullet from a pistol flourished by Tommy
Walker, ex-M.P., somehow or other nearly man-
aged to shoot himself with a revolver at a Blue
Mountain hotel the other day. He explains that
he bought the weapon as a present for his son and
didn’t know it was loaded. When last heard of
the pistol-persecuted parson was still suffering
from severe shock, partly attributable, he says, to
the association in his mind between revolvers and
his own narrow escape years ago.


Three Years
Liver Trouble.
CLEMENTS TONIC SOLELY RESPONSIBLE FOR CURE.
The Case or
Mrs. KATHERINE GARLAND.
(by our special reporter.)
“ Your visit has come as a pleasant
surprise to me,” was the remark with which
Mrs. Garland, of No. 4 Denham-street,
Surry Hills, greeted the reporter from this
journal, as it savts me the trouble of
writing to you about my bad attack of liver
complaint, from which I suffered terribly.
I am a very poor correspondent at the best
of times, preferring like most members of
my sex, to receive letters rather than write
them ; but I am so conscious of a deep
obligation to Clements Tonic for restoring
me to health that I made up my mind to
write expressing my gratitude, and as you
have obviated that duty 1 will gladly give
the fullest details.
“ How long did your liver trouble afflict
y°u ? ” asked the reporter.
“For fully three years, and I hope it will
never come on again. Do you know, I
believe I would rather be dead than
continue like I was then. The first s.gn of
my . failing health was an unaccountable
fastidiousness regarding my food. I some-
times felt like eating a little, and as soon as
it was before me I should turn right against
it, and do nothing but mince and turn it
over, then finally leave it alone. There
was always a sickly, sour taste in my mouth,
my . tongue being so dry and rough,
particularly in the morning, and I was often
seized with a fit of vomiting, which was
invariably followed by the act of belching
distasteful gases from the stomach. The
operation of ridding myself of the bile and
so ir wind from the stomach caused me no
end of discomfort and pain, and blinding
headaches were frequently experienced
after I had been retching. Heavy pains in
the shoulders, and sometimes in the chest,
often paid me more attentions? than was
agreeable, as they made me form the
opinion that I was slowly falling into the
clutches of some dreadful disease. Indeed,
this idea was made all the more likely by
the yellowish hue of my skin, and my eyes
became a dirty green colour. Liver spots
also came out on my chest. Besides the
puns in the chest and shoulders, I would
have severe attacks in the side, and they
were so excessively tormenting that I had
10 a Pply hot fomentations, but they only
i >.ve temporary relief, and made the next
attack more acute. It was next door to
impossible to ever get a brief respite from
iny many ailments, for I could scarcely ever
get to sleep, and the little repose that did
occasionally come to me was marked by
unpleasant dreams and restlessness. On
awakening it required a great stretch of
imagination to believe I had slept at all,
so weary and fatigued would I feel. My
limbs ached with sheer weakness, and I’m
*« re I was an obj'ect deserving compassion
when crawling about the house in my vain
efforts to do a little housework, only to sit
down quite exhausted and fret for hours.
When I wanted to stand up I had to do so
carefully and steadily, otherwise a fainting
giddiness would take charge of me, and my
head would whirl round like a spinning-
wheel. These fainting sensations left me
dreadfully depressed and low-spirited, and
I. sat or lay brooding over my misfortunes
till I got very melancholy. There was not
an ounce of flesh on my bones. So weak and
debilitated had I become that I was almost
afraid to look in the glass.”
“You look remarkably well now, how-
ever, Mrs. Garland.”
*' Yes, thank God, I am like I used to be
before my three years’ sickness. But you
have not learned all my troubles. The
worst and most terrifying thing has yet to
be described. ©I have not yet mentioned the
anxiety my heart used to cause me. You
do not think I look like a woman whose
heart is diseased, do you ? No ; I’m sure
I don’t. But I was almost worried to death
with that thought when I was so ill. My
heart used to start suddenly beating with
such violence that I dreaded it stopping or
easing off, in case it would stop for ever.
But it would quieten down after nearly
scaring the life out of me, only to start
again with renewed rapidity. I have since
learned that palpitation of the heart occurs
in liver disease cases when they are very
serious, so mine must have been a very bad
case.”
“And what treatment did you receive
from your doctor ? ”
“ Oh, the usual—medicines and pills, and
I don’t mind telling you that the liquids
they termed medicines did me no end of
injury. Why, I used to loathe the thought
of swallowing it; the smell was offensive
and the taste was disgusting. C But I
persevered with them for three years’ all to
no purpose. Then a friend told me that
Clements Tonic was the best thing for me to
take, which I did, and you can see the
result for yourself. Clements Tonic seemed
a little bitter at first, but I could soon drink
it like water, without any reluctance what-
ever. But even if it had been gall I would
have drank it, because it brought about my
convalescence in no time. I told my doctor
I would rather have Clements Tonic than all
his stuff, as it was doing me good, and his
physic had completely failed. He replied :
‘ Well, I’ll not make much out of you now.’
And he spoke truthfully, for I can be
independent of all doctors now. I rapidly
experienced ease from pains in the stomach,
chest and shoulders, and the easing of pains
in the head brought delightful, soothing
sleep. My appetite came back, and I did
not notice any discomfort after eating. The
liver spots were eradicated, and my skin
recovered its usual colour. No more fears
of heart disease worried me, as the palpit-
ation disappeared with the other disagree-
able symptoms. To be as brief as possible,
I must candidly admit that Clements Tonic
is solely responsible for my cure. When my
son-in-law was up in the mountains he wrote
saying he was sick, and I sent him a couple
of bottles of Clements Tonic to Katoomba.
He subsequently thanked me, stating that
the medicine I had forwarded had cured
him. One of my daughters has been under
treatment during the past twelve months
for asthmatical bronchitis (another doctor
says it is heart disease she has got). They
have not done her the least good with their
phjsic, so lately she has been taking
Clements Tonic. She has had three bottles
up to now, and she is making good progress
in the right direction. I believe Clements
Tonic will make a woman of her again. My
faith in it is without limit, and I am so
grateful for its effect upon myself and
relatives that you can publish my testimony
as much as you please.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION'.
I, Katherine Garland, of No. 4 Denham-street,
Sorry Hills, Sydney, in the Colony of New South
Wales, do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have
carefully read the annexed document, consisting of
five folios, and consecutively numbered from one to
five, and that it contain* and is a true and faithful
account of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic,
and also contains my full permission to publish in any
way my statements—which I give voluntarily, without
receiving any payment; and I make this solemn
declaration conscientiously believing the same to be
true, and by virtue of the provisions of an Act made
and passed in the ninth year of the reign of her
present Majesty, intituled “An Act for the more
effectual abolition of Oaths and Affirmations taken
and made in the various Departments of the Govern-
ment of New South Wales, and to substitute Declara-
tions in lieu thereof, and for the suppression of
voluntary and Extra-judicial Oaths and Affidavits."
Declared at Surry Hills, this 11th day of August,
one thousand nine hundred, before me,
A. DAYIDSON, J.P,
