Old Days and Old Deeds.
AUSTRALIA'S FIRST WHITE CAIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Austbalia had a narrow escape ! Instead of
South Africa, New Holland might be in this year
of grace the scene of a struggle for supremacy
between Boer and Briton; and the name of the
firbt governor of the Antipodean States-General
might be Stadtholder Boudewijn van der Mylen,
instead of the Earl of Hopetoun —but for an acci-
dent.
In the year 1628, when England was trembling
on the verge of the Great Civil War between
Charles I. and his Parliament, and the King was
confronted by an angry Commons, with its famous
and strongly-drafted “Petition of Rights,” Hol-
land, just emerging from the sea of troubles in
which her struggle with Spain had involved her,
and vigorous in a newly-won nationhood, was
casting eager eyes for new lands and fresh outlets
for her commerce. Rumours of spice islands and
golden continents came washed up by the waves
to the shores of dyke-buttressed north-lands, and
the shrewd men of the market-place listened with
Midas-ears. So it came about that a great project
was set afoot to colonise the latest-won territory,
and to plant a Hutch factory on the north-western
coast of that “Terra Australis Incognita, the
existence of which had haunted the dreams of men
for ages. An expedition was fitted out; the
Batavia and two other ships were got
ready for sea; and Francis Pelsart was ap-
pointed Commodore of the little fleet. The
vessels were manned by Hollanders, Danes,
Norwegians, Erenchmen, Germans and English-
men (much like the British merchantmen of
to-day) the Seventeenth Century scum
of the dockyards, and the riff-raff of the water-
side taverns. The intending colonists appear to
have been wastrels and ne’er-do-wells, with a
sprinkling of more earnest folk ; but subsequent
events showed, curiously enough, that the loyal
section of the expedition consisted mainly of
French soldiers, led by a presumptive Scotchman
named Webby Hays. , , , , . ,
As soon as the Batavia had cleared the roads
with her two consorts the supercargo of the Com-
modore’s ship, a designing fellow named Jerome
Cornelius, began to sound the crew as to their
willingness to take part in a general rising, with
a view to the seizure of the vessel, the murder of
all not of their party, and a career of piracy on
the high seas. This Cornelius had been a chemist,
and his past was of dubious complexion ; but he
soon found tools to further his enterprise, and
simply bided his time and opportunity. The
accomplishment of his project was, however, a
matter of some difficulty, for his hands were tied
while the other ships kept company with Pelsart.
In due course the three vessels made the Cape of
Good Hope, there “refreshed,” and then stood
away across the uncharted seas that stretched
between South Africa and North-Western Aus-
tralia. By-and-by came a week of storm, and
the consorts were hopelessly separated. Cor-
nelius did not, however, strike his blow. His sea-
manship was of too poor a quality for him to
assume the responsibility of the command ) so he
suffered his chief to live until the weather
became calmer. But one moonlight nighty when
all was ready for the uprising, the Batavia
struck on a reef of rocks known as Hout-
mann’s Abrolhos, somewhat south of the lati-
tude of the present town of Gerald ton on the
Westralian mainland. Great difficulty was
experienced in landing the crew and passengers
on the two islands of the Pelsart Group—subse-
quentlv known as the “Batavia’s Churchyard
and ‘ ‘ Th wart-the-way. ” Yery little water could
be brought ashore, and there was none to be found
on the Abrolhos rocks. In the confusion of the
wreck, the supercargo had been left behind, and
his murderous plot was temporarily quashed.
Confronted by all the horrors of a water-famine,
Pelsart took with him eight companions, and, set-
ting out in the Batavia’s “ sloop,” steered for the
mainland on a quest for water. He did not find
any, and, instead of putting-about, kept on a
course for the Dutch settlement of Batavia, where
he ultimately arrived in safety, secured a ship,
and returned to the castaways to discover a per-
fect Pandemonium of murder and rapine.
On the tenth day after the shipwreck, Jerome
Cornelius was washed ashore on a piece of the
bowsprit, accompanied by a cask of wine, a cask
of water, and a barrel of vinegar. He found
affairs in splendid trim for his mutinous purpose.
Pelsart and his most trusty officers were absent
searching for fresh water, the riff-raff of the crew
had broken into the rum-casks, and were whisper-
ing of love and of loot in eager groups of disap-
pointed riders. But first Cornelius had to be rid
of Webby Hays and his five faithful Frenchmen,
so they were sent away in a boat to look for water,
armed only with two old Dutch muskets. They
were absent 20 days, and returned to the Batavia s
Churchyard only to find a general massacre m full
progress.
The first step in the conspiracy was the swear-
ing-in of the conspirators. This accomplished,
Cornelius had himself proclaimed Captain-General
of the settlement. His intention was to murder
all— save some forty of his own creatures—and
then to await Pelsart’s return in the ship he
should obtain from the Dutch Government at
Batavia, to lure the crew ashore, to make them
drunk preparatory to killing them, and to seize
the vessel for “freetrade” on the Spanish Mam.
Prior to the general massacre, however, he began
the study of his butcher’s r6le by a little prepara-
tory slaughter. .
In,his subsequent confessions, obtained by the
application of torture, he admitted to having the
chief barber put to death because he stood in the
way of another buccaneer, and “had not wished
to dance to all their tunes.” The Council of the
Mutineers consisted of three others beside him-
self, “and whenever it had been resolved among
them to kill anyone, it was immediately carried
out.” He was very thorough in his methods.
When a party of four fled from the water and
took refuge in his tent, he ordered a fellow named
Jan Hendricks to despatch them, saying, “ Go
and kill them ! ” —a command which was promptly
obeyed. When the sick were to bj put out of the
way, Cornelius and two of his Council took a com-
plaisant tool named De Vries and led him to the
huts of all the patients, eleven in number, and
ordered him to cut their throats —and De Vries
performed his task with alacrity ; and when the
mutineer Peters had cut the throat of the carpen-
ter’s assistant, it was done in the soi-disant
Captain-General’s presence.
One of his murders was peculiarly atrocious. It
was that of a cabin-boy, whom he first sent to
snare some birds, and then had him followed by
one of his accomplices, with instructions to catch-
up with the lad and cut his throat .while unaware
of his intended fate. A mutineer named Leonard
Michaels confessed that he had been sent out on a
raft by Cornelius with others of his gang to
Thwart-the-way . Island, with instructions to
drown and otherwise put an end to all the people
upon it —a commission which was faithfully
executed as far as the men on the place were con-
cerned, although some women and boys were
spared. The Captain-General also put swords in
the hands of three of his butchers, and told them
to kill De Vries —who really merited something of
the sort for the part he had taken in slitting the
throats of the sick. The family of the settlement s
clergyman had to be sacrificed to the buccaneering
needs of Cornelius and his crew ; but the clergy-
man himself was to be spared for certain diplo-
matic purposes. So the Captain-General sent for
his trusty van Huyssen, a past-master in the
gentle art of shearing weasands, and gave him a
quantity of specially-commandeered provisions,
saying, “ Conrad, invite me to dinner with you in
your tent this afternoon, to meet our good Minis-
ter and his eldest daughter. While we are din-
ing, Leonard Michaels and Wouter Loos, Hen-
dricks, and some others of the boys, will pay a
visit to the good man’s hut and provide for his
family in Heaven.” Then he added with a laugh,
“ And the parson won’t live long either.” And it
came to pass that these things were done, as he
had said.
His great general massacre was in its
incidence and vastness a coup (Pettit; but he
arranged several diplomatic disappearances as. he
gradually prepared for the final demonstration.
On the night of the 12th of July, he called
Leonard Michaels, Jan Hendricks, and another
out of their tents, and instructed them to cut the
throats of Musketeer Van der Enden, Car-
penter Heynoricks, and a sick ships boy—
a command which was strictly carried out. In
the morning of the 6th of August, he entered
the tent of one of his swashbuckler brigade, a
frank, straightforward murderer, named David
Seevanck; and there he gave to lusty Jan Hen-
dricks a dagger, with the remark, ‘ ‘ Go, and cut
out the heart of Stoffel Stoffels, that lazy lout who
stands there working as if his back was broken.
This, according to custom and usage, Jan Hen-
dricks straightway did. On the 16th August,
giving his sword to Jan van Bommel, this Cap-
tain-General of mutineers said: “ Go and try
whether it is sharp enough ; cut me off the head of
Coen Aid erts with it!” But Jan Bommel was
not strong enough in physique for this task, and
wept bitterly as he handed the weapon to Matthias
Beyer, on account of his feebleness of muscle.
Beyer was a villain of more stalwart build, how-
ever, and speedily shore off Coen’s head —in the
presence of the applauding Cornelius. On one
occasion he called into his tent a stout cabin-boy
named Roger Decker, to whom he gave a cup of
wine, together with his own dacrger, and the
injunction, “ Go outside and stab Hendrick Jans
of Purmerant to the heart” —and Hendrick Jans
was carefully and scientifically stabbed. Corne-
lius grew doubtful as to the fidelity of one of his
own followers, Jacob Heyndricks, head “ bottler”
or steward of the shipwrecked Batavia. He
ordered him, therefore, to be dealt with, but
David Seevanck interceded, saying “Jacob
Heyndricks is a good carpenter ; let him live ! ”
But Cornelius replied, “He is nothing but a
turncoat, and quite unreliable. He will only tell
on us some day ; he must be put out of the way.”
He*also ordered one John Pinter, “an English-
man,” to be killed—an order that was successfully
accomplished ; but he was not so fortunate with
the barber’s assistant, for when Albert Jans, the
Captain-General’s henchman, made a slash at'the
foredoomed man, his attempt was frustrated by
the bluntness of his sword. It did not pene-
trate the leathern jerkin of his intended victim,
who managed to escape by swimming away in the
darkness.
Jerome Cornelius was a compound of Frank
Butler, lago, and Louis de Rpugemont :
cut-throat, traitorous liar and imaginat»ve
romancer. He believed in himself. He believed
in God. All his fellow desperadoes he compelled
to swear fealty to himself with great and awful
oaths—“upon their soul’s salvation, and by the
help of the Most High.” These lusty murderers
were very religious men, and all breaches of faith
were safeguarded by orthodox anathemas. I heir
many-sworn covenants were ludicrously solemn—
“ May whoever does the contrary be the Devils
prey,” was one of their most forcible appeals to
fidelity to each other—and their leader. When
the general massacre was decided on, they set
about it with all the fanaticism of religious
enthusiasts.
The day of the massacre was coincident with
that of the return of Webby Hays and his five
loyal Frenchmen, from their search for fresh
water. Hays arrived in the middle of the
slaughter, and seeing the turn affairs had taken, at
once put his boat about and pulled for dear life to
a neighboring rock, called afterwards High, or
Square, Island. He was there joined by some
forty fugitives, who had escaped either by swim-
ming or on rafts. Here they piled rocks together
to form a fort, and Hays set a guard to watch,
day and night, against a surprise. The refugees
on Square Island also fashioned for themselves
weapons out of hoops and nails, which they bound
to sticks and staves with pieces of yarn and sizing.
Meanwhile the work of massacre went on with
vigor on the Island of “Batavia’s Churchyard,”
until there were on the rock only thirty men and four
boys ; and then David Seevanck took a boat, and
crossing over to Thwart-the-way, was joined by
Van Wilderen and his party, and between them
they murdered all the disaffected, save seven boys
and some women. The women were prey.
Cornelius served them out as loot of war to his
followers, giving his good and faithful comrade,
Conrad van Huyssen, the beautiful Judith, the
daughter of the clergyman —who, by the way, was
one of the signatories of the so-called Captain-
General’s commission. For himself,’ Cornelius
selected Lucretia, a widow of ripe and florid
charms. In his subsequent confession he speaks
with rapture of “her skin, so beautiful and fair.
Lucretia was not, however, to be won and worn
without a struggle on the lady’s part. She
responded but tardily to the buccaneer’s wooing.
For twelve days, thongh he was aided by the
“ Batavia’s” chest, the charming widow held the
Captain-General at bay. He complained bitterly
to his crony, David Seevanck, that neither
kindness nor anger helped him in Lucretia’s favor.
Seevanck, a man of Bismarckian methods said in
a scornful manner, “Don’t you know how to
manage that ? I’ll soon make her yield ! ” Where-
upon the Lieutenant entered his chief’s tent, and
said roughly to Lucretia, “I have complaints
about you ! ” She asked, “On what account?
“ Because you do not comply with the wishes of
the Captain-General in kindness. _ Now, however,
you will have to make up your mind, either you
will go the same way as Wvbrecht Claes, or else
you must do that for which we have kept the
women ! ” Lucretia yielded that day and lived
with her Pirate King until he was handed over to

“Is it your old trouble with the Bach and Kidneys?”
It used to be considered that only urinary and bladder
troubles were to be traced to the kidneys, but now modern science
proves that nearly all diseases have their beginning in the
disorder of these most important organs.
The kidneys filter and purify the blood, —that is their work.
So when your kidneys are weak or out of order, you can
understand how quickly your entire body is affected, and how
every organ seems to fail to do its duty.
If you are sick or “ feel badly,” begin taking Doan’s Back-
ache Kidney Pills, because as soon as your kidneys are well they
will help all the other organs to health. A trial will convince
anyone.
If the following had occurred somewhere else
than in Sydney, you might believe it, but you
could not prove it, and that makes all the differ-
ence. The reader must have seen many such
statements before, but he took very little notice of
them, because he knew neither the persons men-
tioned nor the towns they were supposed to live in.
But here is the statement of a Sydney man,
spoken for the good of Sydney people, and that
is why we say you can prove this. It has the right
sort of ring to it.
Mr. W. Hibberd, St. Paul’s street, Rand wick,
this city, says : —“ Por years I have been a sufferer
from backache. In my work I have to stoop a
good deal, and prolonged exertion of this kind
would double me up with pain. After stooping I
would be scarcely able to stand up straight again,
but would have to unbend gradually, and get the
kinks out of my back. I used many sorts of
medicines for this ache, but with poor success.
The doctors I saw were also puzzled, I think.
I procured a box of Doan’s Backache Kidney
Pills at Newman’s Pharmacy, in King-street, and
I am very pleased to tell you that these pills gave
me almost immediate relief. They went right to
the root of my trouble. The pains diminished
after further treatment, and at length totally dis-
appeared. I shall always recommend these pills
for backache trouble.”
Twelve months later Mrs. Hibberd states:—
“It is more than a year ago since my husband
used Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills for backache.
I am now enabled to say that the pills wrought a
permanent cure in his case. The pains never
returned since. I can speak with conviction of
the marvellous way they acted in his case. He is
very enthusiastic about the pills, and is always
recommending them for such ailments as the pills
are meant for. He had been bad over te .1 years,
so you can see it was no common remedy that
could cure him in the way Doan’s Backache Kid-
ney Pills did.”
This is only one case, but there are hundreds
more like it, both here and in Victoria, in Queens-
land, South Australia, and West Australia, in
fact, in every town in Australia. The papers in
every town have a fellow-townsman’s statement
every week, very much like Mr. Hibberd’s given
above, telling what this medicine has done for
them.
Now, there must be some good reason for the
unparalled success of Doan’s Bachache Kidney
Pills in Sydney and N.S.W. generally. \Y hat is
that reason ?
In the first place, they are a medicine of great
merit, for Sydney people are praising them every
week in these columns.
In the second place, their whole action is on the
most important organs of the whole body—the
kidneys. In other words, they do one thing, and
do it well.
But why are the kidneys the most important
organs of the body ? Because they filter the blood.
Every drop of the blood goes through the kidneys
many times a day, and it is their duty to filter
out the surplus water and the impurities the blood
has gathered up during its circuit of the body.
These impurities are the cause of your headache,
restlessness, sleeplessness, backache, general de-
bility, rheumatism, gout, urinary disturbances,
pains in the joints and limbs, irregular heart,
stomach troubles, and a thousand and one symp-
toms. Help the kidneys to rid the body of these
impurities, and you can be well. You can be well
in no other way.

The massacre was ended, Thwart-the-way
Island abandoned, and the mutineers made ready
to subdue Webby Hays and his forty-five followers
on Square Island. But, mad with lust of blood,
and drunk with gore and glory, they first raided
the jewel-cases of the Dutch East India Company ;
broke open the bales of silk and satins that formed
part of the “ Batavia’s ” cargo, and unrolled the
precious sheaves of gold lace. Cornelius, in his
“Satanic pride,” says an old Dutch chronicle,
“did wear different clothes every day, and
flaunted himself in silk stockings, and garters with
gold trimmings.” Moreover, “he invented fresh
cassocks every day.” Cornelius was a veritable
Beau Brummel of a past age in Au tralia’s history.
He likewise made for his personal body-guard
uniforms of crimson embroidered with three
broad bands of gold lace, ‘‘ so that the material
was hardly visible.” This was horrible, in the
careful eyes of the Dutch. Murder and rape were
orimes of some heinousness ; but this desecration
of the Company’s property was the last straw on
the back of the camel of mercy.
After a reckless and riotous orgy, the Captain-
General wrought on his following to make an
attack on Hays and the fugitives who had escaped
to Square Island. They found a pretext for their
foray in the fact that a boatswain's mate, named
Peter Lamberts, had escaped to Square Island in
a little boat he-had himself roughly put together.
So Conrad van Huyssen and 21 other lusty scoun-
drels rowed over to demand both boat and man.
They returned, dishonored and beaten-off by
Webby Hays and his party, without either. A few
days after, another attempt was made ; this time
with three boats, and a storming party of 37, led
by Jerome Cornelius himself. But the redoubt-
able Webby Hays and his men stood their ground
valiantly, and fought like tigers with their rudely-
fashioned weapons, knee-deep in the sea. The
crafty Cornelius next essayed a ruse, and employed
Clergyman Sebastian to negotiate with their
former companions, and to beseech them in the
name of his holy office to sink their differences,
and to live all together on the Island of “ Batavia’s
Churchyard.” The sacred man had many journeys
to and fro, and the creak of his boat’s rowlocks
was always to be heard. Cornelius offered
Webby Hays some cloth with which to replace
his people’s worn-ou; rags ; but he insisted upon
Lamberts and his boat being given up —as a re-
turn favour. Conrad van Huyssen heard of their
parleyings, and rated his chief roundly thereupon,
vehemently declaring that he would fight Webby
Hays the day following in the very teeth of hell
itself. But the Captain-General soothed him with
the advice, that as battle had failed, bribery might
be more successful.
Cornelius then tried to win over the five
French soldiers with an offer of six thousand
guilders apiece. He wrote them a letter—
somewhat humorous in parts. He spoke of a
fugitive, named Jean Coos, then on Square Island,
who had offered to stab another of Hays’s party,
a Jean Thierson. If he would keep his promise
and perform the stabbing, the mutineers would
accept it as an act of friendship. But Cornelius
insisted upon having surrendered to him a number
of hated and feared persons, among whom he enume-
rated “ the bottler’s mate,” “ the fat trumpeter,” a
“deaf” somebody or other, “squinting Heynd-
ricks,” and other shipmates. The French
soldiers showed the letter to their leader,
who was instantly on his guard. So when
Cornelius, accompanied by five other scoundrels,
landed on Square Island with the promised cloth-
ing, wherewith to confirm the pact, they were at
once set upon by Hays and his men. Four were
killed outright: to wit, David Seevanck, Conrad
van Huyssen, Van Wilderen and Cornelius
Peters; the Captain-General himself was made a
prisoner, but Wouter Loos escaped. On the re-
turn of the last-named to the “Churchyard,” he
was elected provisional Captain, and, in this
capacity, returned next day, with all the men he
could raise, in order to obtain the enlargement of
his chief. Webby Hays had four of his men
severely wounded, but Wouter Loos and his gang
were driven off.
At this juncture, Pelsart arrived from Batavia
in the ship Saardam, and, having cast anchor, put
bread and wine into a boat and rowed over to
Thwart-the-way Island. Here, to his astonish-
ment, he found not a living soul. Preparing to
leave the island, he was intercepted by Webby
Hays, who told the Commodore to rejoin his vessel
immediately,_ as a party of mutineers were even
then on their way to board the Saardam, and
further, gave him a brief outline of what had
happened in his absence. Pelsart hurried on
board just in time to see a boat putting off from
the shore full of fellows in crimson uniforms
covered with gold lace. This sight filled the old
mariner with a sublime rage. Burning with a holy
zeal to punish this awful piece of insolence, he
mounted the poop, trained the ship’s guns on the
advancing boat, and then exclaimed, in a voice
choking with fury, “ Why do you come armed?”
“You’ll damned soon see, when we get aboard ! ”
was the reply. “ Throw your arms overboard, or
I’ll sink you ! ” thundered the little man. The
mutineers saw the guns frowning ominously over-
head, and reluctantly dropped their pieces into
the sea. “Now come on board !” was the next
bellowed order. They went, one at a time, and
were heavily ironed as soon as their feet touched
the vessel’s deck. When things had been made
safe and sure with the mutineers, and the fugitives
on Square Island had been succored, Pelsart next
rowed over to the “Churchyard.” When the
mutineers who had remained on the island saw
the fierce little Commodore marching up from the
beach at the head of his men, they immediately
gave up all idea of resistance. “ Now,” said one,
“we are all dead men ! ” expecting to be at once
shot down. Pelsart had, however, no intention of
obliging them in that fashion. He simply put
the lot in irons and marched them off to durance,
as vile as the Dutch, or anyone else, could make
it in the seventeenth century. And now Pelsart
found himself in a difficulty. Should he take the
prisoners to Batavia for trial, or should he deal
with them on the spot ? The Saardam was suf-
ficiently laden, and Cornelius was so subtle a
rascal that he might succeed in corrupting a crew
of archangels. Finally Pelsart resolved to mete
out seventeenth century justice then and there.
Then the Inquisition began ! The first to be
examined was Jan Hendricks, aged 24. He had
killed, or had helped to kill, from 17 to 20 people,
at the express orders of Jerome Cornelius, the
Captain-General, who was charged with the whole
crime of the mutiny, with all its incidental hor-
rors. The torture .was repeatedly employed—
with varying success in the case of Cornelius, who
was prepared to swear any way the wind blew.
; Leonard Michaels, aged 22 years, had some 12
deaths to his score, and was charged with
helping himself to one of the married women.
Matthias Beyr, aged 21, nine deaths and the
abduction of a married woman, was the next.
Then came Albert Jans, 24, murders and wanton
acts; and so on, and so forth. Cornelius was
condemned to the chopping-block, where both his
hands were struck off, and whence he was taken
to the gallows and hanged. Several of his imme-
diate followers were condemned to the loss of the
right hand only before being gibbeted; others
were simply nap 'i ; two were marooned on the
mainland.
Pelsart recovered all his rix-dollars, save only
one chest containing 8000 coins : and that lies
somewhere off Wreck Point, even unto this day.
And such is the narrative of Australia’s first
known and recorded blood stain.
In China.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Of all earth s un-English and heathenish holes,
None comes within cooey of China.
Hie rottenest place, from the Line to the Poles,
Is that same Middle Kingdom of China.
Yet sometimes I think that we say and we do
Lots of things, and applaud them as proper and
true,
Which we d censure and damn with a mighty
“ 800-hoo,”
Were they done by the people of China.
All ranks and all titles by merit are won
In the pestilent kingdom of China ;
ie y S° back to the father, not down to the son—
Shows they’re all topsy-turvy in China.
It is birth, and not worth, that we Britishers
prize ;
We “kotow” to men whom we inly despise ;
Lhe knaves and the noodles make laws for the
wise.
We should call that rank nonsense—in China.
They’ve a praying-machine, which petitions can
string,
Sixty millions a minute, in China ;
Of course it’s a horrid, irreverent thing,
They’d use nowhere else than in China.
We British keep thousands of parsons to pray,
Costing millions a year. But though Chows do
not pay,
They get as much soul-saving comfort, they say,
From those praying contraptions in China.
We send parsons and opium and cotton and guns
To the poor, darkened heathen of China;
We force our religion and Sunday-school buns
On the perishing millions of China.
But supposing they sent us some preachers across,
To make us wear pigtails and worship a Joss,
Should we blandly look on while they tore down
the Cross ?
No ; I guess we’d make ructions in China.
The way they treat women’s a perfect disgrace
To the bad, brutal people of China ;
They .cramp the poor feet and they paint the doll’s
face
Of each dear little girlie in China.
Our women may paint, and it can’t be denied
That their waists may be pinched and their hair
may be dyed,
And their busts may be padded, and lots more
beside •
But, bless you, they don’t live in China.
The practice of gambling’s a terrible vice
’Mong the sin-sodden people of China !
Fan-tan, pak-a-pu, and a species of dice
Are all known to the gamblers of China.
True, we bet in our streets, and play cards in our
pubs ;
We have poker and nap in our big, toney clubs;
But we cannot attend to our own dirty dubs
While we’re preaching reform—out in China.
They have no old-age-pensions or maintenance
laws,
These barbarous wretches in China;
No bankrupts are there ; and to find out the cause
Is to cast a dark shade over China.
There a man cuts his throat when he can’t pay his
debts ;
No child e’er to honor his parents forgets;
And the worst thing of all is that nobody frets
They are most unprogressive in China.
They are all imitation—they’re copyists grand—
The ingenious toilers of China;
But they never invent, and they don’t understand
How to fashion a new thing in China.
Yet I fancy I’ve read that in centuries gone
ihey made silks, and had printing-machines of
their own,
And the mariner’s compass, to Europe unknown—
But, of course, we don’t copy from China.
We pay our physicians for potions and pills;
They pay them for health, there, in China.
While the patient is well he has regular bills; *.
When ill he stops paying, in China.
Our doctor draws fees, if he cures, if he kills ;
He gets paid while we’re sick, he gets named in.
our wills.
How blest are we British, escaping the ills
Of unhappy, illogical China !
We are patriots here, when we hold by our own ;
’Tis a different matter in China.
To expel the invader, or bid him begone,
Is flat-foot rebellion in China.
How happy, contented and proud should we be
That our lot has been cast in the Isles of the Free„
Where we do as we like—if we only agree
To condemn the same things done in China.
M.L.
J. LIDDELL KELLY.
In Victoria the Crown Prosecutor always
challenges a butcher as a juror in a murder case.
Just inaugurated in London a Colonial
Club, which according to its prospectus, “has
been established to afford an Imperial social centre
for colonial subjects residing in or temporarily
visiting the United Kingdom, and for those asso-
ciated with colonial interests in England.” The
Club is to have no politics, and “special’ care is
to be taken to preserve its colonial character -
birth or residence in the colonies or possessions of
the Crown, or a very close identification with
their development or interests, being an indispen-
sable qualification for membership.” The pre-
mises are opposite the new War Office in White-
hall.
